# **On democratic conventions in the year of 2016**

I've had the fortune to have attended the 2016 democratic conventions on
the precinct and district level. It has been a great learning
experience. There is no better way for a foreigner to see how democracy
works in the U.S. and further have a sense of participation than this.

On the precinct level, I chaired the district-43 precinct-1278 caucus
meeting on March 26th at Hamilton International Middle School, helping
dozens of people voice their support towards either Bernie Sanders or
Hillary Clinton. And I collected the votes from my precinct, aggregating
the numbers and presenting the result over to the next level. I was glad
to be able to participate as non-citizen Chair because we prepared a lot
on this, including my background material printouts stating my purpose
and experiences used to run for Chair, and polishing the skill of
"working the room". Plus, it was great to be able to know some great
people there who encouraged me to carry this on. Here is a photo of me
announcing the procedure of entire event to the voters from my precinct.

<img src="./media/image408.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The legislative district level convention was held on April 17th at
Cascadia Elementary. This event was huge. The school hosted delegates in
the 43 Legislative District elected from each precinct, which is
proportional to the number of people who voted in the last election
season. In this convention, my goal is to get enough signatures from
participants to support me to get an official status as a non-voting
observational shadow delegate in the convention on the next level, and
also present another resolution on advancing the [<u>GIX
program</u>](https://www.washington.edu/gix/). I successfully collected
50 signatures, one of which is from a candidate running for 43rd
Legislative District Representative. With the signatures and the
well-prepared resolution paper, we managed to hand it in to the
committee, who is going to discuss which resolutions to pass. Again, I
learned a lot about "working the room" -- I had to practice what I had
to say to convince people over and over again. The feedback people gave
me as I practiced helped me figure out what's important to say for this
matter. I've also learned to not to take rejections personally in such
activities as people have the freedom. It turned out 32 signatures were
collected very quickly with a surprisingly high 98% success rate. See
the picture below.

<img src="./media/image49.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Below is a picture taken at the convention.

<img src="./media/image13.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

These experiences are truly rewarding, in terms of interpersonal skills
and being an activist. Looking forward, my goal is to go the national
democratic convention in July (which means I'll need to get support from
the conventions on the county level, state level, etc.), observe how
democracy in U.S. works in every detail, and present more resolutions
for good.

Thanks to my friends for offloading a lot of the workload, and a great
deal of planning. I was able to enjoy the fun of it and learn more from
it only because of them.

# **A pint of Arabic Coffee at Cedars of Lebanon Restaurant**

The restaurant sequesters itself just a little bit from the bustling
University Way. The food this restaurant serves, of all the middle
eastern restaurants around the University District, is outstanding. And
the staff is welcoming, giving the restaurant a family feel.

Quite unique among all the foods is the Arabic coffee. The coffee has a
bitter and alcohol flavor to it. Quite thick, too. After I finished the
coffee, the owner was so generous and refilled the cup. I asked what was
in the coffee that contributed to the alcohol flavor. She said it was
Cardamom, with a big grin. I said it was the first time I tried Arabic
Coffee. And I’ll definitely come back, have another cup of Arabic Coffee
and dig into more Lebanese food next time.

Another castle charted on my food and culture map!

# **Mingcong Zeng’s visit to Seattle**

Mingcong is a friend of mine since high school. Now he studies math at
Rochester as a PhD student. He and his wife paid a visit to Seattle and
the surrounding area before heading to Oregon where his advisor is going
to have a birthday party with students and colleagues.

It seems that in academic disciplines that have a strong theoretical
flavor (math, high energy physics theory, theoretical computer science),
protégés of a senior person like to celebrate the senior person’s
birthday by putting together a seminar-like fest, where people, senior
and young, get together to present their work. I like this form a lot;
It echoes the essence of a scholar’s career — bearing intellectual
fruits and academic offspring.

Another thing I appreciate a lot is the passion, intensity and integrity
a mathematician has towards basically everything. Much of mathematics
has not found real-world use; Mathematicians march on regardless, in
pursuit of the pinnacle of human knowledge in its most abstract form.
Because of this abstractness, mathematicians are passionately intense,
as their terse symbolic expressions encode rich information. In fact, in
a very good way, Mingcong literally can’t talk without math at all; He
even proved a theorem on the plane! In addition, the nature of
mathematical rigor entails that mathematicians are extremely honest to
their findings and the gems their predecessors have found/created. This
is something I think every serious academic discipline needs to learn
from the math world.

An interesting story told by Mingcong is that the tiny math circle he
belongs to, namely algebraic topology, cultivates a hippie culture. It
might actually be true for other math communities as well. For example,
I learned that Paul Erdős used drugs heavily throughout his career. And
in the same spirit, people in Mingcong’s circle often like to do
conferences in Oregon, where it’s legal to consume marijuana.
Interestingly, according to Mingcong, back in Rochester, where marijuana
is actually illegal, things can still happen, but in a dodgier way.

There’s a lot more I learned from him during his visit, for example, his
view on the subject he is studying v.s. combinatorics (“ad-hoc”). But
that’s another story for another time :)

# **Memorial weekend trip to LA**

For the memorial weekend, I hit the road to catch up with some old
friends in LA, where we also visited some legendary places that I had
been long waiting to see. I have to share this fantastic journey here to
always come back to later :)

Started the trip on Friday night. Took the I5 due south. After
meandering along the narrow roads in the mountains, I entered the flat
Central Valley by late night. It was a starry night. I parked on the
side of the road to see and breathe. The dome of the sky was a gorgeous
painting on an unreal canvas. The camera just couldn’t do justice. There
were spiral and elliptical galaxies in the distance too…

<img src="./media/image691.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

I was trying to reach LA proper by early morning, but was too road worn
at the 1/3 milestone near Firebaugh. So I checked in at Best Western
Apricot Inn, which is a nice little hotel surrounded by palm trees.

<img src="./media/image544.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Checked out at around 9 the next morning, after a quick breakfast. A
local navy sailor sit across from me, chatting about his trip to
Washington for Memorial Day.

I filled up my car and entered the ramp into I5 quickly. Finally, I got
to see just how vast and dry the plain is. It’s absolutely an
agricultural miracle how this land produces the green veggies for the
densely populated cities on the coast, and even the world.

By noon, I found myself reaching the end of Central Valley, where the
giant Sierra Nevada and the hilly coastal range converge. The road
visually makes for a fine cut at the junction. SoCal here I come!

Before long, I was already driving down the scenic Jacarandas-decorated
roads accompanied by tall and vertical palm trees in San Gabriel Valley.
Literally minutes away from those old fellows with whom we used to prank
each other all the time 15 years ago, I was too excited to even capture
these purple wonders on the phone.

Finally we met. Kiddos are now all old farts (well, despite someone’s
strong protests :) What’s not changed? That sparkle in the eyes when we
all looked at each other and immediately knew wtf is going on.

Good old times :-)

The late lunch was in Sun Nong Dan, San Gabriel, allegedly a legendary
Korean joint. Mark Wiens tilted his head hard here at the cheesy ribs. I
couldn’t recall how the food was.

Next stop, the legendary Nest Teahouse in Arcadia. The teahouse is owned
by Steven, and the area is Steven’s hood. He took his talents to SoCal
from SoChina 14 years ago, and eventually amassed formidable commercial
dominance here. We couldn’t help but launched a shopping spree for free
:-) The guava tea was fantastic, at the boss’ recommendation.

We were chilling by the store. The afternoon breeze and the bright SoCal
sun in late May were a perfect background. 15 years passed by in the
blink of an eye. Last time we sat together, we were pupils in basic
school uniforms, a generation of Taiwanese singers were in their heydays
with their songs being played around our tiny campus over and over
again, and tropical thunderstorms came and left every afternoon…

This afternoon made me easily feel at home, despite that it’s my first
time coming to SoCal without a tourist group. I’ll definitely come
again, and potentially snatch one of those Jacarandas-y houses and stay…

<img src="./media/image267.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

One more thing I couldn’t wait to do before the day ended was to visit
Caltech. Oh Caltech, where so many legends left their footprints.

I got there just in time, and was blown away by the simple elegance.

> – Three quarks for Muster Mark!\

<img src="./media/image97.jpg"

At night, we ventured out to see Staples Center, Walk of Fame and UCLA.
I totally missed the exit for each of the places at the first attempt.
It was truly mesmerizing how complex LA freeways are.

Waking up to a rare rainy morning in San Gabriel valley (The atmospheric
river never fails to locate me) the next day, I headed straight north to
the foot of San Gabriel Mountains. Here I came to find Feynman’s resting
place.

The Big Bang Theory crew visited the grave at night, though the Mountain
View Cemetery is actually open 8am-5pm.

It wasn’t an easy find, but the grounds were well worth a stroll. The
lofty San Gabriel Mountains are right there overlooking the park, and
history is all over the place. By the curb of the Founder’s Lawn rests
the physicist’s restless soul.

<img src="./media/image457.jpg"

I put a stone on the top.

> No problem is too small or too trivial if we can really do something
> about it.

Could almost hear his words to Koichi as if he was speaking to my
doubts.

A trip to LA is incomplete without living like a local hombre for once.
For this we followed the noses (mostly Mark Wiens’ wise guidance) all
the way to Taquizas Gilberto in West Covina. Man, does the Taco Dinamita
spice up the taste buds. Sí, these are real dynamites, with lamb
tenderness explosions! You could tell how legendary this place is by
just how much local families love to flock here for lunch.

The OG has accumulated almost 10mil subscribers on YouTube. Nowadays

# **The most expensive Somali meal**

I enjoyed a Somali meal for \$193 today. The price and the diner were
both eye opening. Here’s what happened.

My rental car got towed for parking at a bus stop. So I had to uber to
the impound lot and pay \$138 for the towing (a fine of \$45 was
supposed to be paid separately to the city government). It was the first
time for me to have a car towed. An associate working at the impound lot
joked that it would definitely not be the last :)

At the exit of the impound lot, there was a guy checking receipts. As I
showed mine to him, I noticed that he has East African complexions. I
read from somewhere that there are a fair amount of Somali immigrants
here in Minneapolis, and since had been on an eager lookout for a Somali
experience. Not quite sure if he was one, I asked for his recommendation
for Somali restaurants and food anyway. Luckily, my guess was on target.

“Karmel Mall!” Ali responded after some pondering. Ali was his name, and
he seemed just as curious as I was: “why are you looking for Somali
food?!?” I explained how I heard about the Somali community here. Ali
bursted out laughing, thought for a bit, and then came up with two more
recommendations: “ask for chicken rice at Quruxlow; if they don’t have
chicken rice, ask for a menu, and you can just order anything on the
menu! Also go to Deg Deg”. He wrote down the two places:

<img src="./media/image303.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After thanking him, I headed straight to Karmel Mall. Near the
destination, the demographics suddenly shifted, from Scandinavian
looking white, to East African looking black, men wearing white caps and
white robes, and women wearing *guntiino* with hair covered.

Somali written in Latin alphabet could be seen everywhere in this
neighborhood (also officially in Somalia and Somaliland). The mall’s
name written in Somali alphabet appeared to be Suuqa Karmel. The
exterior of the mall was all painted azure, and there was a date tree
along with a traveler with two camels portraits on the wall.

<img src="./media/image60.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Outside of the mall, there were still Walgreen’s and Popeyes’ that
remind people this is not Horn of Africa. It was a drastically different
world inside. I was likely the only non-Somali there. Everyone was
looking at the new comer.

I got kind of scared, so I walked directly to the counter of the closest
stall from the entrance. With my mind fixated on just trying to get some
authentic food, everything should go fine, shouldn’t it?!?

<img src="./media/image279.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Luckily, the owner of the stall was quite friendly. I asked for chicken
and rice. He said cheerfully that they only had chicken and pasta, and
pointed me to the nearby tables. Everyone had two plates on the table, a
plate of fried chicken thighs, and a plate of pasta.

That’s it! Couldn’t go wrong with chicken and pasta. So I ordered it up.
The owner also offered me a bottle of water. “Enjoy.” He served the two
plates in no time, and gave me a small cup of green chili sauce. The
total price was \$10, and this included three, giant, drumsticks.

<img src="./media/image220.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Below are some more photos taken inside the restaurant:

\(1\) the menu

<img src="./media/image626.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

\(2\) the guests (did I just see Draymond Green and Tracy McGrady?)

<img src="./media/image135.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

\(3\) the chinese broadcasted CGTN

<img src="./media/image336.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

I was curious as to why a Chinese program was played here. Does China
have a huge presence in their native countries, just like China does in
some other African countries for construction and commerce? I don’t know
how much the balkanization of global supply chains would affect lives in
Somaliland and Somalia, but I think these war torn places wouldn’t want
a balkanization of the land after the Italian and British colonialism.

It was a decent meal. The pasta was warm and tomato-y. The chickens were
fried to a right degree. The sauce was on point. It was really stomach
stretching, and it was worth \$138+\$45+\$10=\$193 (not including uber)!

After the meal, it was thunderstorm o’clock. This thunderstorm was
forecasted even 4 days prior to its imminent materialization. Almighty
Midwest clouds all rolled in to smother the town like I had never seen
before. The edges of the clouds were sooo elaborate.

<img src="./media/image26.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Time to rush back before the first drop!

# **NAACL week trip to Minneapolis**

The highway system is complex just like the freeways in LA, whereas
Seattle and Bay Area — situated on an isthmus/a peninsula — only admit
of one or two major freeways. It’s easy to get lost without a gps device
here. Highway traffic here is too polite. People are too disciplined to
overtake, speed or illegally occupy the carpool lane. Talk about the
madness on 101 in Bay!

Speaking of the people in twin cities. In my experience, the locals are
nice and crude, very much unlike the shrewd techies in Bay or Seattle.
Restaurant services here are direct, homey and unsophisticated. People
acknowledge the existence of one another in elevators. There is a
community feel to the few neighborhoods I explored by the u of m campus.
Old folks gather to listen to live country music as the sun sets. People
bond over lake fishing. Small lakes abound everywhere I go. Elementary
school kids are very active and seem to enjoy hanging out even under the
burning sun or simply throwing a football around. I didn’t find people
passive aggressive like in Seattle, or stressed out like in Palo Alto.
The city embraces the giant Bob Dylan mural in the heart of Minneapolis.
And people seem to go to churches. At least, majestic and grandiose
churches, cathedrals and basilicas are everywhere.

And then there’s Mississippi River. Ever since I read Tom Sawyer when I
was a kid, I have been fascinated by this river. Finally seeing it,
although much farther north from where the story took place, although in
the 2010’s, I was still feeling transcending the modernity, back to the
mid 1800’s.

I had interesting encounters with a few unique communities in the Twin
Cities. On the first day there, I went to HmongTown Marketplace. It was
pretty much like a small town back in rural Hainan. The attires, snacks,
decors and everything else were basically the same. Then there was
Karmel Mall in a Somali neighborhood, where [<u>The most expensive
Somali
meal</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/The-most-expensive-Somali-meal) got
consumed. One midnight, troubled by the yearning stomach, I dashed to an
Ethiopian restaurant only to find it was packed with Ethiopians relaxing
in there. The crepe they served was sour and fluffy, and the beef was
oily and spicy. Quite a unique experience. Then there was this
Scandinavian ambiance everywhere. Although these northern European
immigrants have long integrated into the general American culture, there
are still traits of these communities that I found unmistakably
Swedish/Norwegian, notably the windows framed in dark brown and the
light blondness of the locals. Finally, the local signature dish — Jucy
Lucy — was a dime. I ended up going to each of the top three places
recommended by a youtube channel .

It was summertime. Hot air with high humidity was the theme. I’m quite
accustomed to this as I grew up on an east coast. Being back in this
weather gave me a sense of reunion with an old friend. Where there are
now flies, grasshoppers, mosquitos and the lush greenness, it was all
bleakness and snow just 2 months ago — the existence of 4 seasons can’t
be more brash. It was with this summer-ness that I loitered about on the
u of m campus. I stood by those old fashioned academic buildings, and
chilled on the unseasoned green meadow by Mississippi.

“Life starts when the church ends”.

\(4\) A dose of jucy Lucy at matt’s Bar and grill, the birthplace of the
cheese-in-a-beef-patty burger.

<img src="./media/image569.jpg"

It is a classic. Everyone orders it, with a “pop” (carbonated drink). A
working clock on the wall was going through one second at a time in
1998, precisely 21 years from now, as a reminder that “it’s not miller
time until you’re 21” :-)

\(5\) Holy Rosary church, in solidarity with the Ramadan observing
brothers.

<img src="./media/image129.jpg"

<img src="./media/image274.jpg"

It’s a church where the Dominicans hang.

\(6\) umn’s nice campus on the north shore of Mississippi

<img src="./media/image682.jpg"

glows everywhere. I liked the scholars walk a lot, a half mile long walk

\(7\) late night Ethiopian staple

\(8\) the downtown core, reminding me of back bay Boston — stadiums,

\(10\) lake phalen — visiting Minnesota without going to a lake is

This lake is almost exactly a mirror of green lake of Seattle. The

\(11\) the Finnish bistro, just an outlet of the otherwise hidden

The meal was kind of dry, but the service was A+. A band made up of
neighbors and friends was playing inside, harmoniously. Came for the
cultural experience, and was definitely not disappointed.

\(12\) Minneapolis institute of arts. This is another highlight of the
trip. Naacl Tuesday night party was held right here. So many Chinese
gems that only appeared in middle school arts textbooks were on display
here. I was pretty excited to see and was amused by the story telling
ceramic figure from eastern Han (“figure of a squatting drummer”).

\(13\) so long, Minneapolis!

Bon Appétit</u>](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UYmjwE5D1h4)

# **Thanksgiving trip to LA and SD**

Thanks to Quora for bringing back its blogs (rebranded as spaces).

Ever since the [<u>Memorial weekend trip to
LA</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-LA), the
jacaranda blossoms, the towering Mexican fan palms, and the all around
awesomeness of LA have been seeded in my head, hollering for a revisit.
So, when’s the next trip to SoCal?? — the Thanksgiving weekend! With my
cousin Sue, and our friend Janet.

It’s no jacaranda season this time around in late November / early
December, but what’s perpetually true is the rain — rainy, weather, in,
LA! Back in late May, I was extremely *fortunate* to catch the very last
rain of the season in LA (it was down-pouring in Malibu!). On Wednesday
night, November 27th, after a prolooooooonged dry season, I, was
formally inducted into the rain sucker hall-of-fame by being baptized
with the first rain of the season on the entire road trip from Bay via
I-5 to LA. God of rain never ceases to amaze me with her timely
blessings.

Well, l’m sorry, fellow travelers, that you had to go through this
sprinkling-to-stormy surprise. The fun, however, was not slightly
dampened on the road. Janet and I took turns to steer southerly, so none
of us got worn out like I was last time on the road. Sue took charge of
heading our music department — Her inexhaustible music list rocked up
the car non-stop. Spotify and the gradual showdown of our varying
degrees of singing talents obliterated the sense for the passage of
time.

Starting the trip at 6pm in Bay, we arrived at our airbnb in Encino at
around midnight, just north of Hollywood. Not long after we arrived, I-5
was closed as snow started to
[<u>pound</u>](https://www.latimes.com/california/story/2019-11-28/interstate-5-through-the-grapevine-closed-by-snow-as-new-storm-pounds-southern-california)
the Grapevine area. At least Tyche/Fortuna didn’t completely ditch us.

Next morning, first thing we knew, the vast San Fernando Valley was
spontaneously cast over the balcony, into our first impression of the
southland, generously with the entirety of its landscape. Gloomy it
still was, the fresh air accompanied by southern charm glowing from the
palms near and far started us off.

<img src="./media/image615.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

On Thanksgiving morning, not a single soul in town seemed to be in a
hurry. We put the exhausted car to work, driving down the hilly roads on
the foothills of Santa Monica Mountains, due Blu Jam Cafe.

At around 9am, the restaurant was already packed! On the inside, a line
that never seemed to shorten was full of holiday morning chit-chatting.
Seeing no chance of getting a table soon, we decided to move on.

Shortly, we found ourselves in this old-fashioned delicatessen named
Jerry’s. It was a place with a full-on menu and an army of loyal senior
patrons. From sandwiches to tacos to omelets, everything looked worth a
try. While the girls got themselves omelets, and sausage and cheese
scramble, I ordered up a corn beef sandwich. The tender meat and the
whole wheat bread reminded me of Katz on the Lower East in Manhattan.
Not bad!

After the breakfast, we headed for the Beverly Hills Sign. An upscale
neighborhood full of celebrity residents, Beverly Hills centers around
Beverly Gardens Park, where the Beverly Hills Sign stands. We took
pictures of this nice place, did some touristy stuff, and then found a
hip cafe nearby called Alfred Coffee, where we took a coffee break, and
continued the touristy activities, joining other tourists in the room
(e.g. posing with a coffee mug that has the name of the place on it).

The Grove, a huge shopping mall, was not far. As the touristy spirit
wore down, the thanksgiving shopping spirit buoyed up. When we got to
the mall, most of the shops were still closed due to the holiday,
quietly waiting for a large influx of customers on Black Friday.
Fortunately, we did find and end up buying nice things in a few of the
shops that were open.

Heading west til the end of the historic U.S. Route 66, we reached the
Santa Monica beach. The stratified coast dotted by the palm trees of
varying heights in the background struck me with its wild beauty.

<img src="./media/image735.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.59375in" />

We strolled along the beach, excited by the turn of weather, from the
pacific gloom that drifted from near Hawaii, to one that has blue cracks
between the clouds, and a rainbow vivified by the fresh sunshine.

<img src="./media/image93.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

There are exercise equipments for calisthenics installed on the beach
since the 1930’s, making the beach also known as [<u>Original Muscle
Beach</u>](https://www.santamonica.com/original-muscle-beach-santa-monica/).
We enjoyed the enjoyment that other “sportive e sportivi” had with the
equipments and the surrounding nature.

<img src="./media/image319.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image167.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

And who am I to step back from the challenges? We flexed some muscles
too.

<img src="./media/image70.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image61.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Well done! Keep on building your body and mind!

Later we visited the Venice Beach again, revisiting all the fun spots
where those classic pranks took places (documented
[<u>here</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-LA?ch=10&share=71194b97)).

As for the Thanksgiving dinner, we ended up returning to Beverly Hills
for a classic turkey meal, which included delicious roasted turkey
slices with a pumpkin sauce and caesar salads. As local friends informed
us that we’d find turkeys only in people’s homes on this special night,
who would know that there was still this restaurant that was open,
serving turkeys to a huge festive clientele? Luck was again on our side.

Next morning, we were greeted with the return of SoCal’s beautiful sun —
No words should describe the rejoice — I couldn’t help but took a
picture at the same spot on the balcony. With yesterday’s depression out
of the way, we were off to a colorful start with a full schedule ahead.

<img src="./media/image324.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

For breakfast, we met up with my friend Steven at Lunasia Dim Sum House
in Alhambra. Steven treated us to a dim sum feast! — The fried rice with
chicken and salted fish was one of the best I had ever had, and there
was a lot of other food we were unable to finish and had to take away.

Steven’s boba tea shop was the next stop. Me and Steven picked up where
we left off in May, this time walking Sue and Janet down the memory lane
of a 15-year friendship. There, we were also happy to learn that
Steven’s franchise was adding another branch in SoCal, with plans to add
shops to cover Bay Area in the future. Bravo!

<img src="./media/image475.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

For lunch, we met up with my friend Wen at Ho Kee Cafe in San Gabriel.
Wen was an intern on our team over the summer. Both of us share a
passion for machine learning, physics, soccer and basketball. Towards
the end of his internship, we went on an excursion to Half Moon Bay to
capture the magnificence of the milky way in the evening sky.

Lunch was a one-man devouring of Wen since the rest of us were full. As
he finished the delicious-looking roasted duck, we went on a tour on the
campus of Caltech, where Wen studies. Touring Caltech was a bit
emotional last time, as the revered Caltech physicist Gell-Mann just
passed away. This time, the tour was super chill and educational, as Wen
showed us around his department building, Moore Laboratory, which I
wasn’t able to access last time. I loved how much history of human
engineering endeavors this building carries, including these aged
strings that demonstrate the never-aging Viterbi decoding algorithm:

<img src="./media/image263.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We also saw

- a steel cannon that symbolizes the rivalry between Caltech and MIT,

- a pond full of turtles by the library,

- the numerous laboratories in the EE building that host sophisticated
  experimental devices and equipments that I may use to know how to use
  once upon a time,

- the place where press conferences for the newly minted Caltech Nobel
  laureates were held, and

- the nice grad student dormitories that look out on the lofty Mount
  Wilson Observatory.

We waved goodbye to Wen as we embarked on our next journey to somewhere
utterly stylish and glamorous. Will see him soon upon his return to the
workplace, and will see Caltech soon, a place I always feel a strong
connection to, for her sheer elegance on the outside and the hard
engineering core on the inside.

<img src="./media/image123.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Next, for the utter styles and glamour, we showed up on the neon chaos
of Hollywood Walk of Fame, right before the sunset. I believe “LA LA
Land” couldn’t be experienced better elsewhere than Walk of Fame. Here,
one can find all the familiar names on the sidewalks. For example, Sue
found her idol Britney Spears.

<img src="./media/image343.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

And we found the “best cousin” trophy in one of the souvenir stores
(“Galaxy Mall”), and righteously entitled ourselves to it.

<img src="./media/image138.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

The starry ambiance of the place coupled with floods of tourists from
all over the world gave this place an otherworldly feel, where the sense
of reality gradually faded away behind the neon signs, and every
stranger’s personal life stories relating to the certain works of this
giant entertainment empire flashed back, as name upon name of the stars
passed through…

Later that night, we circled back to the Grove, finally enjoyed the full
capacity of it on this Black Friday, by elbowing through layer upon
layer of people, and by truthfully and solidly exchanging dollars for
goods. We were especially happy to have purchased some comfy shoes from
Athletic Propulsion Labs’s first retail store.

Earlier, we went to another shopping district called Melrose, featuring
loads of independent designers’ stores, in contrast to the Grove’s large
department stores. However, it looked like every artist closed down her
store by 7pm, by which time we didn’t even finish the Walk of Fame. So
be it. We’d be back tomorrow.

The dim sum brunch and the tidbits of roasted duck were all digested
particles, by the time we finished the Grove business. We decided to
have a late night dinner at a legendary hot dog joint - Pink’s,
coincidentally, back in Melrose. What a place - it has a wall full of
photos of the owner with different celebrities. And I was drooling over
literally every item on the long menu. The Chicago Polish dog finally
won my heart. We ordered up 2 Polish dogs. One bite and one will
transcend over to a meat heaven.

<img src="./media/image333.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image409.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The third morning arrived, with an exciting tour to the Warner Bros.
Studio Tour awaiting. Clear weather; right on time; everything ready
(even contextually, as we watched Annabelle the night before — over
light wine, at Janet’s persistence — to get sufficiently hyped up for
the tour next day); off we go!

Warner Bros. studios are next to Universal Studios, across from the Los
Angeles River. The studio tour features the tour of, well, the studios
of all those beloved movies and shows produced by Warner Bros. As huge
fans of a lot of Warner Bros’ movies as we are, we were here especially
for the studios of f.r.i.e.n.d.s. (Sue’s favorite), the big bang theory
(accompanying my college years), The Ellen Show, and, well, Annabelle.
And this tour wasn’t at all disappointing, with a decent coverage over
all these shows:

\(1\) Outside the studios

<img src="./media/image438.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:12.54167in" />

\(2\) Inside the studios

<img src="./media/image277.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:12.54167in" />

\(3\) Ellen Show, The Big Bang Theory, Annabelle

<img src="./media/image627.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:12.54167in" />

And to my huge surprise (and great ignorance), National Treasure and
Harry Potter were also filmed by Warner Bros. I like the National
Treasure series for the plots and historical contexts, and I like the
Harry Potter series, just like hundreds of millions of other people. I
don’t think the Harry Potter series is particularly for “young adults”,
as what bookstores and libraries categorize the series to these days; I
think the story can be equally adored by people of all ages; and I hold
that J.K. Rowling deserves a Nobel Prize for writing Harry Potter.
Anyway, here are sample pictures we took at the respective studios:

“Resolute Desk”

<img src="./media/image377.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

A smiley “Avada Kedavra”

<img src="./media/image684.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

For lunch, we went for roast fish, which Sue had been long craving for,
in the east burbs of LA, with a high concentration of Chinese
immigrants.

Before Janet had to leave for Vegas that night, we went back to Melrose
again despite yesterday’s defeat, and we were happy to find out that the
stores were all open. A serious shopping spree ensued.

According to our plan, the next day was supposed to be when we would
drive to San Diego in the morning as soon as we checked out at the
airbnb in Encino. However, a trip to LA would be incomplete had Griffith
Observatory been missed. We didn’t waste this opportunity, as both of us
decided to trek up to the celebrated observatory, from the Greek
Theatre. The signature Hollywood Sign was clearly visible from the hill
top in one direction, and the cluster of skyscrapers in downtown LA was
also within sight in another direction.

Inside the observatory, there was a lot of educational stuff, with
explanations using the underpinning basic physics. I found the myriads
of meteorite samples on display most interesting. Not gonna lie: it was
so awe inspiring to see stuff that came from outer space, stuff that we
wouldn’t be able to see perhaps even in one’s lifetime. There was also a
section that describes how to recognize a meteorite on earth:

<img src="./media/image591.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

We were no expert in any of the related subjects, so all these nuggets
of information regarding meteorite recognition were new and fun.

By the end of the observatory tour, we came across this gift store right
next to a cafe named “cafe at the end of the universe”. I thought it was
a great name for a cafe. Also, in the gift store, there were actual
meteorites on sale, tens of dollars for a nail-sized piece. I imaged
that meteorites could be a fantastic present for a romantic partner.

<img src="./media/image415.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

It was in little dispute that in SoCal, if you were to eat sushi, go to
LA, and as for tacos, go to SD. Well, we didn’t wait until we arrived in
SD to have conned some tacos. Actually, right before Griffith, we
stopped by this little Mexican spot on Sunset BLVD — Tacos Delta — for
tacos. The al pastor tacos were juicy and flavorful, and the tortillas
were fresh and tender. The seafood soup they served was a bomb — loaded
with large chunks of seafood. One bowl of soup and the energy for the
day was all set, plus some surplus for the contending seagulls by the
table.

<img src="./media/image430.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image517.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

With this little expedition in the morning accomplished, we were ready
for San Diego. At around noon, we headed off to I-5.

A few years ago, my first impression of I-5 as an interstate highway was
that it was the vein of Seattle, stitching the city together from North
to South. My life had close ties to I-5. I lived 2 blocks away from it.
My university was a few blocks from it. It was usually what I took to go
to the downtown area, and to Portland for Thanksgiving shopping. And
fast forward a few years, we were driving on the southern most section
of I-5, in the sunny San Diego County, just past the Orange County…

Right before the sun set, passing the delicate La Jolla downtown core,
we reached the La Jolla Cove, a seals’ heaven. We captured on the phone
a seal howling the day goodbye.

<img src="./media/image14.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

But we were not done with the day yet. We wanted to capture the last
moment of the setting sun on Black’s Beach 10 minute’s drive away.

And we did it, on the steep Gliderport Trail. Facing the vast pacific to
the west, overlooking Black’s Beach, we found our way down to the beach.
We were greeted by the last batch of surfers were wrapping up with their
giant boards and climbing back up along the trail.

<img src="./media/image498.jpg" style="width:6.27083in;height:3in" />

As we reached elevation 0, the moon was out, and the entire town of La
Jolla lit up (on the lower left in the picture below is La Jolla, and La
Jolla Cove is at the tip of the land).

<img src="./media/image208.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

And that was it for the day… not.

As we were driving to the airbnb place in Clairemont, apparently, rocks
by the beach hit and poked through one of the tires. At the time when we
reached the airbnb house, the tire was completely deflated. Fortunately,
the welcoming host gave us pointers to nearby body shops, as well as
sushi restaurant recommendations, on a slip of paper. At her
recommendation, we Uberred to a place called Sushi Fish Attack.

<img src="./media/image175.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

One of the items in the specialty rolls on the menu was named
“ex-girlfriend“. Voila ^. It was not bad.

The last day of the trip started with a change of tire at a Discount
Tire nearby, a place recommended by the host lady and her husband. We
drove to the nearby gas station, pumped up the flat tire just so that it
would sustain us to the Discount Tire not far away. As the tire was
being examined, we left for brunch at Pho Hut & Grill, and then shopping
at Fashion Valley. By 2pm, the tire situation was clear - the hole was
too big to be patched, so we ordered that a new tire be installed.
Around 3pm, we wrapped up with new jackets purchased, got the car back,
and went on our way back north.

Driving on the Pacific Coast Highway in SoCal, one would constantly see
beaches. With the sun out, the entire coastline would shine with blue
glitter of the sea. But each beach has its own idiosyncrasies. La Jolla
Cove - sealy, Black’s Beach - cliffy, Oceanside - lagoony, Gladiator
Beach - deserted, Dana Point - entertaining, Laguna Beach - arty,
Newport Beach - yachty, and so on. We stopped by Laguna Beach and did
some wave chasing and seagull feeding there, with the tortillas from the
taco place the day before. Never is it too old to indulge in some kids
activities!

<img src="./media/image392.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Dinner revolved around some nice broasted chicken at The Chicken Coop in
Newport Beach. This restaurant was a gem. The service was top notch,
with the waiter cracking jokes every chance he got. The chicken and the
fish and chips were impeccable. An unassuming place with a very cozy
environment. I enjoyed a Seahawks game against the Vikings on TV.

<img src="./media/image541.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

This got us going for the last stop: The Pike Outlets in Long Beach. Sue
did more shopping for her parents and brother. At 7pm, we wrapped up and
swiftly departed Long Beach. Driving at a crazy speed, we finally
reached Bay a little after midnight.

In the end, I’m truly thankful for the visit of my cousin from Colorado,
and the company of friends. I’m thankful for the memorable trip with
wonderful people, colorful activities and great food. Here’s wishing
everybody a happy, healthy and productive year ahead!

# **New adventures accomplished this holiday season**

1\. Crossed a fairly wide and rapid creek over floating woods near
Mirror Lake in Yosemite, without getting wet, an attestation to the
workout routine I started lately :-)

2\. First Popeyes experience, in Dublin, CA. Had been craving for
Popeyes for a while but didn’t get a chance. The spicy chicken sandwich
was juicy, tender and all around awesome! Later, tried the same things
at a Chick-fil-A for the first time, in SunnyVale. The spicy chicken
sandwich was not bad either, and the chicken nuggets and the service at
Chick-fil-A were even better.

3\. First snowboarding experience at Heavenly, South Lake Tahoe. Learned
the basics of how to balance and control through a sneaked-in private
class taught by Jack (Hopefully he doesn’t see this). Practiced for an
afternoon and got pretty comfortable riding green runs without falling
over. Also, huge thanks for the free suits and the gloves to the good
hearted people :-)

4\. First Texas hold’em experience casino-style at Tahoe. Fairly easy
game, lots of fun with friends. Once a No.2, lost everything in the end
:D

5\. First roller coaster experience at New York New York in Vegas.
Insurmountable fear for this type of game since childhood; no regret at
all after the ride. Succeeding this one is big for me. Even craving for
the next ride - they are 5-minute-long nerve workout sessions after all,
good for your resilience :-)

6\. The Mob Museum in Vegas was awesome. Learned about the Prohibition
in the 1920’s and the repeal thereof in the 30’s. And learned about how
Sicilian and Jewish mobs in the America got started, first in the
alcohol business and then onto casinos after the repeal. As a result,
got hooked on how some of the larger immigrant groups in the US settled
down, stereotyped, blended in and in turn left a mark on the American
culture. E.g., Irish - famine - policemen and babysitters, German -
1848′ers - discontinuation of the use of the German language due to the
world wars - disappearance as a distinct ethic group despite the largest
ethic makeup (except for burgers, hot dogs, Christmas trees and
kindergartens), Chinese - Taiping rebellion - Gold Rush - Chinese
Exclusion Act, Russian - pogroms (Jewish) - October revolution - fall of
USSR (Jewish?).

7\. Ate at some of the most delicate and/or famed restaurants in Vegas.
These included The Buffet at Wynn (meh), Momofuku (meh), Hell’s Kitchen
(mmhmm), Mott 32 (meh), Red 8 (mmhmm), Rivea (meh). Overall I’m not a
big fan of fine dining - to get full you have to deplete your wallet
with no guarantee of either the flavor or the nutrition … Instead, a
good chick-fil-a meal will make me happy at any given hungry time.

8\. Summited the Lick Observatory, right next to Copernicus Peak, Bay
Area’s tallest peak, at an elevation of over 1300m. The observatory is
just 20 miles from San Jose, so yes it was that easy to be on the
“mountain top” of the entire silicon valley :-)

# **A review of spicy chicken sandwiches and chicken nuggets**

Prologue:

As good as the nuggets or sandwiches in some of the following photos
might look, just don’t overdose on fast food chicken all at once😑

**No. 1: Chick-fil-A, 9/10**

Juicy, tender, flavors soaked in, not overcooked, perfectly seasoned.

<img src="./media/image18.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

**No. 2: Popeyes, 8.5/10**

Juicy, as greasy as Chick-fil-A but not as flavorful, crispy on the
outside, pure chicken overdose.

<img src="./media/image297.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

**No. 3: Carl’s Jr., 6.5/10**

Now we enter the fast food regime. Carl’s Jr. spicy chicken burger and
nuggets were savory and wholesome. For fast food, this has been the
best.

<img src="./media/image738.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

**No. 3: KFC, 6.5/10**

Kudos to KFC’s nuggets, they swamped them with hot oil. The patty in the
burger was spicy and fresh.

<img src="./media/image358.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

**No. 5: Wendy’s, 6/10**

Flavorful chicken, well spiced, nutty mayo, crunchy lettuce, a little
dry despite the mayo. pretty similar to Carl’s Jr. except that the sauce
is not as good.

<img src="./media/image328.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.17708in" />

**No. 5: McDonald’s, 6/10**

Simple sandwich, the chicken flavor is surprisingly pronounced and
pleasant, warm and hearty. As Wendy’s, the nuggets taste like fast food
as they are. Unfortunately, they don’t serve spicy chicken sandwiches
(“Hot 'n Spicy McChicken”) anymore.

<img src="./media/image305.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

**No. 5: Arby’s, 6/10**

The largest burger amongst all. Juicy, tender patty with crunch
vegetables. A little too thick and oily, but still pass as a nice
chicken meal.

<img src="./media/image166.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

**No. 8: Jolibee, 5/10**

Fulfilling chunk of chicken, somewhat good of an aftertaste. A bit too
sweet due to the sauce used. The chicken was dry and didn’t have a fresh
taste.

<img src="./media/image17.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

**No. 9: Burger King, 4.5/10**

Nice and soft bums, nice and warm mayo, nice and spicy seasoning.
However, their chicken tastes a little undercooked, having a tiny hint
of raw chicken odor.

<img src="./media/image411.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:9.40625in" />

**No. 9: Shake Shack, 4.5/10**

A bit overrated brand tbh, the chicken burger is a weird blend of chilly
and chicken. As fresh and high quality it appeared to be, the flavor
just wasn’t the best, in contrast to its cheese burgers.

<img src="./media/image704.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

**No. 11: Denny’s, 2.5/10**

The good thing about it is its large portion, but the chicken is really
flavorless and dry. The caramel and ranch didn’t help much.

<img src="./media/image667.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:9.40625in" />

**No. 12: Jack in the Box, 2/10**

Despite the appearance, it didn’t taste like chicken. It wasn’t spicy,
and was even a little bitter.

<img src="./media/image665.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

**Honorable (not) mention:** Safeway chicken tenders, 0.5/10

Not chicken, not tender

<img src="./media/image736.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

There you have it! This has been me trying chicken sandwiches and
chicken nuggets at every major fast food chain that has such an option
and offering my personal opinions. Hope it’s helpful for your decision,
and I’m curious to know what you think about these fast food restaurants
as well :)

# **Memorial weekend trip to SoCal**

It has somehow become habitual to come down to SoCal during a memorial
weekend.

thanksgiving: [<u>Angli Liu's post in
marathoner</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Thanksgiving-trip-to-LA-and-SD?ch=10&share=61f05328)

memorial day: [<u>Angli Liu's post in
marathoner</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-LA?ch=10&share=71194b97)

This time, we have a group of 3. With a Romanian and a Turk, we can
either call this group The Silk Road gang, or Covid exodusters. We set
out at around noon on Saturday. Having had the rental car stocked with
food from a Santa Clara Safeway, we cut through Santa Cruz mountains and
started driving down hwy 1.

The first stop was Santa Cruz. This was not only the first time for all
of us to go to LA via hwy 1, but this was also the first time for all of
us to visit Santa Cruz. Apparently, the climate down here is already a
bit milder than the Bay Area. There are tall and dense Washingtonian
palms along the beach, reminiscent of those in SoCal. I always can’t get
enough of these slim yet mighty tall palms.

<img src="./media/image193.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image647.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image347.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

While the beach was officially closed due to Covid, tourists and locals
alike didn’t hesitate to get themselves as close to the ocean as
possible. Some were happy with the exotic fish they captured, and some
were enjoying themselves with sea lions at the stack. Sailers were out
in the ocean too on their stylish ships.

<img src="./media/image76.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image325.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image2.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Past Carmel by the sea, we had officially left civilization behind,
progressing along the rugged central California cliffs. The sun was
shining and the sky was clear. There was no sign of may grey or June
gloom. Outside of the few population centers, the rest of the California
was stunning and rustic. There was a mystical charm to the vastness and
emptiness of the landscape.

<img src="./media/image326.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image196.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image432.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Such views quickly became monotonous. Luckily, before I knew it,
mountains suddenly gave way to a strip of flat land, sandwiched by much
more gentle hills. That was where the ocean view temporarily ended, and
San Luis Obispo - Santa Maria metropolitan area started.

<img src="./media/image476.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Before the nightfall, we made it through the Gaviota cross, and
officially entered the SoCal land. It’s always pleasing to be welcomed
by densely spaced palm trees significant of SoCal. The Santa Ynez
mountains in the background were a huge plus to the otherwise already
picturesque view.

<img src="./media/image342.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image292.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We spent the night in Santa Barbara, at the best western hotel across
101 from the UCSB campus. The ocean side college town was pretty chill,
in sharp contrast to the locked down Bay Area. Some restaurants even
welcomed dine-in guests. Parties could be heard and seen everywhere in
isla vista. Young people could be found lining up by dispensaries. Just
be here, and stress would be gone. The sea breeze and the ocean smell
gradually calmed people down to a good night of sleep.

<img src="./media/image218.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The next morning, I woke up early to catch the first glimpse of glory on
the UCSB campus. Running a few miles in the ocean direction, passing an
alleyway by the Santa Barbara airport, I was presented with a gorgeous
beach and campus buildings like those in fairytales. Yes, the kitp
building is always a stunner.

<img src="./media/image677.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image592.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image74.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

As I came back to the hotel, the 2 weedheads waking up from last night’s
indulgence were found blissfully back to senses and ready to set off.

We were greeted by the signature late May blooming jacarandas, and
again, towering Washingtonian palms as soon as we headed to the road.

<img src="./media/image320.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image510.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

By noon, we were near Calabasas. There I recorded Kobe’s crash site as
we drove past. A tragic location, it looks paradoxically so mundane and
boring under the bright sun and clear blue sky…

In LA, we visited Feynman’s grave at my earnest recommendation,
Hollywood walk of fame, and Beverly Hills, in order.

Since Cris is particularly digging Elon Musk, we all invited ourselves
to Elon’s house in bel air, on the Santa Monica mountains. It had a
futuristic look. But he sold this house not long after we visited. Then
we made our way to the headquarters of spaceX, in Hawthorne. There we
saw a giant model rocket, and a neurolink prototype. A week after the
trip, SpaceX would send humans to space and recycle the rockets, the
first time in history. For now, we were just admiring the engineering
geniuses of Elon and co.

<img src="./media/image204.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image246.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We then headed to Santa Monica beach, and spent a good part of the
afternoon enjoying the rare open beach in the middle of the pandemic
(although the beach was closed again as a result of the resurgence of
Covid in LA shortly after our visit). May grey and June gloom would
later come covering the entire SoCal in late June in a typical fashion,
but for now we got quite a bit of sun and warmth.

<img src="./media/image57.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

At night, we reached Long Beach, where our second day residence was.
Orange County took the pandemic with more caution than LA county, as it
had ordered Long Beach to be closed. Cops would patrol the parking lot
and ask any car that tried to park there to withdraw. Our hotel was
close by, so we, along with a few other frivolous local residents, still
allowed ourselves to spend some time on the beach.

<img src="./media/image521.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image90.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

unfortunately, I sprained my left ankle in a skateboarding accident at
this point at the hotel. My mobility was instantaneously negated, so we
had to visit but a place right by the hotel for dinner.

<img src="./media/image724.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

The next morning, my plan to charter some SoCal lands on Strava was also
naturally aborted. This also spelled a terminating point for the running
streak I had had, in the face of the severely swollen ankle…

Fortunately, we had gone to all the planned places the day before.
Waving goodbye to the beautiful Orange County, and cruising through I5,
we made our way back to bay in the early afternoon…

<img src="./media/image495.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

# **Trip to Boulder and Denver**

One week after the [<u>trip to
LA</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-SoCal?ch=10&share=a95a3602),
I traveled again east to Denver, Colorado. One of the things I wanted to
do on this trip was to visit my cousin, who was going to CU Boulder.
Another reason for the trip was just because of the restless souls. Me
and Cris booked 2 round trip tickets, each \$136, 2 weeks ahead of the
trip.

On Friday evening right after work, I jumped on an Uber, which Cris was
already on, heading from his place via my place to SFO, through an empty
101. The airport, which I had been to for quite a few times, running at
its full capacity each time, was almost totally empty, besides a few
workers. It was a bit strange to see SFO without passengers.

The plane took off, and the dimming twilight was soon submerged by
artificial lights over Bay Area. Burlingame - San Mateo Hayward bridge -
Fremont - the brown and dry foothills by the green-ish Central Valley…
in the beginning I could still tell the whereabout, with the help of the
smaller and smaller lit up Bay Area in the background as a reference.
Soon, darkness prevailed, consolidating the unfamiliarity underneath.

A few uneventful hours went by. Suddenly, appeared a large web of lights
on the horizon. Smaller satellite webs scattered over the outskirts of
the main web in the center, clearly carving out the shape of a major
city, and a prohibitive mountain range on one side. Denver here we are.

Traveling in the middle of the covid pandemic, my first impression of
Denver was that there were a line of restaurants that were still
offering dine-in services, with no masks required! Despite the shock, we
took advantage of the liberty and enjoyed unusually large portions of
food at an Applebee’s premises for a late dinner.

<img src="./media/image364.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

One thing I was particularly looking forward to on the trip was the
Airbnb location. It was on a farm 15min from downtown Boulder, for \$60
a night. I had never lived on a farm! After dinner, we headed west to
this mysterious farm. The sky was super clear, offering a rare chance to
observe the stars, and the vast prairies/steppes/savannas they shined
upon. The traffic was minimum. We soon arrived at a farm, supposedly the
destination. There were a few branches at the end of the paved road,
each leading to bottomless darkness. The signs lit up by the headlights
were mighty confusing. Where are we supposed to go? Right at this time,
an elderly lady emerged from one of the houses. It was way past 12pm -
we must have woken her up. I felt bad, ready to extend my apology. “Can
I help you?” She walked up and asked sincerely, in a distinct American
accent. The “e” was almost like an “i”. We explained our confusion and
asked her how to get to the Airbnb address. “Alright” - she seemed to
know what was happening, and pointed to a guy who just emerged with a
bicycle. - “follow him”. So we followed the guy, much younger, who must
be a family member of hers. He seemed very happy about taking on the job
assigned to him seconds ago of leading us to the destination. After half
a minute, as we retreated from one branch and entered another, he
signaled that we had arrived. We shut down the car as he hopped off his
bike. “Hi, I’m Alberto, and you must be Angli.” He greeted. Needless to
say, we were happy to meet the Airbnb owner in an amusingly surprising
way, and were appreciative that he came to pick us up from a neighboring
address late at night.

The Airbnb building was a family business. Alberto’s grandmother started
a lodging business and an associated church, as indicated by the many
portraits of her in the common room and hallways in the building. The
surroundings were dead quiet deep into the night, which gave a strange
feel to these portraits. Nevertheless, my exploration of the building
was successful, where I found many helpful booklets and brochures.

<img src="./media/image449.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

The one extra hour from the time zone shift was balanced by the
exhaustion from the travel and the initial excitement. Soon sleepiness
got the better of me.

Next morning, we started off the exploration by visiting a restaurant my
cousin highly recommended in downtown boulder - the buff. Buffalo is the
mascot of CU Boulder, and so the buff is a fitting name for the
restaurant. Not surprisingly, the buff was packed with people dining in.
The food was of hearty American brunch style. What was amusing was that
the owner, upon hearing our accents, invited us on a photo to appear on
the restaurant’s official Instagram Account.

<img src="./media/image186.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:13.59375in" />

One of the most important missions on this Colorado trip for me was to
get a pair of shoes from my cousin. Well, this was truly a pair of
storied shoes. Back in November, my cousin and I got both of us a pair
of APL shoes during the [<u>thanksgiving trip to
LA</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Thanksgiving-trip-to-LA-and-SD?ch=10&share=61f05328).
The store shipped my shoes to Boulder, a wrong destination, along with
my cousin’s shoes. Since then I had been waiting to retrieve the best
sneakers I would (n)ever have\*.

\* story twist alert

Anyway, finishing the brunch, we met up with my cousin. It’s always good
to reunite with family. My cousin, specifically, is smart, cheering, and
always shares a lot of topics with me to chat about. I was also happy to
have her come out of the building to get some fresh air for the first
time in months.

<img src="./media/image157.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Me and Cris went on to explore the Rocky Mountain. My cousin was much
more thoughtful about the Covid, as you can probably also tell from the
picture, so she stayed.

On the way to my cousin’s place, we saw some protesters gathering on a
small scale on the Main Street of Boulder. This was in the wake of the
murder of George Floyd by cops in Minneapolis a week earlier during our
[<u>trip to
LA</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-SoCal?ch=10&share=a95a3602).
We would see much more fierce protests in Denver the next day, but let’s
put that on hold for a second.

Rocky Mountain was a beast. We set out to scale the bear peak trail
right outside of Boulder, which is still a far cry from the real
heartland of Rocky Mountain, and we were already stalled. The
mountainous roads were long and windy. We had to settle with option B at
the second flatiron, as the only 2 roads leading up to the bear peak
trail head were both blocked.

As we zigzagged our way to the flatiron trailhead, it also came time
when SpaceX’ reusable rocket was about to send 2 astronauts to space,
the first time in human history. We stopped at a location in the middle
of the mountain overlooking the whole town of Boulder and beyond, to
stream the event live. As was described in the [<u>previous
post</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-SoCal?ch=10&share=a95a3602),
Cris is a huge fan of Elon Musk. I too very much admire Elon’s vision
and work ethic. So when the launch succeeded, our rental car exploded
with cheers.

<img src="./media/image439.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The hike was successful too. We were able to capture deer from an
intimate distance on film without scaring them. We discussed about lots
of ideas about machine learning, such as backprop from middle layers
instead of from the final loss, multi-task training with much more tasks
than what had been achieved now, and enabling more sensory input to make
agents more aware and so more intelligent. We exchanged teachings of
words in the respective native languages. We attempted rock climbing to
the top tip of the second flatiron but decided to call it off because of
the steepness of the rocks near the top and the imminent rain. The
thunderstorm eventually broke out, after we safely descended to the
parking lot. Before that, I managed to capture some majestic views of
and on the mountain on photos.

<img src="./media/image217.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image429.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image743.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image442.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image251.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image638.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image505.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image77.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image673.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image375.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

As a connoisseur of food, my cousin recommended Rocky Mountain oysters
for dinner. But due to Covid, the major restaurant that usually served
what are actually fried bison testicles was not serving this dish. We
made the best of it and had bison burgers instead. At dinner time, it
started to down pour. The power of summer weather systems east of the
west coast reminded me of how mild west coast always is in comparison.
The powerful rain also reminded me of [<u>a similar encounter I had in
Minneapolis</u>](https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/NAACL-week-trip-to-Minneapolis?ch=10&share=38cee6e4)
exactly a year ago. The diversity of weather dynamics and the patterns
extracted from them fascinate me as always.

Due to the ankle injury I suffered from a week ago in Orange County, I
couldn’t charter lands in the mile high city as I would have wanted. So
next morning, I drove to meet with my cousin again instead of running.
We explored the city of Boulder a bit more, and chatted about some of
the happy and unhappy family matters.

<img src="./media/image229.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Back in the Airbnb, 2 nights ago, when we first managed to follow the
owner down the right branch, we saw headlight reflection upon a rather
haunting sign reading “the dead Tibetan”, right next to the entrance of
the Airbnb farm. Ever since then, we wanted to see about it. With the
midday sunlight bright as ever, the opportunity came.

<img src="./media/image106.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image583.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image260.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image648.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image532.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

It turned out that the dead Tibetan sign was indeed a rather special
pointer, to a horse farm, which was completely obscured by darkness the
first time we ran into it. A worker there explained that Alberto also
owned the farm. Quite a wealthy person with a peculiar palate for arts!

Back in the Airbnb building, the Sunday church service just finished.
People from near and far gathered to socialize. Curious as i was, i
mingled into a small group trying to get the ethos of it. A middle aged
lady happily described her apparently fantastic trip to Japan in length.
An old timer gave us a hearty welcome to the US upon knowing our
nationalities. He had the ability to chat non stop, with topics ranging
from the geography of Ohio, where he was born and raised, to Seattle,
where his relatives live, to SoCal, where some of his other relatives
live, to Colorado, where he lives, to Afghanistan and Tajikistan, where
he once had traveled and puffed on weed, to Trump’s policies, which he
avoided to the maximum extent to show his subtle, invisible embrace of.
No matter what, the group’s friendliness was so soothing and contagious,
something almost unheard of in Bay Area.

Upon checking out at the Airbnb, which simply consisted of leaving the
keys in a dropbox, we departed for Denver, which was but half an hour of
drive on a land whose flatness extends from here by the rocky mountains
all the way to the Appalachian mountains.

Denver has a Minneapolis vibe to it, as far as I’m concerned. The
architecture, the (slightly more semi arid) vegetation, the local
mannerism, the flatness and vastness, the downtown layout, and the
afternoon thunderstorms, all are reminiscent of the twin cities. We had
a Brazilian barbecue buffet for lunch, the only restaurant in downtown
that offered a dine-in service. I had more meat than any other days in a
few months. Meat done right was pure satisfaction.

To further the exploration, we also visited an office building in
downtown that was said to host fb’s Denver office, albeit closed. We
then went to the Denver zoo, only to find out that it was closed. All
the shops in downtown - closed. The only area that had lots of openings
was a few blocks outside of the downtown core, where open tents lined up
on the sidewalks, one of the largest homeless slums I’ve ever seen.
Eventually, we found a bison refuge on a plain east of Denver that was
not closed. These bisons are huge grass eating animals.

<img src="./media/image662.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image482.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

We checked out the state team’s soccer arena nearby. Its location in the
suburbs far from the city center perhaps suggested soccer’s secondary
importance in American sports.

<img src="./media/image256.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Well, it was still too early for the evening flight back, but there
wasn’t too much to see either, especially given the Covid situation. We
decided to head back to downtown to potentially find protesters and join
the BLM protest.

Protests are one of the most sensitive and taboo topic, if not THE most,
in where I came from. I had naturally been wanting to learn more about
protests by observing one close up. Yesterday, we did see one with the
same appeal, although quite small, in Boulder. Guided by the helicopter
noise from high up, and following the gradient of the graffiti density,
we found the mass in the state Capitol area.

<img src="./media/image486.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image428.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image547.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image600.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image440.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image262.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image340.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

A massive protest was underway at the state Capitol. There were black
and white protesters, rarely Asian. Folks took turns to speak on a
voluntary basis to the crowd using a loudspeaker. The topics included
personal stories of being discriminated against, persecuted, personal
appeals for racial equality, social justice and against police
brutality. Between these stories, an organizer would charge up the group
by shouting slogans. Usually the organizer started the first half of the
slogan, and the protesters would follow to chant the second half. These
slogans included “no justice, no peace”, “hands up, don’t shoot”, “if we
don’t get it, shut it down”, referring to the drones the police deployed
over the crowd. I was truly deeply touched by the way people stood up
against injustice, and the way freedom of expression was practiced right
in front of my eyes. I had watched the footage where George Floyd was
choked to death by the police, and felt every bit of the sentiment the
slogans and signs were conveying. So I joined the protest. This marked
the first time of me doing so in my life. I was feeling angry, touched
and noble all at the same time.

<img src="./media/image700.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image108.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image78.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Cris and I discussed about potential solutions to the racial injustice
problem. To me, a major part of the problem is education, or lack
thereof. And the root cause of that is the lack of influences from more
successful people. Singaporean mixing has been a success, where people
of different races live in the same community in a quota system, so good
collective habits from one group have a high chance of impacting another
group. Also, understanding of the differences between different groups
can be deepened.

However, such a system would be impossible to be implemented in a
democratic and historically racially and economically segregated country
like the US.

With many questions, and many memorable events, the Colorado trip came
to a closure. We headed to the airport before protester would disperse
to the street at 8pm, against an active curfew.

<img src="./media/image598.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Back in SFO, I forgot to take with me the APL shoes in a bathroom at the
arrival floor in the international terminal (designated for the frontier
airline). In the next few days, I contacted the lost and found office of
SFO, the lost and found office of the frontier airline, and the Uber
driver that took us back from SFO, to no avail. As a result, these shoes
that traveled from LA, to Boulder, to San Francisco, over the course of
half a year, became the best sneakers I would never have. The picture
where they were last seen is attached, taken on a tram traveling between
terminals in the Denver airport.

<img src="./media/image376.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

# **Trip to Alaska, the formidable north**

Alaska had been on my bucket list since I was living in Seattle, when it
was just one British Columbia away! My idea of Alaska then pretty much
only involved whale watching and dogsleds in the snow, attributable to
Jack London’s *The Call of the Wild*. In other words, almost everything
up there was up to discovery.

It was however only after the move to California that this great
opportunity to visit Alaska finally presented itself. Just like Buck the
dog in *The Call of the Wild*, the trip also started in the Bay area
this time, via Seattle, then landing in interior Alaska. Unlike Buck
though, who went on the adventure pretty much by itself, I had the
privilege to have fantastic company. Yidi, Shelly and I kicked off this
week-long journey together on a Saturday morning.

The flight from SFO to Seattle saw the gradual transition from
mediterranean terrains to the good ol’ Pacific Northwest. The latter was
covered with a thick layer of clouds as per usual at this time of the
year. I was joking as the flight from Seattle to Fairbanks took off and
got above the clouds that there wasn’t a better duo than Seattle and
rain. As the plane moved further north over the coastal region of
southeast Alaska, the first sight of Alaska broke through, as the sun
returned. Layer upon layer of snow-capped mountains, formidable as they
looked, stood shoulder by shoulder with the Pacific Ocean. The sun
characteristically shone much dimmer than seen in California. That’s how
you tell how far north we’ve reached. Alaska here we come!

<img src="./media/image212.jpg" style="width:6.27083in;height:3.75in" />

(Mt Adams, Mt St. Helens and Mt Hood standing above the clouds)

<img src="./media/image420.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Flying over southeast Alaska)

Getting out of the Fairbanks airport was straightforward. There was only
one baggage carousel. And there was one mandatory line for passengers to
present covid test result — as soon as the officer vetted our PCR test
results done within 72 hours prior to the arrival, we were good to go.

Our lodging was situated about an hour outside of Fairbanks, at a place
called Chena hot springs resort. There is an interesting history behind
how gold miners discovered this natural source of hot mineral water over
100 years ago. Today, accommodation facilities like pools, clean thermal
energy transferring, an ice museum, sledding dog and motor trips have
been established around the hot spring.

It didn’t take long before we learned that the week while we were there
actually lay in the shoulder season of Alaska tourism. Apparently, bears
had just gone into hibernation, and the snow was not thick enough to
support dog sledding yet. The season also had a silver lining. We
quickly realized that because of the timing, we got nature all to
ourselves pretty much wherever we went. More on this later.

It had been a long day, and so we settled to watch videos inside our
warm wooden lodge. But it was destined to be a long day, as we found
that the Wi-Fi was crappy and nominally not free, and that there was no
cellular signal. Later, it turned out that the front desk would still
give Wi-Fi accesses if asked.

<img src="./media/image200.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Chena hot springs resort Wi-Fi with an un YouTube friendly bandwidth)

We embarked on the next day’s journey with a packed schedule. In the
morning, we made our way back to Fairbanks to visit the Museum of the
North, on the campus of University of Alaska, Fairbanks. The campus
overlooks the quiet town of Fairbanks, entirely covered by snow. The
museum has 2 floors. The main level featured the natural history of
Alaska, including gold (in large chunks!), large animals typical of
Arctic and subarctic climates (one of the bears has facial features
resembling Jay Chou), extinct animals (such as dinosaurs), the Native
American tribes, and the European explorers. The upper level featured
Native American arts. The Native American arts were beautiful and unique
and all. Interested in ethnolinguistics stuff more than arts as I am,
what actually struck me the most was how frequently one could run into a
Native American looking person here in Alaska. Visually, this frequency
is much lower in California. To put this into perspective, native people
constitute 15% of the Alaskan population, whereas the number is less
than 1% in California. Indeed, the receptionist of the museum, many
restaurant workers, the hotel front desk, and a large fraction of the
grocery store cashiers were all Native American represented. So actually
seeing this difference gave me an exciting prospect for people we were
about to meet on this trip.

<img src="./media/image394.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Dinosaur fossils in the museum of the north)

Next we visited a Santa clause themed gift shop. Here, one can buy
postcards and have the store send them so that the cards are promptly
received on Christmas. It was also quite a festive place. The shop
featured 2 chairs hugged by Santa and his reindeer squad, which made for
cheerful pictures.

Right next door to the themed shop was Antler Academy. Due to Covid,
tours were canceled. But Santa’s entire reindeer crew could still be
seen chilling on this patch of land. They were getting ready for their
next trip in just 2 months :-) The reindeer looked quite relaxed. Some
were gently rubbing their heads against a blue brush. Don’t know what it
was that they were doing, but it sure looked very satisfying to them :-)

<img src="./media/image731.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Reindeer chilling in Antler academy)

The subarctic nightfall came pretty early in late October. By 6pm, we
returned to the resort for the hot spring, upon an expensive KFC
experience. Meat produce was noticeably pricier in Fairbanks than Bay
Area, and Anchorage as we later discovered. Anyway, back at the hot
spring, the locker room was freezing cold, so we quickly submerged
ourselves in the water, joining a sizable crowd that was already in the
pool. The sheer contrast of the cold air and hot water made for a very
