unique experience. The closer you moved to the source of the spring,
where the extremely hot spring was pumped up and crashed into the pool,
the greater the contrast. There was a couple settled right next to the
fountain’s crashing point, embracing each other as if time had
suspended. This really left me wondering if their skin was built
different, as I was trying to survive the temperature disparity, and the
violent evaporation going against my lungs.

<img src="./media/image471.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(“Vaping” in Chena hot spring)

After this outdoor sauna session, we moved on to a tour in the nearby
ice museum as scheduled. The ice museum was essentially a huge freezer.
Hosted inside were an array of delicate pieces of ice sculpture work,
ranging from animals, castles, chessboards, music instruments, to even
roses. The guide proudly reported that the extent to which the details
of each piece were carried out attracts people from all over the world
to hold ceremonies such as weddings in this museum. Indeed, in the
middle of the museum were an ice podium and ice seats. For now, they
were used as a bar. One could pay to get the guide to serve you apple
martini in ice glasses. If you get fancy, there were igloos in this
freezer you can also book and spend a night inside. Since we had YouTube
videos to watch back in the warm cabin, we spared ourselves the
opportunity, but we did get some nice photos inside :)

<img src="./media/image601.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(An ice flower and an ice patio in the background)

Later that night, we did a bit more outdoor sauna, and finished the
latest episode of *Everybody Stand By* that was left over from the
previous night :-)

The plan for the third day was to march farther north to reach the
Arctic circle. Since rental cars were prohibited from going on the only
paved road in that direction, we had booked a tour in advance. The
guide, Michael, picked us up in Fairbanks. Before we knew it, we had
left Fairbanks behind, the last substantial human settlement before the
Arctic Ocean, and were driving on Dalton highway. Only accompanied by
nothing but a trans-Alaska oil pipeline on the side, which goes all the
way from Deadhorse (by Arctic Ocean) to Valdez (by Pacific Ocean),
supporting a significant fraction of the Alaskan economy, Dalton highway
was truly lone and rough.

There was rarely any traffic in either direction. In fact, as Michael
explained, if there was a truck going Deadhorse bound, it must be
sending food and other supplies to the oil field. On the other hand, if
a truck came in the opposite direction, it must be carrying equipments
from the oil field back for repair.

Although covered by snow, the road could be seen alternating between
dirt and paved segments. Whenever a truck came, both vehicles would slow
down a bit, so neither would be hit hard by the otherwise deadly small
rocks stirred up from the road by the tires. The meeting drivers would
also greet one another using hand gestures. In this extreme isolation
with extremely rough conditions, obviously any human contact with
acknowledgement is a display of hope and warmth.

<img src="./media/image371.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image572.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(The start of Dalton highway right outside of Fairbanks, and the oil
pipeline that parallels it all along)

As bare as the place was, there were a few notable places by Dalton hwy
between Fairbanks and the Arctic circle. These include a rime ice
viewing site called “stone”, the Yukon river camp, which is one of the
very few roadside shops that do basic fuel and food supplies, and a
small hill that overlooks a valley which is apparently the habitat of
various arctic animals…

<img src="./media/image94.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Rime ice at “stone”)

<img src="./media/image254.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(A social distancing sign inside the yukon river camp)

<img src="./media/image365.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Bushes up on the hill supposedly inhabited by some fantastic beasts)

The Yukon river camp is a family business. According to the owners, they
had served the area for many generations. What had kept them staying?
One owner recounted that she would go up to Deadhorse in the summer by
the oil field, and visit friends in communities along the sparsely
populated Yukon river from time to time. Satellite communication, albeit
intermittent, was their way of staying connected. Despite being at the
crossroad of Dalton hwy and Yukon river, at this moment, this small
store was very, very quiet. Indeed, with the off-season and the pandemic
going on, there was no business aside from us - the time seemed rough.

After about 4.5 hours of driving, shivering and tired, we celebrated the
accomplishment of reaching the Arctic circle. The location featured an
Arctic circle sign. Next to it there were boards demonstrating the
science of the Arctic circle (ie midnight sun / polar nights).
Technically, it’s unclear whether the Arctic circle still exactly
crosses this sign as the circle moves a bit northwards every year. But
seeing the sign was absolutely humbling nonetheless — this was the
northernmost location on planet earth we had ever been to. Farther north
than Iceland!

<img src="./media/image202.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(at the Arctic circle)

What was quite amusing was that quite a few birds flew over from nowhere
as Michael started putting out cookie crumbs. So we each put cookie
crumbs on our hands, and observed the birds happily indulging in the
afternoon snacks as they came over for a peck, took a brief break back
on a tree, and took a stint on their federal food reserve again,
repeating the process.

Aside from feeding birds by the Arctic sign, there was not much to do
around there really. The nearby camp ground was apparently closed; no
one else was around. We hopped back on Michael’s van as the last bit of
the cookies was devoured by these cookie loving, cold proof birds.

As a guide, Michael is also a great story teller. He shared with us his
many hunting adventures in Alaska on the way back, and time passed
quickly. It was already pitch dark when we reached the Yukon river camp.
Underrating the importance of this camp in the beginning because of the
somewhat inattentiveness in the service, now I couldn’t stress how
critical this store was enough, as grabbing some food and taking a
restroom break after driving in the dark near the arctic circle for
hours were of such fundamental importance, no matter if the store put up
a big welcome sign or not.

Approaching the outskirts of Fairbanks, we made another stop to try our
luck with auroras for the first time. As a proud Alaskan resident,
Michael really wanted us to see auroras and so proposed this stop.
Although the “realistic kp index” for auroras was not high at the
moment, suggested Michael, auroras might still be seen on a clear arctic
day. Surely, the sky that had been misty all day long cleared out a bit.
Unfortunately, no hint of auroras was in sight, but the North Star came
out. With no light pollution in this colossal wilderness, even seeing a
partially starry sky was really inspiring. This wrapped up our
expedition in interior Alaska.

The fourth day marked the start of our trip to warmer parts of Alaska.
No one was looking forward to it more than us, having just survived the
extremes of the Arctic circle :-) Early in the morning, we entered hwy
3, leaving a quiet Fairbanks behind.

<img src="./media/image745.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Morning in Fairbanks)

We reached Denali Visitor Center before noon. It turned out that the
center, and the entire park was closed due to covid. So we couldn’t do
the planned 30 mile trip into the mountain, where we were hoping to see
bears that possibly had yet to fall into dormancy. Fortunately, we came
across a ranger, who happily pointed us to the nearby horseshoe lake
trail (Additionally, he couldn’t seem to resist brining up the city of
Palmer, which he praised has “nice restaurants and nice people”). This
was a trail about a mile long around horseshoe lake. The trail started
on a railroad, and soon branched out into the woods. After a short
scenic hike with trees all covered by new snow, the trail descends
quickly to the level of the lake. The lake was almost frozen. Small dams
possibly built by beavers could be seen dividing the lake into several
parts. But it was dead quiet down in this little valley; no trace of
animals could be seen. Near the end of the lake trail, another short
trail led out to the bank of Nenana River. The river was full of rapidly
floating ice. A tiny resort town sits on a hill right across the river,
offering a trace of humanity enclosed by this pure and white nature.
(Did I say we had nature all to ourselves :-))

<img src="./media/image712.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

(Nenana River seen at the end of the horseshoe lake trail)

<img src="./media/image603.jpg" style="width:6.27083in;height:4in" />

(Horseshoe lake almost frozen)

Everyone was starving and frozen after this snowy hike. We had a quick
bite in the much needed warmth in our car before resuming the road trip.
The sky took a pleasantly sharp turn into clarity as soon as we climbed
over to the south side of Denali. Cheers broke out in the car, because a
clear sky meant a chance of seeing auroras, which were forecasted to be
at the brink of a larger breakout. By the time we reached Denali
Viewpoint South, the sky completely cleared up.

While hopes were solidified with the better weather, what changed was
not only the weather. As the south side of Denali directly faces the
moisture constantly blowing from the pacific, the air was palpably more
humid, and vegetation became recognizably more oceanic, a dramatic
contrast to the interior Alaska’s subarctic feel.

In this newly acquired optimism, we made a stop at the Denali Viewpoint
South, and spent time admiring this rare, clear, view of the Denali
summit.

<img src="./media/image223.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image708.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(vista of Denali with Chulitna River in the front)

A small hike called Denali Viewpoint hike led up to a higher spot, where
we had a nice overlook of the Chulitna river under the majestic Denali
peak. As the sun gradually set behind the conifers, the top of Denali
became engulfed in a pink hue, calmly and marvelously. The view
attracted a small crowd of photographers. Everyone looked like they were
trying to absorb the whole picture in full concentration in the last few
moments before sundown, either with eyes or with professional gadgets.

By nightfall, we arrived in the town of Talkeetna. On the south end of
this resort town, we found our airbnb, Susitna River Lodging. As the
name suggests, this set of wooden houses sits right on the bank of
Susitna River, of which the earlier Chulitna River is a tributary. In
fact, the town of Talkeetna is just a bit downstream from the confluence
of Chulitna and Talkeetna rivers.

With the geographical setup out of the way, we started to look for a
practical spot for aurora viewing. The lodging area was nice and dark,
but the airpot nearby was emitting periodic white lights that could be
misleading. Some websites recommended Talkeetna Alaskan Lodge as a
viewing spot, but the entranced was closed. After trying a few
locations, we picked Talkeetna Riverfront Park on Michael’s
recommendation (delivered from Fairbanks). The park sits on the river
bank right at the confluence of the few tributaries of Susitna River.
Despite the Talkeetna Airport-induced beacon lights and other possibly
interfering lights emitted from the main street of Talkeetna in the east
direction, the park provided an unobstructed view of the north, where
the aurora was forecasted to appear.

“Is it going to be the same as last night? how long would the wait be if
we have to wait? It’s freezing cold outside!” Low hopes intermixed with
doubts filled the car as we found a parking spot by the park.
Understandably so…

Bam!

Hmm what’s that!? — BAM! — the exact moment when the headlamps of our
car were switched off, **abruptly**, **bluntly** and **rudely**
projected right across the entire northern dome of the sky, was the
aurora — the long-awaited aurora — unmistakably, unhindered, aurora — No
wait was needed this time!

The setup ended up being perfect — the city lights in the east happened
to be conveniently blocked by the park bushes; even the sound of the
world was turned off, leaving only the frictions between the ice sheets
flowing down Susitna River soothingly heard. We were only faintly lit by
the crisp milky way, and yes, by the aurora, and we were left utterly
speechless.

<img src="./media/image381.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

<img src="./media/image585.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

<img src="./media/image571.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

<img src="./media/image52.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

<img src="./media/image105.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

(northern lights in action)

The aurora was truly like nothing I had ever seen. It was not static. It
was folding itself at one point, and was spreading at another point. It
came in different shapes. Sometimes it was a green-red-ish mist, and
sometimes, it was a bright green band. As the “realistic kp index”
plateaued, the intensity of the aurora further heightened. Each move
became faster. The coverage became enthrallingly overwhelming. The
shapes became more elaborate. The colors became much brighter. Half of
the sky became alive! It was as if a higher being sent a multifaceted
performer teasing us earthlings with its humorously whimsical surprises.
And I really couldn’t keep my eyes off of it for a moment. Not only was
the aurora cast as the sole performer, but it was everything -
everything jumped on this stage and joined force. Denali peak and the
nearby mountain peaks struck from the shadows. Ice in Susitna came to a
climax in its symphony. Shooting stars turned into the loud cannons in
the sky’s 1812 overture. The big dipper stars kept winking on the side,
as a mischievous reminder that there’s more to this myth… This
otherworldly performance completely obscured our sense of the flow of
time. It was only the cold in the depth of the night that reminded us of
our own existence. This aurora party would undoubtedly go down as one of
the most memorable nights in my life.

The next morning, we returned to the riverside to pay tribute to the
theatre where magic happened last night, before heading to today’s
destination: Seward.

<img src="./media/image542.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.55208in" />

(Denali and Susitna in the morning glory)

En route to Seward, we passed through Palmer (mentioned by the Denali
park ranger) and Anchorage. As the largest city in Alaska, Anchorage had
that Seattle vibe, in terms of the maritime influences on the
vegetation, the status as the economic hub of the state (the city has a
proper downtown with bustling businesses), as well as the snow capped
mountains in the distance. The difference was that the late October day
in Anchorage was already freezing cold, when Seattle was just entering
the height of its fall season.

<img src="./media/image716.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

(downtown Anchorage with Chugach mountains in the distance)

After a lunch at Popeyes, we launched our final push to Seward, the
southernmost destination of this trip. This led us to Alaska route 1,
Anchorage to Portage section. This section provided an ocean view much
famed among Alaska travel guidebooks. Very fortunately, despite the fact
that Anchorage averages 15–20 days a month with precipitation at this
time of the year, a clear blue sky was upon us. This offered us a rare
opportunity to see the Denali mountain range again from a distance.

<img src="./media/image45.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(a gorgeous outlook from Alaska route 1, Anchorage to Portage section)

As we headed south, the scenery became more autumn-like. Oft-seen was
the stratification of vegetation from red foliage at the foot of a
mountain to the tundra top capped with snow. Throughout the trip,
formidable had been the first word that came to my mind that described
Alaska, with its ruthlessly cold and bleak landscape. But at this point,
the ambience really started to dispatch a touch of liveliness.

<img src="./media/image98.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.15625in" />

(a turn of season on Alaska Route 1, Anchorage to Portage section)

Going back to the previous evening for a moment, we met a local at
Susitna River Lodging, who recommended a place called Alaska Wildlife
Conservation Center, which is located at the end of the Anchorage to
Portage section of Alaska 1. Yidi wanted to see black bears bad, so we
decided to give the conservation center a go. Luckily, at the center /
refuge, not only did black bears turn out to have not been dormant yet,
but we also found shy foxes and lively wolfs (to Yidi’s liking;
apparently she has a thing for white fluffy animals :-))

<img src="./media/image92.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image170.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image241.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image637.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image359.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image527.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(animals in AWCC; the full list can be found on AWCC’s website)

Here are some observations at the conservation center: the black bears
were digging a hole on their own for hibernation, while the brown bears
in a separate area were to use a cave the refuge staff made for them.
The black bears were not reactive to people on the other side of the
fence, creating the illusion of them having an impaired sensory system
(these were all rescued animals after all) or being close to dormancy.
When the staff came with food even from a far distance, however, the
black bears all congregated by the fence eager to be fed. The food that
was tossed over the fence by the staff consisted of raw meat, nuts and
whole fruits. Ravens and eagles would come in mass to fight with the
bears for food. Some food was tossed into a shallow pond so only the
bears were able to reach, not sure if this was done deliberately.

After touring the refuge, the ride to Seward continued. As we entered
route 9, we ended up in the valleys and fjords that open up straight to
the pacific to the south. The vegetation became even more oceanic. Trees
on average were taller than those seen along Talkeetna - Anchorage. The
air was even more moisturized. Kenai lake was pristine. Fjords slowly
gave way to an open seascape. It was again fortunate that the sky was
clear instead of pouring rain characteristic of the season. After all,
Seward sits on the same latitude as the quintessential oceanic city of
Bergen, Norway, only a bit more encircled by land.

Anyway, we finally arrived in Seward a little after the sunset. Driven
by curiosity, we invaded a local Safeway and indulged in a pack of
reindeer sausages.

<img src="./media/image176.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Reindeer sausages seized from a Safeway in Seward)

Seward has a harbor in the south-facing fjord, where seafood restaurants
cluster, serving fresh catches of the day. Ray's Waterfront was one of
them, particularly recommended by Michael. Unfortunately, it turned out
it was temporarily closed. So instead we picked a nearby restaurant
called Chinooks for dinner. The slogan of the restaurant is “work like a
captain, play like a pirate”.

On top of the harbor area, the picturesque waterfront park was another
highlight. From the park, one can see the Resurrection Bay, sandwiched
by lofty mountains on the 2 sides, extends into the vast Pacific.

Our plan for the rest of the night was to chase the auroras once again -
who would get tired of them? The aurora forecast and the crystal clear
sky were both in our favor. We drove to a recommended spot 15 min north
of Seward. This was a trailhead at a relatively high altitude, not far
from Bear Lake. Near midnight, there was a serious group of cars waiting
there already. It was a polite group; the lights were all off. This
scene reinforced our belief. As per experience, Shelly took her camera,
and took a scan across the possible angles in the sky, checking if there
was any hint of green after a 30s exposure. Surprisingly, it turned out
there wasn’t any. To further seek evidence, I walked up to a car close
by. In the car were sitting a couple. They were Seward residents, having
recently moved from the state of Georgia for jobs. They kindly
recommended a couple of eateries and activities in Seward. But it turned
out that they didn’t have further information or judgement regarding the
aurora either. We waited in the dark for another half an hour or so.
Under such uncertainty, excitement quickly turned into impatience and
fatigue, and so we decided to call it a day…

…only prematurely. Later, we found out that people reported auroras over
Seward not long after we left — Life is such a tramedy!

<img src="./media/image717.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:13.59375in" />

(report of auroras over Seward on Twitter)

We had our luck restored pretty quickly the next morning. Our plan was
to tour Exit Glacier right outside of Seward. Not known to us
beforehand, the park was scheduled to be closed for the entire winter
season starting 9am that day. We arrived at the park at about 8:40am
only to find out about this. And we found ourselves becoming the last
visitors of the park in 2020; the rangers closed up the entrance after
we entered.

We took a 0.6 mile trail (“overlook trail”) that led us to the terminus
of the glacier. Once again we had nature all to ourselves. There were
curious Alaskan hares occasionally sticking their heads out of the
bushes. As a protective scheme, these hares would turn white in winter
and grey in summer. As for the glacier, the signs in the park
demonstrating how the seemingly static glacier is on the move and carves
bedrock were pretty informative. The glacier itself was not the most
impressive in hindsight, compared to Matanuska Glacier and Worthington
Glacier, which we later saw. But this stroke of luck made us happy. At
the park exit, we expressed our gratitude to the rangers for not locking
us out.

<img src="./media/image551.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Exit Glacier)

After Exit Glacier, it was time to say goodbye to Seward. With its
petite size, Seward had an uplifting feel to it. The town was well
planned and maintained. The surroundings exude nature and a sense of
exploration. The waterfront was clean and delightful. Even with a missed
date with the aurora, and a rushed tour of the Exit Glacier, we loved
Seward. Next up, off to Valdez we go!

<img src="./media/image651.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(The inside of our lodging in Seward — Anderson House Lodging)

Following the scenic route through which we came to Seward, we cruised
back to Anchorage. We had crab rolls for lunch at Humpy’s. Took a stroll
on the chilly Coastal Trail near Elderberry Park. Saw the Denali
mountain range one more time over the waterway of Knik Arm. Visited a
Wild Scoops location for a taste of Alaskan ice creams. Fully charged,
we were ready for the longest drive in this trip: Anchorage to Valdez.

<img src="./media/image121.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(View from Coastal Trail in Anchorage)

The road to Valdez consists of 2 major sections. The first is between
Anchorage and Glennallen, following an eastward course. The second
section between Glennallen and Valdez is southernly. Going deep into the
1st section, we entered a region right to the north of Chugach
mountains, falling again into an interior Alaska-like territory. The
imposing mountains block the moisture coming from the Pacific to the
south. Lush vegetation gradually transitioned into desolate, formidable
terrains.

Halfway through the 1st section, we made a stop at Glacier View. Glacier
View offers a vista point to the 27 mile long Matanuska Glacier in the
Chugach mountains. In order to get a closer look, a guided tour turned
out to be a must. Off Alaska 1 (Glenn hwy), over the Matanuska River,
down to the terminus of the glacier, the guide Gin guided us in a pickup
truck during this little excursion, as the road to the terminus was
rough and without any sign.

Gin explained that over the last 60 years, the Matanuska Glacier had
receded for hundreds of meters; 60 years ago, the edge of the ice
touched on the parking lot, whereas today the parking lot and the
closest ice were separated by grey, loose, permafrost. Interestingly,
she also seemed to suggest that the ice was building up on the top, and
that it would take 600 years for the accumulation to move to the bottom.

<img src="./media/image238.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Matanuska Glacier)

The rest of the section 1 was characterized by Mount Drum — a prominent
volcano that the road would constantly point to, while everywhere else
was perfectly flat. The view was reminiscent of that of Mt. Rainer seen
from Rainier Vista on the UW campus.

<img src="./media/image1.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Mt. Drum seen from Glenn hwy)

Section 2 began as we hit Glennallen and took a right turn onto Alaska 4
(Richardson hwy). This was where we were joined again by the oil
pipeline that stretches the entire Alaska from Deadhorse in the north
and Valdez in the South.

The night had fallen at this point. We arduously pushed forward, uphill
to Thompson Pass, the highest point in the section. The road saw a
scarily sharp sheer along the contour to cross the pass. Finally, after
7 hours of driving and a final downhill stretch, we reached our airbnb
on the outskirts of Valdez. Having never had to be so focused on a long
drive on a narrow icy mountain road in the dark before, I was hopelessly
gassed at that point.

Nevertheless, after settling down, the restless spirits and the good kp
index lured us into another aurora hunt. Who are we to back down? First
we tried Robe Lake, where a glimpse of the aurora could be recognized at
the back of the mountains. It was dim and partially blocked. In order to
get a less obstructed panorama, we drove up to Thompson Pass again. The
temperature dropped; the spirits were up! Unfortunately, when we
arrived, the aurora was already gone. As we were left alone on this wet
and windy pass, a line from Harry Potter suddenly crossed my mind: “*The
night wet and windy, two children dressed as pumpkins waddling across
the square and the shop windows covered in paper spiders, all the tawdry
Muggle trappings of a world in which they did not believe...*”.

It was most likely the weather and the condition at the moment that
prompted the memory of that line in *Bathilda’s Secret.* However,
Halloween was indeed around the corner, and so was an important
election. There was much uncertainty around it, as well as life ahead.
The anxiety and vigilance in that line suddenly became numbingly
sobering. It was a bone-chilling night on Thompson Pass.

<img src="./media/image276.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.22917in" />

(Auroras seen by Robe Lake)

The last day of the journey was here.

Our mission was to get back to Anchorage to catch an overnight flight
back to SFO. With yesterday’s experience, we took it real easy with
driving. We made our first stop at Kesley Dock in downtown Valdez. This
was where ferries went out for whale watching in summer time. For now,
the dock quietly stood by the green-blue Port Valdez.

<img src="./media/image728.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Kelsey Dock)

Our next stop was The Solomon Gulch Hatchery. This is a salmon hatchery.
In early falls, salmon would enter in the hatchery using a fish ladder
swimming upstream following their natural instinct, and their eggs would
be harvested by the hatchery. What’s interesting is that black bears
would also come to the narrow inlet built at the estuary of the creek,
and effortlessly feast on these salmon. It was off season at the time.
There wasn’t any salmon or bear around, but there were an abundance of
seagulls and sea lions. They were playing and munching something. Such a
curious ecosystem :-)

<img src="./media/image750.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(the creek where salmon would work their up to the hatchery)

Next, we found our way to Worthington Glacier, a little after Thompson
Pass. The snowiest place in Alaska, and the place where the heaviest
snow was recorded in all of the US, Thompson Pass-Worthington Glacier
area also has a mediterranean precipitation pattern. Due to the dense
vegetation, we didn’t find any easy access to the terminus of the
glacier; we drove down to the end of a rough road, to get as a close
look as possible.

<img src="./media/image12.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Worthington Glacier)

The rest was a 5-hour drive back to Anchorage. Near Glacier View, there
was a mountain which Gin said looked like a lion head (and was named as
such), if you look in the same direction in which we were driving.
Unfortunately, too dull as I am, I failed to notice any resemblance
despite knowing which mountain that Gin referred to.

In the evening, we finally made it to Anchorage. A Korean food fest was
in order to warm up 3 depleted Asian stomachs. With hours left before
boarding, we decided to try making an appointment with the aurora one
final time. This was not a random decision, as the realistic kp index at
the time was off the charts, the highest of the highest of the few days
when we were in Alaska. Seeing as there were promising cracks in the
cloud cover, we soldiered on to a trailhead in the foothills of the
Chugach mountains, with high hopes. The location reinforced our
confidence too — as it turned out, there were again cars waiting there
like they knew *IT* would happen (remember Seward)! We waited, scanned
the sky with the high-tech camera as we would normally do, and waited.
The promising cracks in the clouds were still there, but all we saw was
city lights in the long-exposure pictures of that clear patch of the
sky. It looked like the higher being that used to send out beautiful
auroras missed another appointment. For lack of better things to do, we
comforted ourselves by playing party games in the car. It turned out
that these guessing games were super, super fun, easily helping us
forget about the bail.

All good things must come to an end. We returned the rental car in the
airport when the time was due, and then hopped on the flight. Anchorage
→ Portland → SFO.

It looked like that was it with Alaska.

I was tired, planning on sleeping through the overnight flight anyway.
Right as I was about to fall asleep, in an unexpected turn of events,
Yidi came up from the back seat, urging me to look out. I’d never forget
what I saw. I saw two bands of shining, dancing auroras spanning the
entire northern horizon. It was a shame that we didn’t take a picture,
as those northern lights were the brightest of the 3 that we saw. The
aurora was so perfect that it looked like a joke. We’ve done it again!

So, that was Alaska giving us a final surprise, and I was really happy
it closed out the way it did.

Time to wrap up this long journey. I was so amazed by how beautiful and
pristine Alaska was. I enjoyed every part of the trip as much as anyone
could — the auroras, the wild and the sheltered animals, the nature that
we really felt we owned, the big and the small glaciers, the Alaskan
cities and the unmanned structures in the absolute wild, everyone we met
on the road, those who told stories, those who gave us pointers, and
those who simply shared some time with us, the mountains that I couldn’t
get tired of and the snow that I was kind of tired of, and the energy
bars that kept us going on the solitary highways and the more refined
food found in the Alaskan restaurants — all these intrigued and
tremendously enriched my mind for good. But most importantly, I was so
happy and grateful that I got to do this with 2 great people, great,
great friends. We looked out for each other in the formidable north. We
shared all the absolute thrills and weathered all the absolutely
difficult rides together. We had many a stimulating conversation and
silly game, and these memories still made me smile all the time. We made
sure tomorrow would be just as fun as today for each other with proper
plans — the logistics couldn’t be simpler with their thoughtfulness.
Together we conquered some of the northernmost locations on earth,
managed to have watched the latest episodes of some of the most popular
variety shows without WiFi or cellular signals, and even succeeded in
getting some work done in the most challenging environment humanly
imaginable. Couldn’t be happier. Couldn’t be more thankful.

Here’s to great adventures in the future!

# **Trip to US southwest, the grand staircase**

Enjoying a 9-day holiday thanks to our company’s policy, we went on an
expedition to the US southwest during the thanksgiving week. US
southwest was picked because (1) Michael the tour guide from
[<u>https://www.quora.com/q/lxsbbufyxgfyypve/Trip-to-Alaska-the-formidable-north</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-Alaska-the-formidable-north),
who is seasoned with road trips, spoke highly of it, (2) none of us had
been there, (3) it’s somewhere between bay area and New York City,
relatively easy for people from 2 coasts to meet, and (4) I looked
forward to picking up camping skills from my teammates, and the
landscapes in US southwest are great for camping.

# **Nov. 22**

Inna, Cris and I arrived at the McCarran airport in Vegas from the west
coast, Guillaume from the east coast. Both flights arrived almost at the
same time around 11am. Upon getting the car, we drove to the nearby
Thomas & Mack Center for a covid test. Like many great structures during
covid times, this arena, which has hosted legendary NBA games, like 2007
NBA all-star for example, has been converted to a test center.

<img src="./media/image588.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Thomas & Mack Center in Vegas)

Frontier airlines charge for carry-ons, so we packed very lightly.
Sleeping bags and pads were not packed, and for those we went to an REI
(Boca park, Las Vegas). Canisters and pots and some other disposable
stuff were later cleared to make room for these camping gears on the
flight back.

At night, we arrived in our motel in Hurricane, Utah. Hurricane is a
suburb of St. George, and is half an hour from Zion National Park, which
we were going to the next day. I have long been interested in the
northern limit of Mexican fan palms, which is supposed to cut through
the St. George area. Truthfully enough, in St. George, palm trees were
still seen lining streets, but as soon as we drove past St. George in
the northeast direction to Hurricane, I was not able to spot a single
Mexican fan palm any more.

<img src="./media/image733.png"
style="width:6.27083in;height:3.875in" />

(Mexican fan palm distribution in the US)

# **Nov. 23**

We woke up to an overcast sky in the morning, which was rare for
southwest Utah in November, because it was past the monsoon season, and
the northeasterly atmospheric river had yet to pick up steam here.
However, a packed schedule ahead dismissed the abyss.

We came to the northwest edge of Zion to explore the Kolob Canyon area,
specifically the Taylor Creek Trail. The trail goes in parallel with
Middle Fork Taylor Creek. It’s a tributary of Ash Creek, which
eventually joins Virgin River near Hurricane, which in turn joins
Colorado River in Nevada. As the trail goes deep into the canyon, the
mountains on the two sides grow in height, and the gap between the
mountains narrows. Finally, the canyon ends where the mountains
converge, and the trail concludes at the source of the creek.

<img src="./media/image203.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:15.45833in" />

<img src="./media/image100.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:16.625in" />

(the source of Taylor Creek)

This was a relatively easy hike. There was barely any elevation gain,
and the entire out and back hike took 3 hours.

Next, we picked up Inna from the motel in Hurricane, who was doing some
studying in the morning and so couldn’t join. Following the Virgin River
river line, we entered Zion again from its south side. Near the visitor
center, we entered the Watchman trail. Watchman trail goes up to a
foothill in the Virgin River / Zion canyon. While still sandwiched by
taller mountains even on the summit, the trail end offered us a first
glimpse of the valley, which is entirely carved out by Virgin river.
With autumn colors glowing everywhere in the valley and red-white rocks
decorating the higher elevations, the view was spectacular.

<img src="./media/image345.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image257.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(views on Watchman Trail)

At night, we camped at a Bureau of Land Management (BLM) campground near
the junction of Meadow Creek and Route 9. This camp site is outside of
the east entrance of Zion, on the same level as the mountains in Zion.
Hence, it was colder than the bottom of the canyon, where we visited
during the daytime. Also, at this moment, the storm was gone; the
overcast sky was replaced with an exceptionally bright moon,
overshadowing all the stars. Crisper it was than the daytime.

<img src="./media/image96.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:1.84375in" />

(the grand staircase; our campground is between the white cliffs and the
gray cliffs)

I camped before in Washington State, for Mount Adams, and in Deception
Pass State Park. During those trips, I learned how to set up a tent. But
building a campfire and cooking over it were out of the scope, because
there were energy bars and snacks. This time, I learned everything all
over again, and was really happy with the result. We collected dry tree
branches first, lit up small branches to help the fire grow to kindle
the larger ones. We then set up 2 tents following their respective
instructions, and in parallel, boiled water to cook pasta. The most
interesting part was collecting woods. Since it was really cold outside
the 2 meter radius of the campfire, spotting feasible branches, brining
them back, and bringing them to the pit to help the fire grow and myself
warm up were a satisfying process. In the end, we had a delicious pasta
meal in the warmth of the campfire, chased with vino navegado (wine,
orange skin, cinnamon sticks, etc.), all behind the mastermind of Inna,
who is from Chile.

<img src="./media/image48.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image4.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image466.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image213.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Navegado is a drink that is particularly consumed during the winter. It
was very popular in the 1960s. This drink remains associated with the
counter-culture and the protests in the collective imagination. Chilean
youth used to meet to debate, discuss and lead protests. During these
evenings, empanadas and navegado were widely consumed.)

Camping in the wilderness in southwest Utah was quite an experience. It
was a few celsius degrees below 0 at the lowest at night, which is a bit
below the comfortable temperature my tent is designed for. With all the
clothes on in the sleeping bag, the weather was bearable. It was the
coyote howling that was enthralling and frightening. Without any prior
encounter, I thought the sounds were made by a wolf pack at first, and
got quite scared — could the tents protect us from a wolf attack, and do
we stand a chance fighting wolfs with bare hands? These were the
thoughts that crossed my mind when that strange, primitive sound broke
the silence of the night. Guillaume, who has rich experiences, put us at
ease by confirming these were coyotes, which were much less dangerous
and normally wouldn’t attack humans. Two coyote packs had a good
10-minute chat, one from a far distance, one 200m from our tents,
judging from the magnitude of the sound.

Finally, the silence resumed. I had an okay night’s sleep, ready for
more strenuous hikes tomorrow.

# **Nov. 24**

To access the core of Zion, i.e., the headwaters of Virgin River, one
would need to either drive or take a shuttle. In July, Zion shut down
the shuttle service and had a limit of 80 cars everyday in the park,
because of Covid. Right now, cars weren’t allowed inside, but instead
shuttles were operating again. The shuttle tickets were released a day
prior at 9am on recreation.gov. First come first serve. So four of us
each grabbed a time on the recreation.gov app to maximize our chance,
promptly at 9am the day before. A few moments later, we were notified
that we got the tickets.

<img src="./media/image306.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(of course we need to be one Turkey Vulture apart to social distance at
shuttle stops)

The core of Zion features 2 iconic hikes, Angel’s Landing, and The
Narrows. Our first stop is at the trailhead of Angel’s Landing. Unlike
the Watchman trail, Angels’s Landing goes all the way up to the next
level of the grand staircase. We started at the ground level. That is
the level of Virgin River. The morning sunlight brightened up the
mountains peaks with golden glows. Down in the canyon, the cacti were
not exposed to the sun yet.

<img src="./media/image568.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(trailhead of Angel’s Landing)

A view of the canyon emerged as we went further up. However, the
imposing mountains on the opposite side of the canyon reminded us that
this was just the beginning.

<img src="./media/image501.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image481.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(outlook of the canyon and the lofty mountains on the back)

After a big switchback followed by a zigzag segment was the beginning of
the true wonder. Only on from here did the game begin. The danger was
that from here on the trail sits on a sharp rocky ridge, with very risky
chain protection. The reward was that the view was truly breathtaking.
If the bottom was the Yosemite Valley, Angel’s Landing was the half
dome, *joyas de la corona* of Zion.

<img src="./media/image361.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image516.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(the beginning of the chain protected section — scout lookout)

Unfortunately, I couldn’t take as many pictures as I would have liked on
this final section, because my heart was racing for the high stakes.
Falls could easily be fatal. But at a few spots where safety was
(almost) guaranteed, I managed to snap a few.

<img src="./media/image59.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image639.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(the task was to reach the top via the ridge)

But I was made for such challenges. Gone were the days when I couldn’t
finish Mt Stuart in Washington due to exhaustion, or when I’d struggle a
lot doing half marathons due to lack of training. Soon I found myself
acclimated to the steepness of the slope, the height, and kept a
reliable balance even without the chain. True happiness emerged from
this trust in and reliance on myself. And towards other passengers this
abundance radiated, as I began to make way on the narrow trail for
hikers coming from the opposite direction, and to comfortably use room
others made for me to proceed. In the end, as we reached the actual
Angel’s Landing, the reward was sweet.

<img src="./media/image709.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image556.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image533.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(views from Angel’s Landing)

The descent turned out to be much easier than the ascent, because of the
acquired experience, and because gravity was in our favor. During the
ascent, I didn’t pay much attention to the chipmunks, but apparently
there were plenty of chipmunks during the whole hike, trying to get a
bite from hikers. A ranger would tell us the next day that chipmunks
shouldn’t be fed because (1) they’d be subject to hunger in the
off-season, (2) they carry diseases and you might get bitten, (3) human
food is not healthy for wildlife, and (4) feeding attracts a large
concentration of chipmunks, which in turn attracts snakes, imposing
danger on tourists.

Our next mission was to explore Virgin River further upstream. A shuttle
took us to the end of Scenic Drive, where Riverside Walk Trail begins.
Like Taylor Creek Trail in Kolob Canyon, the gap between the mountains
becomes narrower and narrower at this point. This gave rise to a scenic
riverside trail, and the paved road shall not proceed.

Water streams emerge from cracks on the rocks. Sunlight couldn’t reach
the narrow valley. All these bring about a cool, subterranean world.
Further upstream is where the Riverside Walk ends and The Narrows
begins. The difference is on Riverside Walk you still walk on land,
where on The Narrows you get your feet wet, because the gorge is so
narrow that the trail constantly crosses the water. Not wearing anything
waterproof, and since the water was cold, we left The Narrows for a
future adventure.

<img src="./media/image99.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(There is a crack in everything; that’s how the water gets out)

<img src="./media/image560.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(The narrows begins where riverside walk ends)

20 minutes before the sunset, we embarked on our final hike of the day -
Canyon Overlook Trail. This is a trail on the east side of the main
canyon. It features a smaller canyon, carved out by Pine Creek, a
tributary of Virgin River. The trail begins at the east end of the
Zion-Mount Carmel Tunnel, by Pine Creek. After a mile, it ascends to The
Great Arch, overlooking the junction of the smaller canyon and the main
canyon.

<img src="./media/image75.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(view at The Great Arch)

At night, we camped at a Dispersed USFS campsite near route 12 right
past the Red Canyon area. This is a campground close to Bryce Canyon,
which we wanted to go tomorrow. This campsite was different from
yesterday’s in that a thin layer of snow covered the ground, and the
source of firewood — pine trees were sparser. There was snow because
this campsite is on a higher level on the grand staircase than
yesterday’s, hence higher elevation, and because its location is farther
northeast. It is drier because of the rain shadow caused by the mountain
range on the west. This created a situation where we had to endure much
colder temperatures at night, and where creating a campfire was more
difficult. In fact, this night turned out to be the most difficult for
us. None of us got a minute of good sleep. First, there was the -11
celsius degree temperature. Also, the campfire was out in the middle of
the night as the firewood was burned out. Those who slept in the tents
close to the fire suffered from this incident. Because my sleep bag
wouldn’t work with that temperature, I opted to sleep the car.
Nonetheless, when I woke up from *awakeness* the next morning, I found
that even the bottled water in the car frozen up.

<img src="./media/image727.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image549.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(a hot dog meal to weather a cold night)

The stunning view we woke up to the next morning instilled much hope.

<img src="./media/image607.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(“When the dawn comes / Tonight will be a memory too / And a new day
will begin”)

# **Nov. 25**

With no shuttle ticket to fight for, no shuttle to catch, the drive to
Bryce Canyon visitor center was pretty chill. At 9, we arrived at Sunset
Point in Bryce Canyon. Immediately upon seeing this amphitheater setup
at the rim, my breath was taken away.

<img src="./media/image191.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image205.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image725.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Bryce Canyon at first sight)

<img src="./media/image395.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(The amphitheater overlook at Inspiration Point)

The rim lies at the highest level of the grand staircase. The idea is to
descend from the rim at Sunset Point and do 2 contiguous loop trails
down in the canyon — Navajo Loop Trail, and Peekaboo Loop Trail. This
would lead us through the pink cliffs to the same level as the top of
the gray cliffs, which are still one layer higher than Angel’s Landing
in Zion.

As we descended, we entered a world of Hindu temples. Guillaume, who’s
been to Cambodia, commented that the structure reminded him of Angkor
Wat. Indeed, these columns looked too organized to originate from
nature. The pointy peaks, the symmetric outlines, the uniformly
stratified composition, and the fact that these structures always come
in an array easily give people the illusion that they were religious
institutions built by adherents to communicate with God. Hoodoos are a
technical term they gave to these thin rocks.

Occasionally, we were not passing **by** these structures, we were
passing **through** them. It was at these moments that I was even more
amazed at how elaborate they were — there were doors and windows **on**
these structures and bridges **over** them! As a matter of fact, there
was an entire wall of rocks with holes through them, which they dubbed
Wall of Windows. There was a shorter trail named Wall Street too.

<img src="./media/image624.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(citadel)

<img src="./media/image10.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(two bridges)

<img src="./media/image474.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(fortress)

<img src="./media/image22.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(castles)

<img src="./media/image47.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(wall of windows)

<img src="./media/image435.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(hindu temples)

<img src="./media/image545.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(a human shaped tree)

<img src="./media/image396.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(horizon through the window)

<img src="./media/image469.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(the various shapes of the sedimentary rocks)

Back at the rim at 1pm, we had a guided tour with a ranger from Sunset
Point to Sunrise Point. The tour lasted for about an hour, during which
the ranger, who studied geology in college, expertly explained about (1)
the formation of the rocks, which have to do with Farallon Plate, (2)
the types of vegetations here that can survive the extreme heat in the
summer, and the dry weather throughout the year, and (3) the animals in
this region. The presentation was super clear, informative, and
engaging. For this, my admiration for people who study geology and the
related studies of nature grew substantially after the presentation. And
yet, this tour was totally free. This made me realize and appreciate the
fact that rich countries can afford to pay rangers with such expertise.
I also appreciated that Guillaume recommended a book named Watership
Down when the topic was prairie dogs inhabiting Bryce Canyon. Apparently
he learned quite a bit about leadership from the book.

<img src="./media/image555.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(The Bryce Canyon ranger was the one who warned us of the danger of
feeding wild chipmunks)

After the guided tour, we drove to Inspiration point, which is a little
down south from Sunset Point at the rim. Inspiration point offers a
comprehensive overview of the amphitheater from a different angle. The
hoodoo rocks are primarily circumscribed by the semicircle rim, and
Sunset Point and Inspiration Point are the 2 tips of the semicircle.

<img src="./media/image222.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:2.55208in" />

(a panorama at Inspiration point)

Wrapping up Bryce Canyon, we concluded our exploration in Utah. Our next
stop was in Nevada. Following route 15, we passed through Cedar City and
St. George, and entered Nevada at night through Mesquite. Mesquite felt
like a liberation from the Mormon and gun culture in Utah. As soon as we
entered Mesquite, Nevada, casinos and a ton of liquor stores were on
display.

<img src="./media/image595.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.27083in" />

(a random grocery store in Orderville, Utah, featuring gun-shaped
lighters, and a random liquor store in Mesquite, Nevada, featuring a
booze-themed social distancing sign)

With the lessons from last night, we picked a much warmer campsite, in
Mojave Desert. The exact location was near the intersection of
Northshore Road and Sand Mine Road near Valley of Fire State Park, which
we were visiting the next day. Due to its southwestern location and it
being on the lowest level of the grand staircase, the exact lowest
temperature at night was above 0 celsius. Different from the previous 2
campsites, this site was in a true desert; Only bushes grew. The
firewood to build a campfire with in turn came from branches of these
bushes. It took them less time to burn out. But in a warmer weather, we
didn’t need a big long-lasting fire either. With 2 days of experiences
pitching tents and building campfires like an apprentice, I decided to
take charge of these this time. The end result was that everyone wined
and dined well :-)

<img src="./media/image742.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image140.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(me taking care of the usual business :-) “In fra le tende, bandir la
cena. In una sera così serena”)

During the fireside chat, Inna told about her hitchhiking and camping
trip from Puerto Montt to Coyhaique in Chile with a female friend when
she was in college. I admired that 2 girls decided to do a trip that’s
at least 660km long. On the other hand, I was shocked when Inna told me
that it was common for Chileans to hitchhike like that in Chilean
Patagonia. She added that in fact, it was especially easier for girls to
go on such trips because guys would have a harder time stopping a car.
Reflecting on that general lifestyle, Inna lamented that the Latin
culture embedded too much hedonism, which kept people from pursuing
bigger achievements. For instance, during college years, young people
would party all day long whenever there’s a school break. After college,
they would find themselves either unemployed or working a random job. I
found her perspective very interesting because growing up immersed in
that culture, she could actually see another side of it. That was
perhaps why she chose to come to America for a different life.

# **Nov. 26**

After a good night of sleep, awaiting was an amazing desert landscape.
Under the clear blue sky, nothing blocked the view. Utility poles
extended to miles away. Recreational vehicles (RVs) scattered around.
Short bushes dotted the landscape. Hills made up of red rocks could be
seen from afar.

<img src="./media/image194.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image558.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image443.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(woke up to a desert scenery in Nevada)

Our plan for Valley of Fire State Park included (1) Elephant Rock Loop
(2) White Domes Loop (3) Fire Wave Trail (4) Atlatl Rock. Each features
a different rock formation, surface firmness and surface stability .

Elephant Rock is a mile-long loop trail that offers a classic wild west
scene. A long straight road, curious rock formations, a fire-like zeal,
and tenacious desert vegetation, are all here. It was interesting to see
a phenomenon where deep straight lines cut across multiple giant rocks
too.

<img src="./media/image410.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image681.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image473.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(views on Elephant rock loop trail)

White Domes are a different world from Elephant Rock. It was as if you
came to a beach in Southern California, as delicate white sands are all
over the place, and the rocks are white for the most part. Intermingled
were some red rocks, apparently eroding at a different speed.

<img src="./media/image748.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image722.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:11.125in" />

<img src="./media/image726.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(views on White Domes loop trail)

Fire Wave, reminded me of Turpan Depression in Xinjiang, China. This was
my favorite trail in the park because the rocks were the most
structured, like those in Bryce Canyon but with smoother curves, like
lava flows or rolling fires.

<img src="./media/image228.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image39.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image236.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(views on Fire Wave Trail)

Last but not least, Atlatl Rock. On the rock there are petroglyphs
written by ancient Native American tribes. These petroglyphs are not yet
interpretable, but I believe machine translation technology will be
advanced enough some day so that these ancient scripts are understood
again.

<img src="./media/image528.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(petroglyphs on Atlatl Rock)

Overall, Valley of Fire was a quite nice place. More rock formations can
be seen here than in Zion or Bryce Canyon. If a park’s status as a
national park is based on the variety of rocks, Valley of Fire would
quality. Also, I could imagine some of these must be very similar to
Antelope Canyon, which we thought of going but didn’t, in the interest
of time. But we were very happy about the variety of experiences we had
in three different parks across Utah and Nevada.

We headed for our hotel in Vegas after finishing the exploration at the
park. The idea was to enjoy an evening in a good hotel because we
deserve a very good night of sleep and shower after camping for 3 days
straight, and because it was Thanksgiving day. Treasure Island was what
we picked, right across from Trump International Hotel. The sunset was
beautiful viewed from the 30’s floor; there was no cloud obstruction but
a giant ad on the exterior of the building.

<img src="./media/image425.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Sunset at Treasure Island in Vegas)

Guillaume booked a nice restaurant called Mon Ami Gabi under Eiffel
Tower for Thanksgiving dinner. Being French, Guillaume explained the
concept of French fine dining and typical French urban cafes to us, as
well as the table etiquette and characteristics of the French cuisine.
We ended up ordering the thanksgiving meal, where each of us had

- smoked salmon (pumpkin spice brioche, black peppercorn crème fraîche,
  chives) as the first course,

- fairytale pumpkin soup (amaretto cream puff) as the second course,

- herb-roasted turkey (served with caramelized yams, garlic and sage
  cornbread stuffing, haricots verts, potato purée, bourbon gravy,
  vanilla-cranberry relish) as the main course, and

- pecan chocolate tart (caramel sauce, vanilla whipped cream) as the
  desert.

Cris was the only one who ended up devouring his portion, citing living
in Romania before 1998 leading to lots of hunger and his determination
to not waste food thereafter as the reason. For me and Guillaume, the
cold leftovers were consumed the next day, in the spirit of thanksgiving
traditions.

<img src="./media/image71.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(French Thanksgiving dinner at Mon Ami Gabi during the main course)

# **Nov. 27**

In the morning, we had some time before Guillaume left for NYC. We
decided to do Taiwanese food in Chinatown. Unlike French fine dining,
the setting was casual. We had numerous dishes ranging from oyster
omelettes, scallion pancakes, fried Singaporean noodles, minced pork
rice, radish cakes, potstickers, pork buns, intestines noodle soup,
braised beef burritos to soy milk. Based on the exit poll of my friends,
oily and savory Chinese food like potstickers and scallion pancakes were
favored over sweet food like oyster omelettes and over non-savory
non-sweet food like soy milk and beef burritos. Intestines got none of
our vote either. I mistook it for rice noodle rolls based only on the
menu. Overall, it was funny to see their reactions to authentic
Taiwanese/Chinese breakfast food, because these restaurants tended to
focus on getting Chinese-speaking customers only and non Asians dining
here was a rarity.

<img src="./media/image489.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Chinese Black Friday breakfast at Yi Mei Champion Taiwan Deli)

For the rest of the day, me, Cris and Inna toured the Strip. During the
pandemic, the street was not as half crowded as usual. We went for
burgers in Hell’s Kitchen but it didn’t serve burgers due somehow to
Covid. Instead we visited Gordon Ramsey Burger but it didn’t allow
dine-in without a reservation. Even the statue of liberty was wearing a
mask. One highlight though was the finding of a döner kebab shop on the
Strip, which gave Berlin vibes.

<img src="./media/image644.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image508.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Strip views in Vegas)

We managed to catch the evening flight after rushing through the rental
car return and everything. Back in SFO, tech startup ads were seen
literally on every wall. This was very different from the gambling
machines seen everywhere in the McCarran airport, which created an
entertaining atmosphere. On the contrary, this was a reminder that it
was business time once again.

Generally, this had been a fantastic expedition. We worked together to
make sure the plan was good. I learned a great deal about camping. I
learned that with great power comes great responsibility in such
teamwork. The most memorable was surviving the extremely cold night. We
were all happy about being rewarded afterwards with the majestic
amphitheater view in Bryce Canyon.

On to the next adventure!

# **Trip to US southwest continued, Sonoran Desert**

In the [<u>last
episode</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-US-southwest-the-grand-staircase),
we explored 3 of the most distinct ecoregions of southwestern US — the
grand staircase on Colorado Plateau, bordering Great Basin, and came
down to Mojave Desert following the altitude gradient. Although the
memories of camping on the cold plateau, and then coming back to our
senses in the mild desert, are still fresh, came Christmas, me and my
cousin made a last minute decision to explore 2 other major ecoregions
of southwestern US — California coastal sage and chaparral (or, SoCal :)
), and Sonoran Desert.

<img src="./media/image734.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:6.59375in" />

(terrestrial ecoregions of the US southwest )

**Dec. 23**

Hours before the trip, my cousin was informed she got her dream job
offer. As part of the celebration, the decision to go on this trip to
Santa Barbara was made 5 hours before midnight. After quick packing, a
long drive ensued.

After midnight, following 101 and 154, we overcame San Ynez Mountains
and approached Santa Barbara. As soon as we reached the southern slopes
of the mountain range, greeting us were the warm socal palms en masse.
Despite late in the night, the sudden change of vegetation was very much
felt. The mostly ever-green nature of the vegetation, and the diversity
of the palm leaves and other plants dotting the night sky, were easily
noticeable. Glowing was a more active vibe than the California interior
chaparral and woodlands landscape typical of bay area.

A warm Greek couple welcomed us into their house. Being very careful in
the face of the pandemic, they took our temperatures upon our entry to
the house. One of the rooms was turned into a 2-bed guest room for
airbnb. The room was nicely decorated with art works featuring ancient
Greece and China.

<img src="./media/image73.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(art works in the guest room)

In the morning, we visited Hendrys Beach and Campus Point Beach. Whereas
Hendrys Beach saw many locals hanging out, Campus Point Beach on UCSB
campus was very quiet in comparison. The only competition to pick up the
abundance of the beautiful seashells was against the seagulls :) In
addition to the tranquility, the weather was gorgeous. While it was
pouring in bay area, down here in socal, the sky was delightfully clear.
Always preferring sun over rain, I couldn’t help but appreciate this
last-minute decision to go on such a trip :)

<img src="./media/image248.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image537.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image427.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image721.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Campus Point Beach)

We spent the afternoon driving to the next airbnb in Desert Hot Springs,
which is located in the west part of Sonoran Desert. On the way, we
stopped by at [<u>Kobe’s chopper crash
site</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Kobe-is-no-more) in Calabasas. This
afternoon, despite the overcast, the hill was not covered by fog. As a
result, the whole landscape could be seen in full. The closeness of the
crash spot to the hill top, which the chopper almost overcame back in
January, when seen in person, hit different. Growing up watching Kobe’s
games, I couldn’t resist paying tribute to that part of my memory by
doing this little hike to the actual site.

<img src="./media/image117.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image669.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(hiking up to the site)

Laughters and barking could be heard on the trail in this empty
mountain, down from the dog park by the trailhead. Life goes on.

By dinnertime, we reached this legendary restaurant called Mini Kabob in
Glendale. The restaurant has been run by an Armenian family for 25
years. The famed food vlogger Mark Wiens visited this place in his LA
series and spoke highly of it. With this prior knowledge, my first
impression was that the setting of this family business was extremely
simple — simple menu, one table (for dining-in pre-covid). This made me
wonder whether there was a strong correlation between the degree of
greatness of a restaurant and its simplicity. In any case, the
combination plate and the lamb chop plate we ordered were exceptionally
good. (update:
[<u>Hanshu</u>](https://www.quora.com/profile/Hanshu-Zhou) commented
that the tenderness of the lamb chops could have been better :D) The
meat was tender, juicy and flavorful. The rice was aromatic and oily.
The garlic sauce was creamy, fragrant, exotic tasting and yet not
pungent at all. This hearty meal trumped all the middle-eastern /
mediterranean / caucasian food I’ve had so far.

<img src="./media/image16.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image265.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Mini Kabob in Glendale)

We arrived at our airbnb in Desert Hot Springs in the upper Coachella
Valley at night, passing through many huge wind farms along the way.
Coachella Valley is part of Colorado Desert, which makes up the west
part of Sonoran Desert. Sonoran Desert sits to the south of Mojave
Desert. Close to Gulf of California’s warm water, at a relatively low
elevation, and susceptible to summer monsoons, Sonoran Desert features
different flora and fauna from Mojave Desert. For example, desert fan
palms (Washingtonia filifera) are native only to the oases of Sonoran
Desert, while Joshua trees are native only to Mojave Desert. In
colloquial terms, Coachella Valley / Colorado Desert part of Sonoran
Desert is called “low desert”, and the high elevation, California, part
of Mojave Desert is called “high desert”. It was fascinating to see
these differences in person and understand what lead to these
differences after visiting both deserts.

**Dec. 24**

Leaving Coachella Valley, we drove via route 62 (“29 palms highway”) up
to Mojave Desert again to visit Joshua Tree National Park from its
northwestern entrance.

<img src="./media/image613.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(a mall in Desert Hot Springs with Little San Bernardino Mountains in
the background)

The road up to Mojave Desert goes through Morongo Valley, which
separates San Bernardino Mountains and Little San Bernardino Mountains.
Little San Bernardino Mountains in turn separates low desert in the
south from high desert in the north. Very interestingly, as soon as we
entered Mojave through the valley, Joshua trees started to be spotted on
the mountain slopes; they tended to be short and bushy at first. As we
kept on going and reached a town called Yucca Valley, Joshua trees were
seen thriving with greater density and height, some of the specimens so
tall that they were planted like street trees. It was as if we entered a
totally different world with these Joshua trees.

Joshua Tree National Park northwest entrance was right after Yucca
Valley. We started the national park tour by hiking the Boy Scout Trail.
On the trail, regardless of the desert stereotype, many types of
vegetation could be identified. The most prominent were the massive
hordes of Joshua trees. It was a Joshua tree wonderland.

<img src="./media/image300.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image327.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Lush Joshua trees dotting the landscape)

Under these Joshua trees and various cacti, there were usually kangaroo
rat and rabbit burrows. Sometimes these burrows were so close to one
another that when they were stepped on, a part of the network collapses.
So to protect these animals’ habitats, it’s recommended to stay on the
trail.

Next, we checked out a place called Hidden Valley. This is a 1-mile loop
trail featuring rocks of curious formations. It is a climber’s paradise.
These rocks are often not too tall, yet pose interesting challenges with
their various formations. On these rocks one often saw climbing robes
and carabiners.

<img src="./media/image485.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Hidden Valley)

One may ask, how did these rocks come into existence in the middle of a
desert, and how did they take up such shapes? The answer is plate
tectonics and volcanic activity. As explained in the [<u>grand staircase
episode</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-US-southwest-the-grand-staircase),
hundreds of millions of years ago, the collision between the North
American Place and the Farallon Plate produced magma chambers, which
eventually cooled underground into granitic rocks. These rocks cracked
due to pressure, plate movement and groundwater. And groundwater further
shaped these rocks into various formations. As the top soil eroded away,
some of the rocks made their way to the surface.

Aside from the rocks in Hidden Valley, perhaps the most famous rock in
the park — Skull Rock, which looks like a human skull — was formed this
way, the result of groundwater and acidic soil from decaying plants.

Next, we drove to Keys View on the southwest edge of the park, which is
a vista point at a relatively high elevation on Little San Bernardino
Mountains. From here one gets to overlook the entire Coachella Valley,
from the northwest end at Desert Hot Springs, to the east southeast end
at Salton Sea, and all the desert resort towns in between. San Andreas
Fault cuts right through the valley. It was a fascinating revelation
that it was tectonic activities that formed such a huge valley.

<img src="./media/image426.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image374.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(enchanted at Keys View)

At Keys View, it was also very interesting to notice that the Joshua
tree forest end right here, signaling that this is the end of high
desert / Mojave Desert. Apparently, down in Coachella Valley in low
desert / Colorado desert / Sonoran Desert, no Joshua tree could be found
in sight.

Next, we followed Wilson Canyon, and entered the Sonoran Desert part of
the park in its southeast. We came to see Cholla Cactus Garden. This
part of the park differed greatly from the Mojave Desert part of the
park in its vegetation. Instead of miles upon miles of Joshua trees, in
this area one could see endless Cholla cacti. Cholla cacti feature
brighter and lighter-colored thorns/leaves than Joshua trees. And while
they really look fluffy with their furry like thorns, the thorns are
actually hard. Rodents could be seen hanging on these cacti dead with
thorns piercing through the body. It was truly a world of dangerous
beauty!

<img src="./media/image490.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image562.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Profiling Cholla cactus thorns)

Finally, before the nightfall, we went along to see another wonder in
the park. We left the Sonoran Desert part of the park for the northeast
entrance of the park. Here one can find Oasis of Mara. This is a small
patch of oasis in the heart of Mojave Desert. Together with 49 Palm
Oasis and Lost Palm Oasis, Oasis of Mara features a water source in the
desert that cultivates California fan palms native to the area.

<img src="./media/image51.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(California fan palms in Oasis of Mara)

When groundwater hits a fault plane, it rises to the surface and creates
conditions for an oasis. Seeing a small cluster of towering palms amid a
vast dry land of short desert shrubs was truly spectacular.

Right after we wrapped up Joshua Tree National Park, we encountered a
rare desert rain session. The outbreak of the downpour stirred a strong
mud smell. The rain would continue until we entered San Diego county via
route 79 and then 15. Down in San Diego, the sky was clear, and the moon
was bright. It was Christmas Eve, and San Diego was full of holiday
vibes.

<img src="./media/image21.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Holiday spirits in San Diego)

**Dec. 25**

After a Christmas Eve of hiking in Joshua Tree National Park, we spent a
super chill Christmas in San Diego.

In the morning, we checked out from the gorgeous RV airbnb in the Ocean
Beach neighborhood, and explored Ocean Beach. The sun was burning, but
the sea breeze kept the air cool. The Mexican fan palms and all kinds of
tropical looking plants could be seen everywhere in the neighborhood.
People were surfing, strolling, biking, and playing volleyball. Everyone
looked active and laid back at the same time. The ambience was super
charming and delightful :)

<img src="./media/image424.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image379.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image307.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Our RV airbnb with a yard, beach volleyball, and beach houses at Ocean
Beach)

Almost all restaurants were closed on this Christmas morning, but we
found a Chinese restaurant that remained open. Jewish (presumably) and
Chinese folks alike lined up at the business for take-out food. The
fried beef pancakes and pork buns were delicious.

<img src="./media/image491.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(a restaurant serving authentic northern Chinese food on Christmas)

Then we toured the old town district. This was the birthplace of
California, and still preserves numerous historic buildings dating from
the colonial time. Today this district is packed with themed shops and
museums, occupying these historic buildings. We had a blast in the old
town, enjoying the hospitality of the shop owners, the bright colors of
the buildings, souvenirs, and plants, and the beautiful weather.

<img src="./media/image472.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image33.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image574.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image649.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image561.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image581.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image62.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image663.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image502.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image197.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Old Town San Diego State Historic Park)

The exuberant Mexican culture in the park was memorable. Because of
this, we decided to check out the US-Mexico border, specifically the San
Ysidro border crossing.

<img src="./media/image206.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

<img src="./media/image496.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />
