(US-Mexico border near San Ysidro)

At the border crossing, being able to see the skyline of Tijuana close
up was exciting. Next time when we have proper paperwork, we’d
definitely like to visit Tijuana in person!

Also, after this trip, one still remaining major ecoregion I have yet to
explore in southwestern US is Chihuahuan desert. Would love to go on an
adventure for it in the future!

\[1\] [<u>WWF terrestrial ecoregions
v1</u>](http://worldmap.harvard.edu/maps/6046)

# **Giant sequoias or coconut palms?**

Visited sequoia - kings canyon national park, and Miami - Everglades on
the last 2 weekends. The big draws for me are the giant sequoia trees,
the largest trees in the world, in sequoia, and coconut trees, the trees
I’ve long missed since Hainan, in Miami, respectively. Here we go.

**March 27**

Sequoias grow at a certain elevation in the Sierra Nevada mountains.
Below that elevation, central California bushes and farmlands abound.
Above that elevation, vegetation tends towards the alpine kind and gives
way to bare rocks. So, very peculiar of these giant trees. Have to grow
with the right moisture and precipitation level and temperature.

Me and Gaurav stuffed ourselves with Indian food in Apna bazaar for
dinner. We arrived in the town of Visalia on a Friday night before
midnight. Visalia is situated in the Central Valley agricultural
heartland, half an hour away from the mighty Sierra Nevada mountains,
especially the kings canyon and sequoia section. We were ready for some
hikes.

<img src="./media/image383.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Dinner at Apna bazaar in mtv the night before)

The first destination was a nice trail in mineral king area in sequoia
national park. As stupidly adventurous as we were, we didn’t check the
warnings on the website or along the road. This area turned out to be
closed, as we drove half an hour on a dirt road into the mountain only
to see a road blocker.

<img src="./media/image519.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Road blocker at the mineral king entrance of sequoia national park)

But that’s ok. There was a couple camping at the entrance too. They came
here the night before only to find out about the closure. We turned
around and headed for the other entrance towards general Sherman tree.
On the way we passed by a pretty river named squirrel creek.

<img src="./media/image550.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Riverbed of Squirrel creek, which looks like it was hit by a meteorite)

As mentioned earlier, before the giant sequoia trees, we passed through
low elevation bushes and a bevy of spring flowers. The flowers were of
yellow/orange and purple kinds. It was so pretty that we felt this by
itself is good enough for a national park.

<img src="./media/image567.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Flowers at lower elevations)

Suddenly, as we drove up to a certain elevation, we found ourselves in a
different world. Both snow and giant trees showed up. These trees were
largely pine trees, with giant sequoia trees sporadically dotting the
landscape.

<img src="./media/image65.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Pine trees in snow at higher elevations)

Then we arrived at the general Sherman tree. This is the largest tree in
the world by volume. While it is not the tallest, widest or longest
lived, it’s crazy huge. Below is a picture of tree vs man.

<img src="./media/image174.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Largest tree in the world)

The tree itself is mighty worthy of a visit. Not to mention there are
many equally shockingly giant sequoia trees around the general Sherman
tree to complement the experience. Indeed, the giant trees make it so
that this whole place feels like the mysterious entranced forest in
Frozen 2. However, time just ticked through the middle of the day, and
we wanted an actual hike. So, we soldiered on upward to Wolverton
parking lot. There we had the trailhead to Heather lake.

Heather lake is this alpine lake that sits right at the tree line. So,
to see this lake, we gotta trek through all these trees, and conquer the
elevation span that gives rise to these trees.

The trail itself was ok. Not too strenuous. But the snow was killing me.
I didn’t check the snow situation before coming here. As a result, I
slipped every 5 steps on my running shoes. When we finally hiked up to
the lake, I was exhausted. Fortunately the view was rewarding. The lake
was frozen, and the surface was covered by an additional layer of snow.
While Bay Area was sprouting spring, it was a winter wonderland up here.

<img src="./media/image587.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image86.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image339.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image674.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Heather lake winter wonderland)

And we were among the last that day to arrive at heather lake. The good
thing about this is that we were also the last to descend; the mountain
was left all to ourselves. If we don’t talk, there was no sound at all
in the empty mountains to be captured by our delicate auditory system,
but blood pumping and heart beats.

It was another gearless 4 miles of torture. Before the last bit of sun
was down, we made it to the trailhead. The exhaustion was eased by a
nice pizza meal back in Visalia later that night.

<img src="./media/image271.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Dinner at pieology in Visalia)

**March 28**

As unexpected as it was, we actually visited most of Sequoia national
park the first day, given the gears we were equipped with. Seeing as
Visalia is also close to another national park, Kings canyon, we had no
better choice than heading towards our second national park on this
trip.

On our way to Kings canyon, we came across parcels upon parcels of
orange farms. The prosperity of these oranges was too pronounced for us
to not give it a visit. So we stopped by one of them real quick. The
fear of being shot in the head by the owner limited this visit to a mere
span of 5 minutes, culminating in a small loot of Safeway-tasting but
much fresher oranges.

<img src="./media/image653.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Orange farm)

I promised I’d never step on a molecule of snow given yesterday’s
disaster. So as we entered kings canyon, we picked the sneaker friendly
locations. These included the big stump grove, which features the stump
of a dead sequoia tree, which we actually didn’t find, and general grant
tree. general grant tree is the second largest tree in the world, again
in terms of the volume. What’s nice about the general grant tree is that
it was once conferred the title of the national Christmas tree. What was
also nice was that the trail was almost snow free, and that there was a
higher concentration of giant sequoias around general grant than around
general sherman. Nevertheless, at both locations, there were not many of
these trees. Due to its rarity, I tried to capture all of them in the
area on camera.

<img src="./media/image354.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.66667in" />

<img src="./media/image103.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:10.21875in" />

<img src="./media/image421.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:9.95833in" />

<img src="./media/image168.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:10.10417in" />

<img src="./media/image509.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:11.21875in" />

<img src="./media/image706.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:9.39583in" />

<img src="./media/image739.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:9.34375in" />

<img src="./media/image346.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image696.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:7.95833in" />

(Profiling the sequoia trees around the general grant tree)

Like the closure of the mineral king area in sequoia national park, a
highlight of kings canyon national park — grizzly falls area — was also
closed. That is where kings canyon really is but that’s ok. We ended up
going to Hume lake, which was also spectacular. It is a tranquille and
warm lake at a relatively low elevation. We did a picnic by the lake
admiring snow capped mountains in the distance.

<img src="./media/image518.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image11.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image464.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Kings canyon vista and Hume lake)

That wrapped up the little conquest of the southern Sierra Nevada range,
an overall dry region blessed with a narrow band of giant sequoia
habitat. We cut through the entire central valley to reach home by
sunset, sometimes accompanied by road side palms.

<img src="./media/image230.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Palm trees in Central Valley)

**April 2**

Speaking of palms, I had long been missing the “real” palms. I grew up
in a tropical climate with lots of coconut trees, but haven’t had a
chance to visit back for ages. Since I came to California, I had been a
bit of a palm fanatic, because I could see some actual Mexican and
California fan palms, which resemble coconut palms to some extent. But
not really. The fruits of these palms are very different. Even the
leaves are miles apart. For a while, I did some research into the
northern limit of coconut trees in Northern America in related fb groups
and on forums on tropical gardening. My finding was that there used to
be one coconut palm which miraculously lived for years in Southern
California, and a handful in Palm Springs under watchful human
attention. There were some that are sparsely distributed across the rio
grande valley in southern Texas. However, these are outliers and so are
hard to find, hard to enjoy en masse. After all, coconut palms couldn’t
survive the cold winters even in the southern most coastal regions of
California. The rio grande region winters are warmer on average, but
cold fronts frequently assault the region, bringing the temperature
briefly to lows that coconut palms can barely survive.

Me Jessy Anelli Cris Inna and Bo going to Miami for Bo’s birthday
presented a great opportunity to see the “real” palms. Of course, the
parties are the focal points of the trip. In fact, Bo’s sole mission was
to celebrate a birthday with a happy amount of alcohol. For me, I was
happy to help Bo achieve this hedonistic goal, but my additional mission
was to observe coconut trees again after a long hiatus.

Me Inna and Cris arrived at Fort Lauderdale in the early evening. Bo
Anelli and Jessy had arrived earlier that day. As the latter group had
decided to spare the opportunity to pick us up from the airport after
drowning themselves in some magic liquid in the pre-parties, we Ubered
to Miami to unite with the group. The temperature and humidity of the
air was familiar. It was that tropical monsoon vibe and it felt like
meeting the childhood best friend.

<img src="./media/image122.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Our Airbnb)

That night we went back to Fort Lauderdale, for the club district near
downtown. The clubs were crazy — they open until 4am. The music was
crazy and the place was about to explode. I didn’t remember much what
went down. Jessy showed me some nice dance moves. Conquering salsa
definitely became a thing on my bucket list. I took the club hopping
intervals to get footages of coconut trees up close. Here they are.

<img src="./media/image751.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image131.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image357.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Coconut trees at Fort Lauderdale riverfront)

The coconut trees have a slimmer trunk than the ones I saw in Hainan.
Probably they are a different kind. Or perhaps Fort Lauderdale is too
northernly for the trees to develop a thick trunk. Also, the leaves are
not as spread out. Later in the trip in Miami, I did find specimens that
are exactly like those I saw in hainan.

**April 3**

The night didn’t end until early morning. Given that I didn’t drink much
as the driver, I had a relatively early start of the day and explored
the downtown coconut grove area by the waterfront myself. Again the
environments were quite similar to Hainan, despite the fact that it’s a
little north of the Tropic of Cancer.

<img src="./media/image705.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image373.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image7.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Coconut grove waterfront)

In the afternoon we went to the south beach. The place was full of
legends, from the infamous YouTube pranksters to what went down in 2010
with the NBA super stars. The reality was just as cool as the legends.
The water was warm with the Gulf Stream and the tropical atmosphere was
in full swing. Apparently in mainland US aside from the Florida keys,
south beach is the only place that has never seen a sub 0 Celsius
temperature. Looking beyond Miami, Gulf Stream probably contributed a
lot to west Europe having led the way in human civilization in recent
centuries: it gives a mild climate to Europe despite its high latitude,
while the continuation of it — the canary current — provides a
convenient pathway for the voyagers to discover the new world across the
Atlantic.

<img src="./media/image575.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:5.1875in" />

<img src="./media/image88.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image187.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(south beach)

We made it to the Wynwood Walls at the last minute. The wall features
murals, graffitis and paintings by some of the accomplished artists.
Some of the arts had a strong political undertone. I was able to
recognize some of the political movements these murals portrayed.
Nevertheless, the colorfulness and energy at Wynwood Walls were amazing.

<img src="./media/image162.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image577.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image423.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image286.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image20.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image173.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:3.01042in" />

(Wynwood walls)

We had the second party night in a row in Fort Lauderdale. The
blissfulness of the place never ceased to amaze me. Music, dances,
hangover food stores, drugs, drinks… the energy was off the charts. Even
though I forgot to bring my id with me unfortunately that night, just
being at the location at that time felt incredible.

<img src="./media/image245.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(An interesting sculpture in Fort Lauderdale club district)

**April 4**

We had a 1:30pm appointment for a boat and canoeing trip at Chokoloskee
in the Everglades national park. After 2 days of partying in southeast
Florida, we are going southwest. Between the Miami metropolitan area and
the settlements on the west like Naples and fort Myers is the vast
wetlands of Everglades and big cypress. The place is a huge sponge,
storing fresh water and serving as the breeding ground for tropical
birds. Apparently, where we are going — chokoloskee — is also next to
the largest mangrove ecosystem in the Western Hemisphere (ten thousand
islands). I’ve been to the Dongzhaigang mangrove forest, which has a
similar climate. But the mangrove forest here is way larger.

<img src="./media/image312.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image180.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image219.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(Canoeing through the mangroves)

We had Daniel as our guide and pilot. He used to work as an investment
advisor. Now he’s enjoying life in the tropical paradise away from human
chaos. He told us lots of interesting facts about how native Americans
used to live here until Spanish invasion, the various invading species
on these mangrove islets, the innocuous species migrating around, the
tropical birds, the dolphins joyfully following the turbulent water flow
stirred by our motor boat jumping up and down, and dogecoins. From the
way he talked about nature, economics, political affairs and so on, you
can tell that he is just very content with his life now.

<img src="./media/image207.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image161.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image233.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image719.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image618.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image511.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image270.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image366.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image143.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

(The harbor view)

Unlike the water on the Miami side, the water on the side of Gulf of
Mexico is cold. Daniel said the water, when warmed up in the summer,
will reach 30 degree Celsius. Coral reefs grow on the Miami side but
don’t exist on this side. This again speaks to the power of the Gulf
Stream / Florida current on the other the Florida keys.

On the way back we were all super hungry. Daniel recommended a place to
eat nearby, but surprisingly the line was super long, given that there
were few people we encountered at the harbor. But we couldn’t wait and
there weren’t many choices around, so the hunger issue was resolved at a
subway.

<img src="./media/image398.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(A packed restaurant in Everglades city)

This was supposed to be the last day in Miami, but the birthday was not
officially celebrated yet. So in the evening we surprised Bo with a
cake. We then chased it with parties in Wynwood and again Fort
Lauderdale. In the middle of the night, Anelli’s friends came by and we
had Japanese food. I don’t remember what happened as I was super sleepy
and tired. And was trying to have a power nap to get ready to catch the
morning flight and get back to work the next day.

**April 5**

The coconut trip had an unexpected ending. We missed the flight in the
morning due to lack of sleep. After all, there was essentially one hour
of sleep permitted in the situation but none of us managed to get up.
Seeing as the tropical paradise doesn’t want us to leave that soon, we
enjoyed a chill day in Miami. I explored the coconut grove waterfront in
more depth, and managed to find coconut trees with a large trunk, spread
out leaves, and copious of fruits. They look exactly like those by the
beaches in Hainan. Inna managed to get a covid test, the result of which
came out in an hour. We were relieved for the negative result, because
Miami didn’t seem like it was aware of the existence of the pandemic,
the way people go out to parties, beaches and restaurants. We all had a
super delicious Cuban meal. Cuban sandwiches were a must. The Mamey
juice was sweet and refreshing. Before nightfall, we hopped on the plane
flying back to the real life on the west coast. Anelli stayed in Miami
for another day with her friends.

This trip made me fall in love with southern Florida, so, definitely
there will another trip to Miami, and another trip to ten thousand
islands for uninhabited island camping and stargazing, in the future.

(Miscellaneous)

So, giant sequoias or coconut trees?

# **LA again in the Jacaranda season**

Every year in the Jacaranda season I visit LA. There has been
serendipitously no exception to that. The reason for the trip to LA
during the Jacaranda season this year is because me and my cousin are
visiting her best friend who just relocated there.

Having visited LA a couple of times, I didn’t have a bucket list of
places to visit. Also it was the middle of the week. So I spent most of
the time staying in the hotel working. The hotel was Courtyard by
Marriott in Monterey Park. The WiFi was okay, but the productivity was
low. May Gray was in full swing so everyday it was gloomy, which more or
less hurt the productivity.

The fact that the hotel was in Monterey Park, bordering Rosemead means
that we were close to tons of Chinese restaurants. It turned out a lot
of these are cantonese. I don’t speak the language / dialect, and was
treated clearly differently from the other people who spoke it. For one
thing, I would have been able to make quick adjustments based on the
unavailability of certain dishes, but since the owner didn’t speak good
Mandarin or English, she didn’t care to explain or present options. The
food quality was nice. And the area is full of seemingly high quality
small restaurants. The Chinese restaurant competition scene seemed to be
much more tense than in the Bay Area.

The first day was good. Three of us visited universal studio. We did a
couple of roller coasters there of different themes. And the Harry
Potter theme park felt nostalgic for an HP fan. The movie set tour was
also quite informative. Lots of the things that look realistic in
especially those well-known action movies turned out to be staged and
heavily fabricated. A couple of movies I like, I was surprised to find
that they are produced by universal studio, including A beautiful mind,
Schindler's list and les misérables. The only complaint was the wait
time for each event. It ranged from 1 hour to 2 hours.

Another highlight was meeting with middle school friends. This time I
managed to meet with all three of them at the same time. We had Korean
BBQ
[<u>again</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Memorial-weekend-trip-to-LA). It
was so nice to see friends with whom the first meeting was almost 2
decades ago, on the other side of the planet. We also had our dog Bambi
have a sleepover at a friend’s house, because we couldn’t take her on
the universal studio trip.

The drive from bay area to LA and back were surprisingly not tiring this
time around. Me and my cousin talked about stuff the entire road trip.
She got a job offer in Beijing and took it. And LA was her last long
road trip before leaving. It felt as if she just landed in Cali but in
fact it had been a year.

One thing I found interesting was that my cousin’s friend’s boyfriend
Ted met us and told us a lot about the demographics of Orange County.
Apparently there are lots of mistresses of Chinese officials living
there. Ted runs a musical instrument business in OC and he had a lot of
these people visit his shop. According to Ted, these people had a sense
of prestige, and did all they can to display that, including hiring the
best tutors, buying the best instruments, insisting on speaking English,
and driving the best cars. One other thing is that thanks partially to
the money of these exiled politicians, the city of Irving is well
planned and new.

Finally, on Jacaranda trees. It surprised me that after returning to Bay
Area, I paid closer attention than before and also saw jacaranda trees,
just not at the same density. Also, one thing I carefully examined and
noticed was that the average height of Mexican fan palms in LA is
greater than in San Jose, which is greater than SF. It turns out the
[<u>hardiness</u>](https://planthardiness.ars.usda.gov/) of SF is the
same as coastal SoCal at 10b, whereas peninsula and east bay is 9b to
10a, and San Jose is largely 9b. So the lowest temperature is not the
only factor that determines the individual and collective attributes of
palm trees, and jacaranda trees.

The next trip will be going to Iowa to visit the family of a friend /
colleague, to the heartland of America. Highly look forward to it! See
you in August.

# **Trip to America’s heartland — Iowa**

I believe one of the happiest things in one’s life is that your friend
invites you to their hometown, because you get to see the people, the
culture, the history, the economy, the politics and the landscape up
close, with an “insider”. This to me is much better than just reading
online articles and watching YouTube. You will have a good time and you
end up with a better understanding of yourself and the world, which is
always nice.

One fond memory of such is that back in high school, during a school
break, a friend of mine invited me to visit his hometown Wenchang, which
is where the dish Hainanese Chicken originated. I had lots of fun riding
a motorbike in the countryside at night with my friend. We ended up
biking to a beach, where we caught crabs, brought them back and made
congee. Freshest crabs ever. Later in high school my friend and I had
fun competing together in a national physics Olympiad contest, and to
this day our friendship is still going strong, many years after.

Andy and I met when I first joined the company. I was doing a task with
the team he was on. Andy was sitting next to me during those few days,
during which we talked a bit. Later I joined the team, and we got to
befriend each other. We have worked on things together at work and would
often run in the neighborhood on weekends.

Andy’s hometown is in Iowa. For me, Iowa had been about this friend Matt
whom I got to know on a research internship a few years ago. He was also
interested in machine learning theory and ended up doing a PhD at MIT.
Since Matt is from Iowa, I always associated Iowa with someone doing
cool math.

Which is still accurate, as Andy does cool math as well — He is a
physicist, who also enjoys spending time solving math and physics
puzzles outside of work. But obviously Iowa is more than that. About
that, as Andy invited me to visit his family in Iowa, I had an
opportunity to learn more, which I had been super excited about.

One thing about traveling at covid times is that it’s really
discouraged. I’ve flown a couple times since covid broke out, to
[<u>Alaska</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-Alaska-the-formidable-north),
[<u>Florida</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Giant-sequoias-or-coconut-palms),
[<u>Nevada</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-US-southwest-the-grand-staircase)
and
[<u>Colorado</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/Trip-to-Boulder-and-Denver)
for example. For this, I have been seen as a bit careless among people.
But for the Iowa trip, I really didn’t want to mess up. It would really
be a bad idea for Andy’s and his wife Carole’s parents to have someone
show up at their houses infected. It turned out that when the vaccine
came out, my reason to take it was to go to Iowa.

(August 8th)

My flight left San Jose on Sunday morning. The dawn glow unveiled a
thick marine layer over the peninsula, coexisting with a layer of early
season wildfire smoke.

<img src="./media/image154.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

When the airplane arrived in Denver, the city was going through THE
worst air quality in the world that day, due to the same wildfire and
the westerly wind. The connecting flight got delayed by an hour because
of the smoke, which was rare.

<img src="./media/image694.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

I arrived in Des Moines in the afternoon. Before coming, I watched a
bunch of videos about the city, most of which mention the state capitol,
which looks like a splendid landmark. When I actually saw it from the
sky for the first time, I got so pumped. It was indeed a remarkable
piece of art.

<img src="./media/image296.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

So, upon landing, I got a car and the first destination was the state
capitol. It turned out that it was not open to the public on Sunday.
Nonetheless, I took a walk around the beautiful building, and would
return in a few days to see the inside when it opened.

<img src="./media/image456.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image9.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Aside from the capitol building, Andy gave me the names of a few places
beforehand, which coincide with what the lady at the information desk at
the airport told me about. So I knew where to go next.

I arrived to the modern art museum before its 4pm closure. It was a
quiet day in the museum, not sure if it was because it was late. So the
staff didn’t have much to do, but to tell me about the arts. There was
no admission fee and the staff seemed to be all volunteers. This and the
fact that they were all super friendly and talkative to me told me that
they really love the arts.

<img src="./media/image747.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image112.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Next I went to pappajohn sculpture park in downtown. The park was nice.
It features several happy looking statues. Each sculpture has a phone
number which directs you to a voice tour if you call. The sculpture park
was a contrast to the Fremont Troll in Seattle, which has a weird and
creepy face. Sorry Seattle. The locals greeted me when I was just
walking by on the park trail, which felt really nice.

<img src="./media/image605.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image523.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Next I decided to find a German restaurant, because of the significant
German heritage of Des Moines and Iowa in general. I ended up having a
huge meal at hessian haus which is a Bavarian beer garden near downtown.
The schnitzel and bratwurst were superb and cheap. It was one of the
best schnitzels I’d ever had, up there with its counterpart in Oren’s
hummus. A friend who used to go to school in Munich told me that the
restaurant looked exactly like those in Munich.

<img src="./media/image504.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Unable to finish the food, I needed a walk so I drove to the waterfront
of Des Moines River. There is a Chinese style pavilion called Robert D.
Ray Asian Gardens. It turns out it was built to commemorate the
humanitarian assistance to the boat people fleeing the atrocities during
the Vietnam war. By the pavilion there is a walkable bridge over the
river. There were a few people shooting a movie on the bridge. And it
made you feel like you were in Prague or Budapest.

<img src="./media/image536.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image192.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After this, I was supposed to meet up with Carole and Andy at Carole’s
parents’ house in Madrid. They arrived 2 days before via a long road
trip, for Carole’s parents’ 40 year anniversary. But being curious, I
made a quick visit to a Casey’s, which is this chain of gas stations /
grocery stores headquartered in Iowa. The breakfast pizza there was said
to be great. Since I didn’t have room for any food, I just asked the guy
working there a bunch of questions. They made these bacon sausage cheese
and scrambled egg pizzas everyday, and also donut holes and cookies. The
store was well run, clean and organized; the donuts that didn’t get sold
that day were being thrown away. The guy was pretty chill; he gave me a
tour of the kitchen. I was thinking they should really expand their
businesses to California.

<img src="./media/image159.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Finally I made it to Madrid, where Carole’s parents, Steve and Tanya
live. In their comfy and spacious living room, they shared stories about
their family history, from their ancestors who were German and Swedish
immigrants during the mid 19th century, to some of their distant
relatives living in Sweden whom they still are in touch with, to how
Madrid was founded by lost Swedish immigrants. They both are going to a
Lutheran church and have had the anniversary party there earlier that
day.

<img src="./media/image115.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Andy later gave me a walking tour of Madrid, which is today more or less
of a commuter town to Des Moines. Houses are seriously huge, and life in
town feels relaxed and peaceful. One of the only changes to the town
over the last few years was the conversion from an abandoned railroad to
a bike trail. I found a lot of inner peace too, and had a really good
sleep compensating for the intermittent naps on the airplane the day
prior, in a bedroom that Tanya and Steve neatly prepared.

(August 9th)

Next morning, I woke up to a beautiful porch and delicious muffins Tanya
just baked.

<img src="./media/image109.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:10.03125in" />

After breakfast, we were off on the road, on interstate 80, via
Davenport to Clinton.

We stopped at a grocery store called kum & go by the highway near
Williamsburg, got pizza. One might assume le pizze made by these
unassuming places are mediocre. In reality they were juicy and
fulfilling, really good quality. In fact, these shops along the highways
in Iowa are all very well built - clean and spacious. Importantly, they
offer a wide range of stuff from food to houseware to farm farm
equipments to freshly cooked goods. Truck drivers and farmers alike
would get things they need here.

<img src="./media/image470.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Driving through the plains of corn fields and pig farms, we arrived in
Clinton, by the Mississippi River. This is where Andy grew up and where
Andy’s parents still live. We detoured a bit to pass by a house where
Andy grew up in another part of town, before meeting with Andy’s
parents, Ruth and Jim, in a newer neighborhood. They were extremely
friendly. Jim was showing me around the house - one with a backyard
that’s almost as big as a soccer field! And Ruth made us sandwiches.
Engraved words that praise family, friends and God can be seen
everywhere in the house - fridge magnets, banners, words on toy roosters
(Ruth loves roosters). What a loving family.

A friend who’s an expert in card games asked me before the trip if I
could ask Andy’s parents whether they knew a game called Euchre. The
reason is that it is a game that originated in Germany, and when Germans
immigrated to midwest in the 19th century they brought the game with
them. Andy’s parents are of German ancestry. Upon meeting them, I
extended the question. Interestingly, the answer was a yes. In fact,
they said they still have Euchre tournaments in the community, carrying
on the ancestor tradition from Europe to the US.

After lunch we left for Dubuque for American Lady Cruises. Dubuque is a
city north of Clinton on the upstream of Mississippi. Said cruise is a
boat tour on Mississippi River, from Dubuque Yacht Basin to O’Leary Lake
at the border of Iowa and Wisconsin, and back. Different from the corn
plains that is Des Moines - Clinton, from Dubuque on upstream until just
before the twin cities in Minnesota, the ecoregion is called Driftless
area. The region features drainage patterns due to lack of glaciation
back in the days, very different from the adjacent glaciated regions.

It was a hot humid summer afternoon. The passengers were either hanging
out on the deck, or enjoying a cold beverage inside. They also served
meat balls and alcohol on the ship. It was such a nice trip. Family and
friends were chatting, enjoying their time, and taking pictures as it
cruised along. There were lush forested hills on both sides, a broad
river body, and even an American eagle in the distance.

<img src="./media/image201.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image499.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image714.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After the cruise trip, we toured downtown Dubuque. What a historic town.
Situated in the tristate area by the river, it’s been a manufacturing
and religious hub for a century. We took the historic fenelon place
elevator, one of the steepest railroad in the world, originally built in
the 19th century for a senator to have a shortcut to work, and there we
saw the splendid skyline of the city.

<img src="./media/image548.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image672.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image711.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

And we took the train back down the hill. We had dinner at a restaurant
in the city center, surrounded by red brick buildings. The portion was
generous. The service was classy. Life in this town felt like clock was
turned back.

(August 10th)

In the morning we came to Ruth’s and Jim’s church for a short tour.
During the pandemic the church services have been conducted over zoom.
Nonetheless the church was open. Even without people, the interior felt
like a really cozy community center. Modestly built, the church features
family pictures and event photos on the walls of its first and second
floors. There’s also a kitchen area where families would cook and eat
together.

<img src="./media/image683.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image183.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image148.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Then Jim drove us crossed Mississippi River. Fulton, Illinois is a town
right on the other side of the river. The town has a strong Dutch
heritage. On the river dike we visited De Immigrant, which is a huge
windmill, fabricated in the Netherlands and assembled in the town
decades ago.

<img src="./media/image344.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image282.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We had lunch at a bakery that is a favorite local eatery of Jim’s and
Ruth’s, called Krumpets. It was a homey restaurant with a big menu.

<img src="./media/image740.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

After lunch we headed to Lock and Dam 13 on the Mississippi River, which
offers a great vista of the much broader river body upstream from the
dam. There the river is so broad that made me wonder what Tom Sawyer /
Mark Twain was working with down in Missouri. Across from the vista is
Eagle Point Park on the Iowa side, where Andy and Carole wedding
ceremony took place, which we also visited later.

<img src="./media/image477.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image177.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image640.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

In the afternoon, we headed down to quad city / Davenport to watch a
baseball game. Watching baseball games with friends is a big thing for
the locals. It was then that a big summer storm was rolling in. The sky
gradually darkened and a downpour seemed to be imminent. Fortunately the
center of the storm missed the town when the game was on. During the
game, Carole and Andy tried teaching me baseball rules. But I was
hopeless :) Nonetheless a sport enthusiastic, I still enjoyed the
competitiveness of the game and the nice family and friends get-together
ambiance at Modern Woodmen Park.

<img src="./media/image155.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image554.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image288.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(August 11th)

We left Clinton for Andy’s brother Tom’s and sister-in-law Sarah’s house
in Ankeny, near Des Moines, the next morning. Ruth made ice cream, pie
and coffee to send us off. The next time Andy would meet his parents
would be Christmas. Ruth shed tears :(

<img src="./media/image3.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image126.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We arrived in Ankeny in the afternoon. Tom and Sarah warmly welcomed us
in. Their house is similarly full of pride, like Tom’s parents’ house.
Funny slogans as well as pictures of the family are everywhere. Tom
started sharing stories as a professional journalist and a brother and
an Iowan with passion, from interviewing president candidates on the
campaign trail in Iowa, to chasing a destructive tornado, to Iowan home
cooking, to Weird West movies, to the local housing market. It was funny
to be part of this sibling reunion.

<img src="./media/image385.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image181.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image729.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image134.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image226.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image384.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image695.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

After a big dinner, as a celebration of Carole’s birthday, we went for a
walk in the nearby Sawgrass Park. It was tranquil, spacious and green
everywhere. The sunset on a summer night in Iowa was really beautiful.

<img src="./media/image675.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(August 12th)

Next morning, we checked out a nice bagel place called 5 borough bagels,
and devoured them by Grays Lake. Grays lake is in an urban park between
downtown Des Moines and the airport. Carole used to jog around the lake.
At brunch, Tom and Andy were debating on something about Holy Roman
Empire by a tree. This park is a very relaxing place with big trees on
the lake bank.

<img src="./media/image87.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After the bagel brunch we said goodbye to each other for now. Andy,
Carole and Tom went for a hike with family friends, and then Andy and
Carole would stay in Madrid with Carole’s parents for another night and
drive back to California (It turned out that Carole’s dad was tested
positive for covid that day, but fortunately he would recover in a few
days). I in turn had one more day in Des Moines, before flying back to
California.

I decided to take advantage of a ticket and coupons that Tom gave me,
and head to Iowa State Fairgrounds - the annual Iowa state fair was
taking place there.

State fairs were something new to me. We have something like state fairs
in China as well, where they’d put out things the province produces and
is famous for, not too different from a big farmers’ market. I didn’t
know much about the ones in this country. Andy had actually hyped it up
long before the Iowa trip, so I couldn’t wait to check it out.

Food stands, amusement park, beer garden, concert, business booths,
aquarium, and animal farms — it turned out that these were the things
that characterized the Iowa State Fair. So not just a farmers’ market. I
had a lot of fun in the giant fairgrounds. As for food, of note is the
Dutch letters, one of Iowa’s iconic bakeries.

<img src="./media/image604.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image304.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image387.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image178.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image399.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image171.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image590.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image480.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image715.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image30.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image341.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image658.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image369.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image566.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image413.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image150.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

It was the very first day of the fair, and it was truly a big party. On
display was predominantly agriculture stuff, and you can see that it was
surely something Iowa is very proud of. Actually not just on the
fairgrounds, but also throughout the trip so far, I had been seeing
hardworking Iowans producing corns and pork and many more, feeding the
country from the heartland of America.

After traversing the big state fair on foot, it was already late.
Nevertheless, I headed to the university area of Des Moines, because I
couldn’t leave Iowa without exploring its university campuses. It’s one
of the interests. On university campuses, you get to learn stuff,
understand the local dynamics, and feel young.

So, I stopped by Drake University, northwest of downtown Des Moines. The
campus is not huge. It took me 40min to see pretty much every building
on campus. It was quiet at night, and supposedly during the entire
pandemic. The buildings were classic and beautiful. There was a mini
prairie in the center of the university that was pretty interesting. It
was set up there to showcase Iowa’s major ecoregion. As for academics,
apparently produced here were quite a few Iowan politicians and lawyers…

<img src="./media/image680.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

…and that would be a nice segue to the Iowa state capitol tour which I’d
embark on tomorrow.

(August 13th)

Finally it was the last day of the trip. I arrived at the capitol
building in the morning, completing the unfinished tour on the very
first day.

It’s a splendid piece of art — the capitol building itself and the
location they chose to put it. I had visited Lincoln memorial in
Washington DC with my mom several years ago. That was a humbling
experience, eventually contributing to my moving to the US primarily
because I loved the messages the words on those walls conveyed. And this
Iowa state capitol is just the same. Especially the rotunda on the
second floor, reminded me of the U.S. capitol rotunda too. We’ll get to
the details of this rotunda.

There are four floors in the capitol building. The tourist entrance is
on the ground floor. There is a gift shop, and the legislative services
agency on this floor.

<img src="./media/image405.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image526.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

One floor up is the first floor. On the first floor, the Iowa
constitution is on display in the office of the Secretary of State. A
model of the battleship u.s. Iowa is also on display. A committee room
for the House of Representatives, previously the Supreme Court chamber
can be visited on this floor.

<img src="./media/image670.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image132.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image529.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image602.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image127.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image699.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image273.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image634.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

From the first floor, one can get to the second floor via the marbled
stairs in the center of the building. The second floor features the
chambers of the Iowa House of Representatives and the Iowa senate. It is
here where the states 50 senators and 100 representatives meet to create
Iowa laws.

Another spectacle on this floor is the state library of Iowa law
library. It occupies the entire west wing of the second floor.

Additionally, some of the most striking artwork in the capitol is the
rotunda on the second floor. At the base of the dome are 8 half moon
shaped paintings and 12 statues. The paintings tell the story of the
progress of civilization: hunting, herding, agriculture, the forge,
commerce, education, science and art. The statues represent history,
science, law, fame, literature, industry, peace, commerce, agriculture,
victory, truth and progress. The emblem is the grand army of the
republic (g.a.r) is suspended across the dome, symbolizing Iowa’s
efforts to preserve the Union during the civil war.

<img src="./media/image321.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image388.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image503.jpg"

Ascending to the third floor via the narrow wooden stairs on the flanks,
one would see 6 mosaics in arched panels depicting defense, charity,
executive, legislative, judiciary and education.

<img src="./media/image169.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image584.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image454.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Very powerful.

Outside the capitol there are more cool stuff, including an area
commemorating lost American soldiers in the Korean War, more statues,
and an obelisk dedicated to the pioneers (I think). From the capitol, a
nice skyline of the downtown could be seen. As I was short on time,
regretfully I didn’t get to explore a whole lot of these spectacles just
on the outside of the capitol.

<img src="./media/image172.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image331.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image214.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image693.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image301.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

And that was it.

This Iowa trip is going to be remembered in years to come. I’ll remember
the loving families, the heartland pride, the powerful capitol, the
tranquil sceneries, and the simple life with large portions of food and
large yards and porches. To capitalize on these memories, later Andy
conferred the title of an honorary Iowan on me at my earnest request.
With this title, I’m now part of Iowa, and can rightfully bring Iowan
tranquility and modesty to my life.

Had a layover in Houston, and at night, decided to take Caltrain back
from San Jose airport. While it was my first time commuting from SJ
airport to home, thankfully the train took me home reliably and
economically.

Back in the hustle and bustle of Bay Area, the Iowan in me shall stay
the course from now on.

# **Third time’s a charm — summiting mt whitney**

Did it finally at the third attempt.

The first was early in the season, with loads of snow starting from a
really low elevation — 2800m as I remember. We followed the main trail
and made it to a spot before the last lake and gave up because cris got
sick. The plan was to camp at the base camp but we were not able to make
it there.

The second time was in mid summer in July and the snow condition was
perfect because there was none. A thunderstorm was probable when we were
going up but didn’t end up happening. The plan was to do it as a day
hike. We made it to the crest right after 99 switchbacks but altitude
sickness struck again. Cris was reacting more to it but recovered
significantly after taking an aspirin. There was a chance to continue
on, but it was like 4pm at the crest, and we didn’t want to hike down in
the dark for too long so decided to head back. It was a shame because we
were really close, after having conquered the treacherous 99
switchbacks.

This led to the third time. The plan was still a day hike because we
knew pretty much everything. Especially personally, I knew how my body
might react at which point and how we would likely do with time.
Unfortunately cris couldn’t move past the spot where we stopped the
first time due to the altitude and lack of sleep (which happened all
three times but also was not easy to avoid because each time we had to
come after a 7 hour drive with the previous day at work), at the pace we
were at. We departed at about 12:30pm, since when cris hiked down and
toured trails near lone pine and I tried to push for the summit. I moved
swiftly to the crest but it turned out I was a bit too fast, as my heart
was racing faster than the second time at the crest and my head was
dizzy. I was reacting badly to the altitude. I took an aspirin but
didn’t feel better. I decided to soldier on because it wasn’t like I was
going to die, and also because I really didn’t want to come back again
knowing the details of pretty much the entire trail. Then on that last
segment of the trail on the back of the ridge, I moved really slowly,
supplied myself with calories, and kept my head down. Eventually I made
it to the top, where there is a hut. The view was gorgeous. There was
another guy at the top when I arrived. He was a pro. he helped me
identify the names of the mountains nearby, including telescope peak in
Death Valley, which was really nice, because i always wondered whether
there are landmarks near the highest and lowest point in contiguous US
that are visible to one another, given that the two locations are really
close. I was the second to last to summit that day, and had to suffer
the hike down alone in complete darkness starting from early on on the
99 switchbacks. Oct 9 was considered late in the season as fresh snow
already covered the switchbacks, making them difficult without spikes.
They were not icy yet so it wasn’t too dangerous, as long as I took
decent caution. At 10:29pm I made it back at the trailhead (with a
headlamp of course). Never have I hiked in the dark alone for that long
but it was kind of fun. I eventually caught up with a few other hikers
descending the mountain, and came across a few others who were summiting
for the sunrise. Some of them I met before on the way up, so the second
encounter was fun and memorable.

so, third time’s a charm. Felt bad about not summiting as a group when
the whole group planned on summiting, but finally making it individually
at the third try was still really nice. Here are some pictures from all
these three times.

<img src="./media/image79.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image563.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image685.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image190.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image209.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:13.57292in" />

<img src="./media/image494.jpg"

The whole post has been a bit of rambling. So to whoever makes it here,
thanks for reading and viewing :)

# **God created the Grand Canyon but He lives in Sedona - back-to-back excursions in Arizona**

Two different trips to Arizona, the first to God’s house, and the second
to God’s masterpiece, both in September 2021.

Without further ado, let’s dive into these 2 trips.

On the morning of September 3rd, I hopped on a plane, without much plan,
with so much expectation, to Phoenix. From there, I was going to embark
on a feast. As soon as I landed, I took Phoenix metro to Tempe, where I
took a turo to navigate the city. It was the first time I used turo.
These resource sharing businesses are really meeting the needs of people
the way no previous businesses did. So, I took the car, drove a little
bit, to the legendary ASU campus. It was a clear day in Phoenix as it
typically is. The hot Sonoran desert sun was grilling the place. But it
was full of excitement on the campus. The locals seemed to know no
boundaries della corona. Why do I say that? Look at the shops and
restaurants packed with people. Look at the coffee shops and bookstores,
where A/Cs were making the biggest noise they could to rhyme with the
sun devils. Now, why is ASU legendary? Just go to YouTube, and you’d
find all the legends in the prank circle, lots of them based in this
area, doing pranks on the ASU campus that garner millions of views. Why
do pranksters congregate in this area? Why not for example university of
Washington, or Harvard? One big reason is the weather. In this corner of
the world, Sun reaches its maximum possible coverage. And humans are
known to have a high spirit when there’s plenty of sunshine. Perhaps
unsurprisingly, the students of ASU have made the university one of the
best party schools in the country. Also, it doesn’t take that many
people to make a place a prank hub. Thatwasepic, bigdawstv, and
angrypicnic, three uniquely talented pranksters that grew up in the
area, have been enough. It was because of their copious, hilarious,
YouTube videos that I had already been familiar with the appearances of
various buildings and streets of ASU. After lunch, at a burger shop on
the main commercial street, recommended by a friend who’s an ASU
alumnus, I entered the campus. What a strangely happy feeling. The
streets, the buildings are the same as I thought. I could picture these
guys shooting pranks by Hayden library, in the gym, by the cafeterias,
on palm walk. In the past I visited Venice beach (topnotch idiots,
vitalyzdtv, hoomantv, Jaykindafunny), Vegas (epicfivetv, limitless), and
Miami Beach (Viktor Drago) in hopes to meet the guys and they didn’t
come out. Today, none of these guys came out either to ASU. But that’s
okay. I went for a walk around the campus, toured the Hayden library,
and spent the afternoon trying to fully absorb the passion exuberant off
of the place.

<img src="./media/image465.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image242.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image698.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image746.jpg"

style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image249.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

After ASU, I drove around Scottsdale and Paradise Valley. The city is so
spread out that going from one community core to another means 20 min of
drive. I didn’t find interesting nooks and crannies, and came back to
the campus, had a late dinner, and slept in the rental car.

Next morning, I came to this Mexican restaurant on the other side of the
town which Mark Wiens visited. The owner explained the items on the menu
enthusiastically just like how she did it in the video. And the food was
great. Between this and Los carnalitos in Redwood City, I can’t say
which serves the best tacos.

<img src="./media/image310.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image455.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image552.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

after breakfast, I met with all the friends coming from Bay Area.
Returned the turo car, had brunch in Phoenix. And we are on our way to
Sedona.

<img src="./media/image85.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image697.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

As we headed north, the dry hot low desert scene gradually turned into a
warm/cold plateau, with more greenery and the disappearing of the tall
cacti. We stopped at montezuma castle national monument to admire the
living space that ancient Native American tribes built on a cliff. The
reason they built it on a wall was to stay away from floods and heat and
to be at an advantageous position against enemy tribes.

<img src="./media/image144.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image744.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

In mid afternoon we arrived in Sedona. People say God live in Sedona. I
can see why. Sedona situated in a basin surrounded by tall green
mountains. But at the heart of the basin are red sandstones. It’s got
its majesty with the jaw dropping color contrasts against the
surrounding forests, while exhibiting great elegance with the delicate
rock formation. YouTuber Harald Baldr visited Sedona a month or so
before, as well as Traveling Robert. They seemed genuinely happy when
they were here. And I was about to find out as we parked at the
trailhead of cathedral rock, one of the most popular hikes in this area.
The parking lot was packed. It took us a while to get a spot but all
this was going to be so worth it…

<img src="./media/image189.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image460.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image492.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image243.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

that’s right. That big rock is cathedral rock. The view was stunning
from the beginning, but got better and better as we hiked up. The trail
itself was easy. As we got close the top, I felt like entering a
fairytale.

<img src="./media/image315.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image69.jpg"

Damn this is a weird emotion where the happy days in my childhood were
all brought back, flashing through my head. Dramatic. To echo that
emotion I risked my old adult life a bit and climbed to a hallow on the
cliff. It was exhilarating.

<img src="./media/image594.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image676.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />
