style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image564.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

By nightfall we tried to soak up the last beam of the reflection of the
dramatic rocks, and finally hiked down. The problem now is where to
spend the night . Earlier that day we got food from grocery store near
the national monument so we were in the mood of camping. Fortunately
there was a public camping site near flagstaff, not far north from
Sedona. We pitched tents there. There wasn’t enough tents so I and Jessy
slept in the car. It was damn cold that night back on the plateau. The
campfire went out in the middle of the night, exacerbating the
situation. Some motor fleets playing loud music going around all night
long, supposedly locals protesting the tourists’ use of the land as a
camping site, didn’t help with the sleep. The sleep was bad for all of
us, but the food was great, and the spirits were high.

<img src="./media/image687.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image284.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image612.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

In the morning we packed up left flagstaff for Tuba city, to meet up
with some Navajo people. So Navajo nation is this nation like entity in
this area between Arizona and New Mexico. It has its own political
system and a president, but doesn’t have an army. Some other Native
American people have their nations too, but Navajo nation is the largest
among all. Me and cris learned a few Navajo phrases on Duolingo
beforehand, fully prepared for communication.

the place we stopped at is called Moenave dinosaur tracks. It’s a field
where archeologists found dinosaur fossils. There are Navajo people
setting up a stand selling handmade rock art at the entrance, \$10 each
regardless of the item. They offered to serve as guides as well free of
charge. But we gave \$20 as a tip. It was scorching hot in the desert.
The fossils of dinosaur eggs and skeletons were visible, embedded in the
rocks. But some explanation and imagination were needed to recognize
them.

<img src="./media/image264.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image463.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Then we left for horseshoe bend near Page. Horseshoe bend was made
famous as a tourist spot in recent years with the advent of social media
like instagram. Previously tourists came to Page to see Glen Canyon
nearby. But Horseshoe bend got popular definitely deservedly. Look at
that elevation drop from the rim down to the riverbed. The size of
humans down in the canyon rafting is similar to that of ants. And unlike
Grand Canyon, look how narrow the canyon is. The carved out wall is
almost perpendicular to the floor. It was a hot and crowded midday. It
took a while to find a good spot for pictures.

<img src="./media/image565.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image414.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image272.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image289.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After Horseshoe Bend, we went for a swim in the lake on the Utah side of
the Colorado River, near Lone Rock Beach. It’s a wide sandy beach
popular with RV campers offering calm water for swimming & views of an
iconic rock. Way to cool down in this desert summer heat.

<img src="./media/image293.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image641.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image661.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image24.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Next stop, Antelope Canyon. There are a few different Antelope Canyons
in the region, all featuring windy narrow pathways under the ground with
red canyon walls on the sides. And they were all discovered by the local
Navajos. They made touring the canyons into a business where visitors
need to do a guided tour to see the canyon. For this trip we were lucky
to get tickets for lower Antelope Canyon beforehand. The view was
exactly the same as shown in those pictures online. But being able to
speak with a Navajo guide was something different. The guide was very
nice. He didn’t only talk about the geology and history of the canyon,
but also about the culture and history of Navajo nation. Something I
didn’t know was still there was still immense bad blood between the
native Americans and the colonists. There was a guy from Miami in the
same group who spoke very arrogantly to the guide. The guide withstood
it and smiled it off well.

<img src="./media/image32.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image66.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image623.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image275.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

We had a big Mexican meal for dinner, and stayed in an AirBnB in Page
for the night. It was warm and comfortable. No more unprepared overnight
camping freeze…

<img src="./media/image531.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Next day our goal was to spend a short time in Sedona again on the way
back to Phoenix. One more chance to see where God’s living. This time we
toured this nice row of boutique and souvenir shops.

<img src="./media/image360.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image422.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image372.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.17708in" />

<img src="./media/image335.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

In Phoenix, Cris and Inna had to take a fight earlier. Anelli Jessy and
I devoured juicy az bbq. Anelli then headed to Tucson to meet up with
her friends while Jessy and i headed to the airport to catch our
flights. Anelli would return to bay the next day.

<img src="./media/image608.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image101.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image37.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

It was really hard to say goodbye to az as the trip was so memorable and
the views were so captivating. And the next opportunity came very very
soon. As soon as I learned that Yidi wanted to conquer the legendary
Grand Canyon rim2rim trail, we booked tickets for az via Vegas, and came
back on sep 24th, 3 weeks after the first az trip.

It was a well, well planned trip, with details down to the minutes. So,
to do rim2rim in one day, everything had to revolve around the shuttle
schedule — the shuttle that goes from the south rim to north rim, which
operates twice (?) a day. If we miss that afternoon shuttle at the south
rim, we wouldn’t be able to make it to the lodge at the north rim, hence
starting early next morning wouldn’t be possible. Fortunately, with our
excellent planning and execution, we made it to Vegas on time, got the
rental car immediately after, raced through the empty space between
Vegas and South Rim, and there was still about an hour left until the
shuttle took off. The podcast on the road about solving crimes of
homicides and frauds wasn’t too bad. The shuttle arrived at the north
rim right before the nightfall.

<img src="./media/image580.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image313.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image597.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We had to start early the next day because it was a 27 mile trip with a
crazy elevation difference between the bottom and the rim for a day
hike. So we had a quick dinner at the north rim lodge and wrapped up the
day.

the next day, on sep 25th, we started at 5am. The trailhead is 2 miles
from the lodge, so we started walking. A good hearted local found us
walking and offered a ride to the trailhead. We were off to a smooth
start.

Dawn broke the darkness soon after, as we were descending from the north
rim. The descend was gradual, but long… much longer than the ascend we
were about to tackle later the day. But the view was so good. Once you
are in the canyon, you don’t realize it’s a giant canyon anymore. It’s a
maze.

<img src="./media/image579.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image237.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image329.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image570.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image350.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image582.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

after crossing a certain elevation line, the temperature suddenly went
from freezing to hot. It was the magic of microclimates in the
mountains. Soon the vegetation looked like those in Sedona (remember the
basin that Sedona is in).

The trek was out it a pause when we arrived at phantom ranch, the bottom
of the canyon, by the rapid Colorado river streams. It was scorching
hot. There was a small shop. Everyone was sitting there getting snacks
to get energy back.

<img src="./media/image573.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image281.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image540.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image720.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image418.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image448.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image234.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image416.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

So we crossed the river and soon found ourselves ascending on a slope.
From here on it was getting harder and harder. The sun light was not
obstructed, and the steepness was higher and higher. Fortunately I was
training for sf marathon and Yidi was no slouch, following a fitness
routine rigorously everyday. We soon found ourselves taking over
everyone. Except for this one guy who seemed fit and wouldn’t give in to
our advance.

finally everyone hit a wall by Indian garden. It’s a water supply spot.
It’s the beginning of a serious sequence of zigzags that would end at
the south rim. If anything, it was harald baldr’s funny video about
burger hunting exactly on this route shot about a month ago that kept me
going. I kept looking for where exactly he did those shots. And I think
I found it as I trudged on.

<img src="./media/image436.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image707.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

In the last section of the trail, we were too tired to even take any
picture. But we managed to arrive at the south rim at about 5pm. It was
a heroic moment. The 12 hour conquest of such treacherous lands finally
concluded. We drove back to Page for the night, with the last bit of
strength left in the knees and feet.

The next morning, the energy came back. We toured horseshoe bend. For me
it was the second time in the month. But we went super early and it was
easy to find a picture spot without a crowd, which allowed me to
appreciate this stunning place more, than the first time.

<img src="./media/image35.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Then we headed to antelope canyon x, which derived its name from the x
shape of the sky when you look up from the bottom of the canyon. This is
a different antelope canyon from the last one I went. But the setup
looked very similar, except that it’s a bit shorter. The guide again was
a member of the Navajo nation. She always patiently waited for everyone
to take pictures before the group headed to the next viewpoint.

<img src="./media/image235.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image184.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

At noon we came back to page and I had a Navajo taco for the first time.
This was at a gas station near Page. It was crunchy oily and fresh, was
a nice bite and a cool experience.

<img src="./media/image43.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image543.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

afterwards we headed back to Vegas for the flight next morning. It was
an eventful drive. I was stopped by a cop when we were driving through
Utah, because I sped. The cop couldn’t make his printer work after 30
minutes of tinkering so he ended up sparing us a ticket. We arrived in
Vegas in the afternoon, and checked out this mob museum which I went two
years before. But it was cool to see the monsters names and careers anew
after I learned a bit of Italian this year. We ate at a famous clam
chowder place, a minute or so before the business closed. How fortunate!
At night we checked out downtown Vegas where all the old school and less
famous casinos congregate.

<img src="./media/image445.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image619.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Arizona, Arizona. After these 2 trips, I have to say I’m sold — this is
a God’s blessed place. Grand Canyon is his master piece, and Sedona is
where he lives. I’m also convinced that he has his gardens and artworks
in Phoenix, Page, Flagstaff and Navajo Nation too. Next time if I come
to az, I hope I could explore the Sonoran desert more, places like
Tucson, Yuma and lake havasu city, are on my bucket list.

# **Trips to LA at the beginning and end of the winter**

This past winter has been particularly sad for me. My beloved grandma
was already very sick when Winter started in Hengyang, and despite the
relative warmth in the beginning phase of the winter, my grandma didn’t
make it through. A few days after my grandma passed away, Hengyang’s
temperature dropped dramatically. The winter went on and saw an
unprecedented count of 4 snow events.

These events hit me profoundly. Since I have been on the other side of
the ocean, far, far away from my grandma, whenever I saw a streak of
cold days in Hengyang on my weather app, my mood would sink, thinking
that it would be another hard day for my ailing grandma, and that I
couldn’t be there, taking care of her. This pattern didn’t change at all
since she passed away - whenever I saw Hengyang’s temperature dropped to
near 0, the first reaction was distress and guilt.

And then I realized she was not in this world any more.

LA is a warm place, even in the DEAD of winter. LA happened to have
become my refuge for this winter. Winter 2022.

On Nov 12, 2021, we drove to long beach. It was Cris’ 30th birthday, and
we (Cris, Inna, me, Anelli, Jessy, Bo) decided to celebrate it in the LA
area. The Bay Area started to display a cool and grim weather pattern at
the time. But long beach was a sunny furnace still. We rented a big
airbnb, went to bars and Disneyland, and went beach hopping. We stayed
in Long Beach, but also went to Hermosa Beach. The beaches were all
sandy, flat and sunny. Tall palm trees lined up on the beaches. Packed
restaurants abounded by the promenade. Kids and dogs were running and
screaming. It was incredibly not hard to forget things for a while here.

Fast forward to March 13, 2022. Aside from the grief, I also got burned
out at work. Cris wanted to do Whitney again, and he needed to get his
passport renewed in the consulate in LA. So we went on another trip to
LA. Turned out Whitney was no go, because the trailhead was pushed back
by 1.5 hours during the sierra winter season, and the snow depth was
prohibitive. So we went straight through High Desert, via Mojave and
Lancaster, and arrived in LA. Again, when it happened to be drizzling in
the Bay Area, LA was warm, sunny and soothing. We rented an Airbnb in
Glendale, tried a couple of Mexican and Armenian places, and saw an NBA
game, where the Lakers lost to the Raptors in a [<u>dramatic
fashion</u>](https://www.reddit.com/r/lakers/comments/teftj9/the_los_angeles_lakers_score_12_points_in_the_1st/).
We hiked to the nearby Echo Park, where we saw St. Andrew's Ukrainian
Orthodox Church. The church was closed, so we couldn’t get closer. But
the golden domes of the church quietly stood on the hill top as if it
was firmly resisting the [<u>Russian
invasion</u>](https://www.latimes.com/california/story/2022-02-27/ukrainian-americans-pray-for-loved-ones-sunday-service-in-echo-park),
far, far away, in its native Ukraine.

“Far across the distance\
And spaces between us\
You have come to show you go on”

“Near, far, wherever you are\
I believe that the heart does go on”

# **Adventure in Texas**

Went to South Texas to find coconut trees, accidentally crossed the
border, got deported. Here’s what went down on this crazy journey.

**April 9**

Landed in Austin, Texas, a city that embodies the beginning of my
American dream. In winter 2012 Austin was the first place I visited on
my first trip to the US. Generous portions of food, open and welcoming
people, great academics, flowers and bushes and prairies, and freedom.
Since that trip I left my heart in Texas. And since always wanted to
come back. I did come back on a campus visit trip in spring 2013, liked
it a lot, but volatility of life struck and I ended up going to Seattle
for grad school.

My first impression of the city was that it had grown a lot since 2013.
Downtown area features a lot of high rises that I don’t remember seeing.

We didn’t have luck with rental cars at the airport, so we headed north
to the highland area for rental cars. After Enterprise rejected us due
to what they thought were invalid documents, we found our luck with Avis
eventually. The trip officially began with a nice Mexican breakfast.

<img src="./media/image370.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We headed south directly after the breakfast, leaving Austin for later.
By early afternoon, we arrived in San Marcos, where Texas state
university is situated. I came back to see the university which my mom
and I were visiting in 2012 for a few months. I couldn’t remember where
my mom’s office used to be, but the campus still looked familiar, and as
inspirational as then. This place was simply what made me quit my
studies in Tsinghua and come here.

<img src="./media/image31.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image614.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image29.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

I had another mission on this trip, which was to find the northern most
coconut trees in Texas, which we’d find out about later on on this trip.
But I had serendipitous findings about the northern limits of
Washingtonia palms. Look at that palm in the photo above. The habitat
border lies exactly along the Austin-San Marcos line.

After San Marcos, we arrived in San Antonio. A historical town in
American history, the touristy area of it was a bit of a letdown. The
river walk area reminded me of foster city, and the Alamo was not very
majestic or mesmerizing. The Spurs are of course a legendary team,
something I’d definitely like to see when in San Antonio. But there
wasn’t a game that day.

<img src="./media/image690.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image54.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image652.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image285.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(Notice that the palm trees start to thrive in San Antonio, a city that
is a bit farther south and warmer than Austin.)

Then we decided to head to Corpus Christi. Through miles upon miles of
one giant continuous prairie, we finally reached the Atlantic Ocean.

<img src="./media/image84.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

(USS Lexington)

<img src="./media/image530.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image287.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image125.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image507.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The water was considerably warmer than the pacific. In the distance, one
can see the oil rigs in the Gulf of Mexico. The local gas price was
significantly lower than Bay Area, within the \$3-\$4 range.

<img src="./media/image294.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

On the inland side of the barrier island, there was a wind surfing area,
which also featured an interesting road sign with multiple world
destinations.

<img src="./media/image133.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image338.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image337.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

We headed back from the barrier island that was north Padre Island, to
meet Svet and Mira in downtown Corpus Christi, who were on a road trip
from Seattle to Texas (and back to Seattle). After dinner, we strolled
around downtown.

<img src="./media/image104.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image636.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image397.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

It was pretty funny to have different friend circles cross paths like
that due to the completely different spacetime in which the friendship
was formed. Svet and I went to School in Seattle. Cris and Mira went to
school in Munich. And Cris and I met in a redwood forest in Bay area. It
was even funnier that we got to meet each other at a quite random third
place that was Corpus Christi. And it couldn’t be more random that 4 of
us ended up working for the same company. Svet was to start in May. The
world is one big family.

**April 10**

Svet and Mira headed north to Austin, while Cris and I headed south to
lower Rio Grande river valley. We started the day with a Texas classic -
whataburger. My first time. I used to [<u>rate chicken
sandwiches</u>](https://anglil.quora.com/A-review-of-spicy-chicken-sandwiches-and-chicken-nuggets)
from different fast food chains. This time I couldn’t resist the
temptation to try its chicken sandwich too. Unfortunately, despite the
ginormous portion, the quality of it fell only into the ranks of
Denny’s, jack in the box, and Safeway — a terrible territory :\| And
overall, if one does a head to head state symbol comparison, I have to
say whataburger is not in the same league as in-and-out. Not sure why
people were comparing these 2 on youtube!

<img src="./media/image309.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image72.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

As we went farther and farther south, it became warmer and warmer, there
was more and more significant Latino presence, and more and more palm
trees emerged. Before long, we were already at starbase, a great rocket
launchpad at the southern border by the Gulf of Mexico, a masterpiece
due to Elon Musk. Seeing the site in person, to think that rockets that
are launched here go to outer space and reach Mars gives me goosebumps.
It was like seeing Da Gama before he first sailed to India! To erect
such a grandiose structure in the middle of nowhere also speaks to the
quirks of Elon Musk. To be fair, the launch site could have been
anywhere south enough to save energy but he chose \*here\*, Boca Chica,
southeasternmost of lower Texas.

<img src="./media/image114.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image258.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image67.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image215.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image453.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image461.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

1 minute from starbase is Boca chica beach, which is the mouth of Rio
Grande, and not far is the border between the US and Mexico.

<img src="./media/image308.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image741.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image225.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

So far still no coconut tree spotted, despite that we were already at
the farthest south of Texas, and eastern most in the region as well,
closest to the ocean, which means moderated winter lows. To get some
help, I messaged a south Texas coconut enthusiast group on Facebook for
expert assistance. From earlier on the trip, the replies were all
negative.

<img src="./media/image441.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

A generous guy named John PM’ed me saying that there were 100 to 150
coconut trees in the lower Rio grande valley region, and around 50 in
the corpus Christi region. After the “big freeze” last winter, almost
none survived in the wild in corpus Christi, and only a handful remained
in lower Rio grande valley. He lives in Corpus Christi and has 2 in his
yard he planted after the big freeze. He invited us to his home to check
them out on our way back.

<img src="./media/image616.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:13.57292in" />

So that was the plan!

Waving goodbye to starbase and Boca Chica Beach, We went on our merry
way. The idea was to visit John in Corpus Christi, via Brownsville.

Near Brownsville I found this cluster of palms that looked very similar
to coconut trees, but it turned out that they were a more hardy kind
named queen palms.

<img src="./media/image165.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image19.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image403.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:13.57292in" />

In downtown Brownsville, we came across a border crossing over Rio
Grande. There’s car crossing and pedestrian crossing on the bridge. The
border crossing is called Gateway International Bridge System. Curiosity
got the better of us. We decided to get a close up view of the border.
And there wasn’t any difficulty at all. We put a coin into the
turnstile, thinking it would get us to a touristy area where one could
see Mexico and Rio Grande up close (up until this point we hadn’t really
seen Rio Grande). Little did we know after passing that turnstile we
were already in Matamoros, Mexico. We realized it when we found
ourselves on av. Álvaro Obregón in Matamoros without passing a single
customs or a checkpoint. So be it! Let’s explore Mexico for a bit and
then come back.

<img src="./media/image631.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image513.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image283.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image56.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image15.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image250.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image622.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image493.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

The streets were quite empty, probably because it was a Sunday
afternoon. The vibes were similar to Haikou I have to say — laid back,
bordering a tropical climate, less developed than the other side of the
border. I was really excited throughout this short Mexico trip, because
I had never been in a country other than the US or China. Alas, the
first visit to Mexico was on such an accidental occasion!

We walked for a few blocks and decided it was time to head back because
it was getting dark, 30 minutes had already passed, and our phones were
low on battery. This was where the real adventure began.

<img src="./media/image378.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image152.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image82.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image182.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image593.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

A hell of an adventure.

From US to Mexico, there was no border check, which led us to Mexico
inadvertently. However, from Mexico to US, it was a whole different
story.

There was a customs buildup on the US side. There I was detained and
interrogated for 4 hours by ICE. The treatment I got there absolutely
warrants a whole different post. In hindsight, I received harsh
treatments because I suspect that they suspected that we were drug
dealers or human traffickers, because this exact border had seen a lot
of those activities, as we were later told by friends who had insights
into that border crossing. In addition to harsh treatments, customs and
border protection officers ended up actually “deporting” me, admitting
me back in the US with the premises that I leave the country within 30
days.

I carried all the documents (in digital forms) with me that could have
established the legitimacy of my re-entry (under the “automatic
revalidation” clause). Nevertheless, CBP used their discretion to land
quite a harsh decision on me. So I got quite upset with the judgement.
Talking about busting my American dream! This led to quite some hustling
over the next few days on my side trying to find a third country to
travel to for visa stamping in order to re-establish myself in America,
under the 30-day time bomb. Thankfully, a few days later this judgement
got corrected, and I was no longer a parolee or had to depart the
country, thanks to CBP’s willingness to approve our record correction
request. My lawyers mentioned that they didn’t actually expect that CBP
would respond, which gave me real chills!

There were 2 interesting footnotes to this story. First, I met a few
Ukrainian refugees on the Mexican side of the border trying to enter the
US. On the Mexican side, there was help organized by local churches to
send the refugees from the airport to the border, giving them food and
accommodation. Humanitarian assistance by the US government in turn
would allow them to stay in the US for one year. The happiness of these
people was so palpable the moment when the officer told them they were
admitted. Witnessing this made me feel close to an actual war and the
distorted humanity that ensued. Second, during the course of researching
for third countries to travel to for visa stamping, I met quite a few
generous strangers, and had the pleasure to befriend them. A Singaporean
couple I met through a mutual friend helped me get a Singapore e-visa
within a day. And a Fijian guy I reached out to on IG helped me book an
embassy appointment in a local post office using Fijian dollars, the
only manner of payment that the US embassy there recognizes. It was
these encounters that kept me going until the record correction took
place. And it was because of these encounters that I felt an urge to
travel to a lot more countries, meeting more people.

Back to the crime scene, we eventually re-entered the US, close to
midnight. We found a hotel in south padre island. I told John we
couldn’t make it to Corpus Christi, and apologized. He welcomed us to
visit his house next time!

The night on the South padre island was quiet (a few kids on their
spring break were heard babbling aloud during the last stretch of the
bar hours) but was not calm. Bothered by the visa situation, I wandered
about on the main street, keeping an eye on coconut trees which, of all
the places in Texas, this place has the best condition to allow to
survive the big freeze. None was spotted.

<img src="./media/image317.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image701.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

**April 11**

We headed back to Austin for the flight early next morning after a quick
brunch on South Padre Island. When passing through San Antonio, we
decided to take a detour and visit the famed Texan German town —
Fredericksburg. What a fascinating place deep in the heart of Texas! A
lot of the signs were in German (Cris understood them), and the churches
and businesses looked German as well. There was a museum telling the
history of how German immigrants established this place as their new
home in the 19th century.

<img src="./media/image268.jpg"

What’s more German than having doner kebabs for dinner? We did exactly
that, on our way back to Austin.

<img src="./media/image666.jpg"

We took a quick tour around the 6th street, the Texas capitol, and the
UT Austin campus, and called it a day. It was late night and the flight
would depart in the early morning next day.

<img src="./media/image656.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

My biggest takeaway from this adventure is that there will always be
unknowns on the next journey — be prepared and have the audacity to
tackle them. Resolving these has been and will be a rewarding
experience. Things will be fine, since your heart is in the right place!

And also I wouldn’t mind going on an adventure in Texas again — what
generous people (excluding CBP! no matter how understandable their
behavior was), what diversity of nature and cultures, what generosity of
meal portions! My American dream started from exactly here in Texas and
will continue crossing paths with it.

# **San Diego and Lake Mendocino**

7/5

So Jess, Joshua, Ailen and I decided to escape from work / take
advantage of the school break for a week, to explore a couple of nice
places away from Bay Area.

On the Tuesday of the week of 4th of July, we drove down I-5 to San
Diego. Had dinner at an Uyghur restaurant in LA, which was visited by
Mark Wiens before. Visiting his recommendations rarely disappoints.
Turned out the food was okay. The one in Fremont was better actually.
But it was really nice to stop by LA before the drivers tired out. At
night, we arrived at the Airbnb near little Italy. It was also so close
to the airport that airplanes felt like they might hit the rooftop!

7/6

We tried another one of Mark Wiens-visited restaurants in the morning,
Ocean 97 Seafood. It’s a food truck on a Safeway parking lot. Wow, I had
the best ceviche there. It’s also interesting how they serve coconuts.
They put chili into coconut meat. It was delicious. They do similar
stuff to mangos and guavas in Hainan. I guess tropical / almost tropical
regions prepare fruits in similar ways. The fish tacos were also bombs.
They were juicy, warm and fresh. The food truck didn’t open at the
advertised time until an hour after, but it was well worth the wait.

(In fact, my cousin and I visited this place 1.5 years ago too and it
was not open at the time, more in

[<u>https://qr.ae/pvuhEq</u>](https://qr.ae/pvuhEq)

)

In the afternoon we visited San Diego Zoo. In the US I’ve done Woodland
Park Zoo (Seattle), Bronx Zoo (NYC) and San Francisco Zoo (SF). I think
San Diego Zoo is the best. Depending on the time of the year of course,
but the animals in San Diego Zoo are the most unhinged by humans,
untethered and active. And it’s huge. We walked the west half, and did
the cable cart for the east half, and finished it off with an all around
shuttle tour. The flamingos were a highlight. They way they were
sleeping while maintaining the standup posture was amusing.

In the evening, we were back in Little Italy, and tried one of Sam the
cooking guy’s restaurants — “Samburgers”, and a gelato place next to it.
I had to say the burger was the best I had had. The buns were soft on
the inside, but a little crunchy on the outside. The patty was beefy and
extraordinarily tasty. And the dragon fruit gelato was also a star! It
was not too sweet, and it maintained that dragon fruit fragrance. People
that can visit Little Italy often are really really spoiled I must say.
There are just so many nice restaurants to choose from — Sam the cooking
guy alone has 3 restaurants here. The street musicians, the neon lights
and the gift shops also lifted the ambiance.

After little Italy, we went ocean bound to check out the Tuna Harbor
Park. There’s this famous Public Art of a sculpture "Embracing Peace",
which depicts this spontaneous and monumental kiss of a sailor and a
nurse in Time Square at the advent of the news of the allied victory in
WW2 in 1945. Since San Diego is an important navy harbor with marine
stationed here dating back to that time, this sculpture is an important
symbol here. When we were visiting, quite a few couples were taking
kissing pictures in turn under these two giants :-)

At night, I went for a run around the block. From I-5 to balboa park and
back. San Diego nights are warm and so relaxing. Reminded me of the last
time when my cousin and I visited San Diego to flee the cold weather in
Bay Area, and were gladly welcomed with warm night temperatures as well.

7/7

Both Jess and I had to catch up with work. So we spent the morning in
the airbnb. Jess made breakfast for all of us. The breakfast whose
ingredients I bought from a nearby liquor + grocery store was really
nice. Powered me through the meetings.

In the afternoon we went for karting. I think Joshua liked it a lot.
Ailen didn’t start off going super fast but did well in the end too. I
tried steering the car according to road signs. For example, I’d go slow
and make a turn when I saw a sign of a sharp turn, and would take the
longest straight line by including parts of the turns on the 2 ends, and
accelerate through. But my approach apparently could have been improved,
as I ended up at 5th in my best round. There were around 10 people. This
little boy with an Italian worded t-shirt was cocky before the race, and
I thought I beat him. Turned out he was one spot ahead of me. Couldn’t
let go of that smirk he had after the race!

For dinner, we visited Jess’ friends Anastasia and Vladimir in a
vineyard. Anastasia and Jess became besties when they were students in
the same class. Since then Anastasia and her husband Vladimir moved
around the country. Their meetings with Jess had not been frequent. This
time they booked this nice vineyard beforehand knowing that we were
visiting. With the sunset in the background and some really good wine,
the margherita pizza and the carbonara were extra delicious. Got drunk
thanks to the wine, so after the dinner when we visited Anastasia and
Vladimir’s cool house at a hill top, I don’t remember much what we were
talking about, but I remember thinking to myself owning a house in the
outskirts of San Diego is a cool idea.

7/8

We drove back to Bay area. We stopped by La Jolla Shores Beach and
dipped in the water. The water was warmer than Half moon bay, but still
cool. The sun was extreme! Since we were pretty much swimming in the
water, I thought the water would block the sun. But I ended up getting
sun burned still. Nevertheless, it was pretty fun. We were basically
jumping the waves — when a wave came, we’d jump, so we wouldn’t absorb
all the momentum of the wave that would crash on us. Just watch out for
the surfers! It was apparently a very popular surfing spot. Ailen also
collected a lot of these small crabs on the beach. I thought the amount
of living animals on the beach was surprising given how popular the
beach was.

On the way back, we stopped by this coconut tree in Del Mar. Coconut
trees are extremely rare in California — California is too cold during
winter time and too dry overall to foster coconut palms. But there are
rare exceptions. Palm enthusiasts like me would know that there are a
fewer than 10 in California, and this one in Del Mar is the tallest and
the most mature one. When we arrived at this resort, we found the tree
nicely situated at the south wall of the hotel building by the ocean.
The microclimate of the building protects it from cold snaps. The tree
possibly doesn’t have the warmth or enough water to bear fruits, but its
height is impressive, comparable to those one would see in Haikou. I had
a slither of homesickness.

As we drove by LA, we decided to eat at this taqueria in Compton. Jess
said the way this taqueria was set up reminded her of Mexico. All the
meat were in one huge pot, with the juice boiling away. One could smell
the meatiness from far, far away. It was unmistakably a place that
locals would frequent. Also notable was the fact that it was \$1.75 each
taco, very affordable! Each of us had from 4 to 5 tacos. These tacos
were so delicious and fulfilling. With the LA downtown skyline in the
background, I felt I was on cloud 9.

When we arrived in Bay Area, it was 2pm and cold, and we were all half
asleep! We crammed in my apartment for the night.

7/9

In the morning, we headed north to Lake Mendocino, to meet Anelli and
Gera and their friends and camp for a night. The whole drive was so
pretty. You have the typical foggy sunset district in San Francisco, and
sunny Sausalito on the sea. Farther north, you have the lush green of
Cloverdale, which receives as much as 5 times of precipitation of some
of the areas in the bay area. Then you have Ukiah, which is a small town
by the lake, which would supply us with all the grocery and alcohol we
needed :)

By dusk, we’ve set up the tent. I dipped in the water in lake Mendocino.
The lake water is no west coast pacific - it’s warm and calm. Warmed up
by the sun for the entire day, the water temperature had risen to 20
celsius, easily. Famous for its gold rush era history, Russian river
would inject water to the lake when the dam was open (at the moment it
was closed). The water went deep quickly off shore, and the stones and
grass at the bottom made navigation tricky. So we were swimming strictly
by the shore. The sun was beaming, offering a nice contrast between the
air temperature and the water temperature.

It had been a while since I last swan in a lake or a river. The most
recent was probably when I was 10 swimming in the Xiang River with my
cousin and uncle. So it was really nice coming back and having this
memory refreshed.

According to Lalo, who had been to lake Mendocino many times over the
years, the draught in recent years has destroyed the ecosystem of the
area. He pointed me to the previous water level visible on the bank,
which separates the tree line (above which trees grow in this case) and
the bank, indicating the normal water level. And that line is way above
the current water level, to an astonishing extent. Media has coverage of
this situation, which I had read about. But seeing it in person shocked
me even more.

The temperature dropped quickly at night, so we sat around a fire pit.
We started eating. The hotdogs and hamburger meats were made perfectly.
The smell of the barbecue and the smoke would make anyone near it
hungry. The swim in the lake, the food coma, and then the warmth of the
fire, relaxed every ounce of my being. We started singing and drinking.
Almost everyone here came from Michoacán, a few of us Mexico City. There
were a few Mexican folk songs almost everyone knew how to sing. It was
nice to listen to music sung in harmony, with passion, even though I
didn’t know how to sing them or the language well. Finally, we started
singing the Anthem of Mexico, which I learned recently. I always find
the lyrics of the Mexican anthem full of passion, specially the line
“Piensa, oh, patria querida, que el cielo. Un soldado en cada hijo te
dio” — Emociones muy fuertes.

7/10

The hangover was real from last night. Anelli made me an iv drink for
rehydration. For lunch, we had carne asada. Lalo and co. expertly cooked
the meat on the grill, served with home made spicy salsa. With onions
and cilantro and corn tortilla, the meal was perfection.

It was really nice to befriend my fellow campers. They are some of the
most genuine and passionate people, each with a storied life. Living in
the little bubble that is the bay area, the daily conversations have
been too often centered around compensation, tech, real estate, school
districts and competitions. Somehow I felt such an environment causes us
to lose touch with the humanity that lies deeply in everyone’s heart. My
revelation and advice to others is this: please don’t conflate moving up
the socio-economic ladder with living a fulfilled life. The latter is
the goal, well, at least mine, which, however, does not always follow
the former. Similarly, the former is something that’s nice to have. But
when the two are in conflict, I always think one should prioritize
fulfillment in life, which more or less revolves around love, passion,
entrepreneurship, and dedication to family, which is fully embodied in
the way my new friends approach life. I really appreciate this encounter
by Lake Mendocino. Need many more of these to come!

# **Baltimore, Philly and Washington DC**

So I traveled to east coast to attend ICML 2022, which was held in
Baltimore this year. The company sponsored the trip. That means I’m
obliged to write about research frontiers I learned from the conference,
which were a lot, and were a lot of fun. But that will be in a different
post. This post mostly talks about stuff outside of academia.

I had never been to Baltimore before, neither had I explored Philly or
DC in any depth. Last time was with a tourist group and my mom 10 years
ago. So when i got to decide which place to go to for this year’s
sponsored machine learning conference trip, I was pleased to find out
that Baltimore was an option.

7/16

I arrived in DC on 7/16, at the Reagan airport. DC was such a different
place from the Bay Area, from the ethnic makeup (black and white), to
the way people walk and talk (fast), to the weather (humid and hot)! The
lady who sat next to me on the plane explained to me that in DC one
simply can’t miss the peach, corn, and tomato, and don’t miss out on the
ice cream trucks. The specialties sound a bit strange but ok! Off we go.

First I went to George town. Pretty trendy place full of boutique shops
and trendy people, on a substrate of a bevy of historical buildings,
including some really really old churches. Tried the ice cream there,
also one of the best kebabs. A rapid thunderstorm came and dissipated
fast as I was in the kebab shop.

Then I visited the Washington monument area. Ten years ago it was
exactly the monument and Lincoln memorial that convinced me of coming to
the US. The mottos, the obelisk, the tranquility of the water seen from
Jefferson memorial exuded powerful and exotic vibes. This second time, I
felt the same. While last time I just took a picture far away from the
monument (which was my fb profile picture for a long time), this time I
touched the monument. The building blocks were pretty cool, like a
pyramid.

At night I drove to Baltimore and checked in at the hotel. One hour
drive. First impression of Baltimore was that it was a pretty historical
town with a small and much less grandiose downtown core.

7/17

Started exploring Baltimore. Met with Haiyan and Ke in the morning.
Family friends, who have long inspired me to come to America. They used
to bring me a gift every time they visited us in China. One of the gifts
was a world atlas, knowing that I’m a map fan. The way borders were
drawn in that atlas opened my eyes because before that every map I had
was made in China. One of the scariest things I know is that they
escaped the collapse of the twin towers on 911 coincidentally, calling
in sick.

With them, had breakfast in one of the most popular brunch places in
Inner Harbor area. Didn’t have the popular crab cake, saving for later.
Had a nice conversation with Haiyan about life, America, family, and
workplace politics. Lol. Nowhere can one avoid that, be it in actuary
industry (her) or tech (me).

She drove 2 hours from Philly to see me this morning, and her son,
playing chess competitively, happened to be in town for a tournament
this weekend. After the brunch, I was invited to visit her in Philly the
next weekend. Couldn’t wait!

For the rest of the morning I explored little Italy (“Piccola Italia”)
area da solo. Compared to San Diego’s Little Italy and SF’s Little
Italy, Baltimore’s is much less touristy, full of old buildings, but
looks more Italian, in terms of originality. Nancy Pelosi, an Italian
American, was born here.

Outside of little Italy, the area was even more run down. Abandoned
factories, desolate residential buildings, were seen everywhere.
Apparently the area was dangerous for a naive tourist. People in the
shops told me “don’t go that way!”, pointing to certain directions.

Later in the afternoon I checked in for the conference at the convention
center. And checked out the recruitment area. Every known tech company
had a booth there. For me, I was there to collect nice souvenirs. Stuff
offered by Two Sigma has consistently been the nicest :-)

That night I met with a bunch of German students who came to attend the
conference. We found a biergarten near the convention center, called
Paulaner. For these germans the beer glass size was normal. For me too
much. A German girl took the beer I couldn’t finish and devoured it :-)

It was really fun. These PhD students had great energy. They liked to
talk politics too. For example, they had a lot to say about that gas
pipeline recently reopened between Germany and Russia, and how it
affected the strength of Euro.

<img src="./media/image50.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:3.52083in" />

After the bar, on the street a guy approached us. He read us some
Christian teachings and then started asking for money. The transition
was very smooth like how coach nick from bballbreakdown transitions from
basketball to commercials. The story he told was cool, about how he was
incarcerated for half of his life and then was reborn and so on. We took
a picture with him. Nothing bad happened.

<img src="./media/image314.jpg" style="width:6.27083in;height:7in" />

7/18

Very first day of the conference. We had a bunch of tutorials and
workshops. I was surprised to find out that a lot of the talks were
pre-recorded. Audience was sometimes left clapping to a video after a
talk.

Of the sessions, LatinX in AI workshop was pretty interesting to me,
because I care a lot about AI and tech education in Bay Area especially
for the LatinX communities. I got in touch with a guy who connected me
to a few organizations that carry out such initiatives in the Bay Area.
Very inspirational guy. He did a lot of such work in the Houston area.

Another tutorial is about machine learning and climate change. I found
the issue mostly a policy one rather than one regarding specific machine
learning technicalities. e.g., how to allocate resources and how to
bring awareness so models get trained and evaluated.

In the afternoon I explored some more neighborhoods in the more rundown
areas. The shops had bullet-proof windows. The locals all looked very
discreet. They warned me against displaying my phone when walking on the
streets, and against leaving anything valuable in the car. The street
scene was indeed creepy. A lot of the people on the streets were either
disabled or blind or mentally ill. Everything screamed poverty and gun
violence. Initially avoiding being too close to potential smoke, I was
relieved to find out that people just wanted to say hi and even to bless
you when you came close.

<img src="./media/image458.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

It was a huge contrast between the street scene and Johns Hopkins
hospital, the latter of which I passed by on the way back to the hotel.
Hard to believe the most advanced medical technologies and the most
prestigious medical school were abreast with some of the least educated
/ poorest neighborhoods in American.

What was different in Baltimore from Bay was also that at intersections,
where is no light dedicated to left turns; you have to wait until the
traffic in the opposite direction goes away and then turn left, when
hopefully the forward light is still green. Also, during the wait,
people often come up to you to sell bottled waters, clean windows and
sell other stuff, sometimes aggressively. I got one unsolicited window
cleaning and got away with paying as the traffic started moving.

<img src="./media/image145.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

7/19

In the morning before the conference started, I ventured out again to a
very local joint for chicken and fish. Baltimore apparently boasts a
variety of chicken and fish sandwich spots. And they seem to be all run
by Koreans! One of the most popular is the lake trout sandwich. However,
the fish is not trout, and it’s from the ocean instead of a lake.
Anyway, yesterday I got a chicken and shrimp sandwich, so today I
switched up and got a lake trout meal. Very fresh and tasty 🙌

<img src="./media/image431.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Can’t remember anything about the conference that day :(

In the afternoon, I visited Mount Vernon area, which is pretty much the
old downtown core. It’s a really historic place, with a lot of churches
and old buildings from the 18th century. Cris commented they resemble
those in Germany, with the coloring schemes and pointy roofs. Baltimore
has the most statues among American cities, and here in Mount Vernon,
statues, big and miniature, are everywhere. There are quite a few Arabic
restaurants in juxtaposition with the churches. The Yemeni restaurant
owner said outside of Michigan and NYC, Baltimore has a high
concentration of Yemenis and other arabs.

<img src="./media/image500.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

7/20

Today there were a few highlights from the conference. Translatotron 2
was a direct speech to speech translation model by Google, which has
made a huge advance over the performance of the first version thanks to
a few architectural inventions and linguistics-informed tricks. The
synthesized, translated speech was amazing. I couldn’t distinguish the
samples from human voices, and the translation was good.

Another work of note was “Symmetric Machine Theory of Mind”, by UW and
CMU. They claimed that most of machine theory of mind prior art is
asymmetric, and so put forth a symmetric version, where gaining reward
efficiently requires theory of mind: reward is given for sharing and
receiving new knowledge. I know the authors from UW times. It was
refreshing to see them expanding their research from pure machine
translation to reinforcement learning and game theory directions.

After the main conference, Aayush and I went to the poster session.
There were hundreds of posters. Was really really nice to engage with
the authors and learn the cool and happening stuff in machine learning.
Although some of them were too theoretical for me, my understanding was
enriched profoundly on a conceptual level, such as some of the gaps
between theoretical reinforcement learning and actual robot learning.

The conference venue offered food and the companies organized
after-parties mainly to attract talents. We went to one organized by G
Research

[<u>https://www.gresearch.co.uk/</u>](https://www.gresearch.co.uk/)

Met a few guys doing interesting research in weather forecasting by
combing physics (in the form of partial differential equations) and
machine learning. Apparently machine learning is not there yet; physics
is still the better forecaster. These guys are based in Stuttgart,
Germany. Apparently they are well funded. They posited that it be just a
matter of a few years when the hybrid approach takes the lead.

After the afterparty, a Stuttgart guy and I went to Mo’s Seafood to
sample some crab cakes. The guy studied in the Netherlands for masters.
His advisor referred him to Germany for geo-machine learning research
for his PhD. Reminded me of Noraly the itchy boots channel on youtube,
although she was more about theoretical field studies than large scale
data analyses,.

The cake was fine. The crab didn’t taste the freshest. I’d take chicken
and fish subs over a crab cake! The bartender was with us throughout the
meal. She was very enthusiastic about everything Germany.

<img src="./media/image514.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

7/21

In the morning I had a run, and ran into something that I would have
never believed exists — alive coconut trees! On the east coast, coconut
trees can’t even survive in Orlando, Florida. Maryland is just too far
north. Nevertheless, it was a pleasant surprise. The owner of the place
must have invested some serious money in keeping the cocos alive.

<img src="./media/image451.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

After the run, on the way walking to the convention center, where the
conference was located, I stumbled upon this huge building. The area is
called inner harbor, easily the safest and least chaotic area in the
whole of Baltimore.

<img src="./media/image164.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:11.70833in" />

Not gonna lie, the stuff presented at the conference today left me with
the impression that none of them, such as the stochastic Hamiltonian
Monte Carlo or graphical model improvements would gain any traction in
real life. The insights were not profound enough, and yet were overly
complicated. That I think is one of the main reasons. The additional
regularization terms and such just wouldn’t amount to a step function
change, or a phase transition.

At the poster session, I found more work I could understand and
appreciate, mostly around applied machine learning in NLP. There I found
that most of stuff deemed frontier research has already been happening
in industry. Sometimes industry doesn’t have the tendency to publish the
results or do rigorous benchmarking. Whatever makes loose sense, leading
companies in the industry usually have the resource to try them out.
Like this kNN-LM extension thing:

<img src="./media/image633.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

In the evening, a guy from the UK I met earlier, Chris, went to tour the
JHU campus with me and Aayush, who studies as a PhD student in JHU. The
campus was old fashioned, the buildings were like those in those English
unis according to Chris. It felt peaceful on this summer evening; There
were fire flies everywhere. Aayush said he never walked 2 blocks out
from the campus, because the safety situation would be drastically
different from that on campus. We checked out Aayush’s office space too,
in the computer science building. The entire department seemed to be
quite theory-focused, as theory books were seen on every desk. Despite
that, they had an open plan office too like in tech companies.

<img src="./media/image352.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

7/22

Last day of the main conference. The highlight for me was this workshop
called Knowledge retrieval and language models. There, a few prominent
figures in the area showed up and presented pretty interesting stuff.
Jimmy Lin talked about retrieval in the era of LLMs. Ellie Pavlick
talked about recognition that documents have an implicit conversation
and how that can help question answering. AI21 labs (Israel) presented
using huge frozen LLMs as readers for open-domain question answering.
Last but not least, Michihiro Yasunaga, Jure Leskovec and Percy Liang
from Stanford presented LinkBERT, which takes into account links between
documents in language models, such as hyperlinks in wiki, citations in
academic papers, and dependencies in github. I found it a nice blend of
graph learning and LLMs. Can be very useful at my job.

For lunch I tried Popeyes. The same lady in the first chicken and fish
shop who warned me of public safety also recommended Popeyes when I
asked her for fried chicken spot recommendations. Well it is chain, but
it was nice. Just had to burn those calories afterwards.

For dinner, at Haiyan and Ke’s invitation I drove to Philly. The drive
was 2 hours. Pretty scenic, the highway occasionally met the Atlantic
Ocean. We had dinner at a Mexican restaurant. In California, the default
street food are taco trucks, whereas on the east coast, the default are
subs and kebabs. Even the latino communities on the east coast are more
Caribbean and Central American than Mexican. So the dinner felt like a
flashback. Although it turned out that it was more Mexican American than
Mexican Mexican, the food was really tasty and bulky. The beer was not
Modelo or Corona but was nice too. Had a fun time with the future chess
master Andrew / Yangyang :-)

<img src="./media/image291.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

7/23

In the morning Ke and I set off for a tour around the Penn campus,
leaving the nice and affluent Wynnewood neighborhood Haiyan, Ke,
Yangyang and Fiona (who was at Brandeis as a freshman at the time) were
living in. Ke is an architect employed by Penn. He helped build several
buildings on the campus since he joined the university about 12 years
ago, working with renowned architects. Before that he worked as an
architect himself for Princeton. Even before that he graduated from
UPenn, school of Design (now Stuart Weitzman School of Design). With his
professional background, I knew I was guaranteed to have the best campus
visit ever.

We parked our cars by Ke’s office near the engineering building, and
started off with the old Moore School of Electrical Engineering
building, where the first digital computer ENIAC was built. We visited
the various undergrad dorm houses that are Penn’s signature buildings,
dating back from the university’s founding years. Then some new
undergrad houses that Ke helped build. Penn’s hospital and children’s
hospital that operates separately. Old Wharton buildings, new Wharton
buildings (its business school apparently is super well funded). Old Law
school building and new law school building. Its theatre, Penn museum
(one of the most comprehensive collections of middle and near-eastern
art in the world). One of the pioneering Corbusier style buildings
([<u>Modern architecture -
Wikipedia</u>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_architecture)). New
buildings like neuroscience, Penn Hillel, economics, etc. And of course,
Penn’s law school, business school, medical school, vet hospital,
women’s dormitory building, all of them the nation’s firsts. Some even
argue Penn is the first university in the States. Below are some of
these extraordinary structures.

<img src="./media/image525.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image147.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image278.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image679.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image46.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image311.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image446.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image153.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.69792in" />

After the Penn campus visit, we visited Drexel nearby too. My summer
intern goes to Drexel. Salamata was born in the US and grew up in
Guinea. She came back to the US for high school, and then Drexel. While
an intern, she found that her internship would need to have a focus on
front end per the program requirement, so we had to relocate her to a
front end team. Nevertheless, during her stint on my team, her
creativity demonstrated in the capstone project, productivity and
aptitude for an uplifting spirit made a hugely positive impression. The
signature dragon statue of Drexel reminded me of her.

After lunch for a halal chicken cheese steak at a food truck with Ke, I
headed for Philadelphia Museum of Art, which Ke strongly recommended.
It’s a huge and nice museum. Not someone who’s very knowledgeable about
classical arts, I did learn a lot there. My biggest impression of the
galleries displayed there was that since Renaissance, European arts
started to really focus on individuals, with the person being in the
center with detailed personal traits, whereas East Asian arts tend to be
about flowers, birds, insects, and fish — there isn’t a focus on
individuality in contrast. I feel this is a huge reflection of the
differences in the mindsets between east and west. Such a revelation was
also somewhat captured by a description I found in the museum:

“Artistic practices in China have since ancient times been intertwined
with ideas of man’s relationship to the cosmos and achieving harmony
with the natural world. Painting and calligraphy were not just forms of
self-expression, but could aid in cultivating one’s spirituality and
finding the truth. Traditionally, it was the literati — men of learning
and high moral character — who practiced the creative arts to reach
these higher ideals. The literati vulgarity and showiness — qualities
that could carry through into thought and action. Activities such as
drinking tea and wine, burning incense, and collecting antiques to study
the past were all considered ways toward self-realization.”

After the museum I headed to Reading Market in Center City (how downtown
is called in Philly). There one can find all kinds of Amish food. Amish
people emigrated to Pennsylvania from Germany in the 19th century en
masse to flee political disturbance. They have largely kept their
traditions and speak Pennsylvanian Dutch, which is a German dialect.

<img src="./media/image487.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Mark Wiens came here also. not that I follow his recommendations
everywhere :-) it was a lady in the museum who recommended this place. I
arrived at the market right before their closing time at 5pm.

Afterwards I didn’t waste any time and headed to the birth place of
Philly cheese steaks — Pat’s. It’s in the little Italy area in south
Philly. There were lots of Italian immigrants in this area and they
invented this famous sandwich while they were in the US. To my surprise,
there was a long line there with people having all kinds of accents. I
thought only Chinese people would do such touristy things :-) Right
before the window where you order, they put a sign demonstrating how to
order with jargons to show to the staff that you are versed with the
local lingo. I did an original “cheese wit”, which is a cheese steak
with onions. Was nice. After the sandwich, I did what Will Smith did — I
played basketball, one-on-one, with a guy who just graduated high school
here, who aspired to do computer science. He had a side hustle where he
bought and sold shoes on IG. Youngsters these days are entrepreneurs.
Very cool.

<img src="./media/image211.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

At night, I returned to Baltimore.

7/24

In the morning, I checked out from the hotel, and headed to DC where my
flight would take off. There I met with Pascal to tour those free
museums, which last time I didn’t get a chance to explore.

The first museum was natural history museum. There were a lot of stuff
on display, from geology to animals to vegetation. Earth is a magical
place :-) Pascal was teaching me more reinforcement learning stuff in
addition to his conference talk throughout the visit. I think I learned
more machine learning in the museum than in Baltimore altogether :-)

The second museum was Archives of the United States of America. I was
super excited about this because of National Treasure. I had to admit
Riley’s role in the movie was pretty influential to my career choice so
far as an engineer. Plus, Diane Kruger was pretty and Nicolas Cage’s
performance was not as bad as they say. Unfortunately no pictures were
allowed in the museum. Neither was I able to decipher the hand written
scripts of the founding fathers. Despite the curly squiggles, I more or
less know the messages conveyed on these sacred scripts, and I really
admire the originality of the system they managed to build.

The third museum was the Holocaust memorial museum. This was a hard one
because, well, Pascal is German. I initially felt awkward. But Pascal’s
explanation dissolved the awkwardness as he said Germans had field trips
to holocaust museums as students, in Germany, Poland, etc. He himself
was on a school-organized trip to Auschwitz. In short, he had ample
exposure to this history and took it very seriously. It was a very
educative place, where they demonstrated the scale of the horror and
replicated the death chamber and the interior of those trains that
shipped Jewish victims to concentration camps. It was somehow also funny
because whenever Pascal started talking with a German accent, people
immediately turned their attention to him, which wouldn’t be the case
outside this place.

<img src="./media/image417.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

After the museum crawl, I made it on time to the Reagan airport, where I
came from. I didn’t have the time to put gas but the rental car guy was
generous enough to give me a waiver.

Back in the chilly, culturally desert-y and anxiety filled Bay Area :(

JK. I like both the west and east coasts. They are great in just
different ways.

# **NYC**

Oct 8th after a night of flying I landed in JFK, to start my visit to
NYC. This trip was sponsored by the company, but I try to squeeze in as
much non work time as possible. Being in NYC means you have access to
all the world cultures and languages and food possible. That was exactly
what I was looking for on this trip.

Having a rental car allowed me to do that with more flexibility. I drove
from the airport to my first stop delicias caleñas, a Colombian
restaurant in Jackson Heights. My friend and spanish teacher Kathe comes
from Cali, Colombia, and I wanted to check out how the food from her
hometown tastes like.

<img src="./media/image386.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

Very delicious and filling. The staff was so friendly. What a wonderful
start (although the car got a ticket for careless parking :)). Right
outside of this little gem of a restaurant there are tons of Bengali and
Indian restaurants and shops. People of different clothes and looks walk
around, doing their thing. Easy to see mixtures like this in Bay Area,
but not outside of work when living their normal life. What a cool
place.

Very quickly I realized it was a bad, bad idea to bring a rental car
into midtown Manhattan, where my hotel is located. It’s impossible to
find any cheap parking, or any parking. Well, I had no choice but to
park at a “FILO” parking lot near my hotel. What an invention. The poor
ticket guy had to move 15 cars out to the narrow street outside when I
needed the car later in the afternoon. I don’t understand how the
productivity can sustain the parking throughput in the heart of
Manhattan.

At noon I met up with my friend Daniel at a Spanish tapas place a few
minutes of walk from my hotel. He used to go to the same office as me
for work in Bay Area, but came here for opportunities of work and life
:-) As I had that rich desayuno caleño not long ago, no matter how
appealing the paella looked like, I had no room regretfully. The tapas
plates were crazy good though, only matched later by Petiscos in
downtown San Jose.

<img src="./media/image269.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

A band happened to be performing at the stage in the restaurant. We
enjoyed the great performance of what sounded like a sad Spanish song.
The girl was so easy on the eyes. I understood why Daniel came.

After lunch, Daniel and I went for a walk around east village. People,
loads of people, walking around, arguing, playing chess in the park,
going to a Japanese fare, etc., At the moment there was an irresistible
charm to living in such a cosmopolis.

<img src="./media/image559.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image535.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image68.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image625.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image239.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image599.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image8.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

We then headed off to Washington square park. The weather on this autumn
afternoon was perfect. People were hanging out, doing all sorts of
things. The air was filled with an intellectual atmosphere. The place is
young and alive.

<img src="./media/image318.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

<img src="./media/image586.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

Later that evening, cris Boxin and I met and brought my rental car to go
to the Bronx. Somewhere a little more down to earth. That’s what we
always do. There we visited little Italy. Apparently this little Italy
in Bronx has preserved more authenticity than the one in Manhattan.
Unfortunately we didn’t find parking and had to head back to Manhattan
in time for a show in cafe wha which Boxin booked in advance. Raghu
joined us too. we enjoyed great live music, featuring a lot of familiar
Latin songs. It was an incredible experience.

We came back from the cafe to raghu’s apartment in the heart of
Manhattan, they were drinking before going to another club. I was
exhausted and fell asleep on raghu’s couch. it was 3am when I woke up
and they were still in the club. So I walked through the sleepless
streets of Manhattan back to my hotel. What a place, it is ever alive;
it never sleeps!

<img src="./media/image261.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image506.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:8.36458in" />

<img src="./media/image710.jpg"
style="width:6.27083in;height:4.70833in" />

——— from here on, the trip is documented in the fashion of more or less
just photo-less bullet points ———

——— in the interest of time ———

——— at Jean’s recommendation ———

Oct 9th. In the morning, I went to 5th avenue where a Latin parade was
happening. It was the hispanic heritage month. The parade features all
the spanish speaking countries in latin america. Each country was
represented by a wagon. It was really cool to be in the parade with the
festivity exuberant with the warmth of the various latin american
nations :)

In the evening I headed to Flushing to meet up with Jinyi, long time
friend from high school, and her husband. We had coconut chicken and
boba, and did a lot of catching up. We said to meet again in a few days
in lower Manhattan for the catchup to continue.

Afterwards, I met up with Cris and Boxin in the main foodcourt in
Flushing, and headed to the Post Malone concert. I never really paid
attention to Post Malone, it was Boxin’s idea, but the concert was fire.
The constant screaming of the fans alone almost made the concert so
memorable. After that, we had chinese kebabs at a spot back in Flushing,
some late night protein resupply. I drove all 3 of us back to Boxin’s
luxurious apartment in New Jersey and decided to park my rental car for
the remainder of the NYC trip for its affordable cost and senseless act
of bringing a car to NYC :)

So next Morning (oct 10th), I took a uber to work in Manhattan. The
office is in a skyscraper, with an amazing view to midtown and downtown.
Qiang and Jingjing took me on a tour an the roof top to capture this
view. The skyline of NYC is marvelous!

In the evening Cris, Boxin and I went to a Romanian restaurant in
Astoria, Brooklyn. It had been a long time since I learned that Romania
was known for mici, a type of meat kebab, and wanted to try. I got to
try in this restaurant at last. It was juicy tender and flavorful meat.
Great! The owner is Romanian and he and Cris talked in Romanian. Flyers
about a Romanian singer Gheorghe Gheorghu could be found on the exterior
of the restaurant. This is the kind of experience, one in which a
restaurant featuring food from anywhere in the world can be found, that
one hardly gets to have outside of NYC in the US.

Oct 11th. During lunch time I went to this Italian restaurant near east
village. Just a random spot and the pasta was amazing. There are murals
and other artwork that send strong messages in support of Ukraine
everywhere. A Ukraine Consulate is nearby.

After work, Cris, Rahu and I had dinner in another Italian restaurant in
Chelsea. Boxin joined for a cocktail afterwards.

Oct 12th. After work, met up with Licheng, Hui and Chenglin in the
office. I got to know these friends from different projects and
circumstances at work, but they are in different offices and they never
talked to each other before. It was so fun to meet up with friends from
different circles.

Andriy, Dmitriy and I met at a Goergian restaurant in Williamsburg for
dinner. The owner is Georgian. She is a good friend of Dmitriy, and
hosted us at the bar. We talked about politics, food, NYC, …, and had
amazing georgian dumplings, and the the Georgian chacha. I had never had
goergian food so amazing. NYC is apparently full of such surprises for a
culture and language aficionado like me :) After dinner Andriy showed me
around williamsburg, apparently a place where the crypto crowd
congregates, in addition to its orthodox jewry.

Oct 13th. I walked to work via the High Line. It is a railroad turned
pathway through west midtown Manhattan’s dense residential and office
areas, decorated with lush vegetation. In Hudson yards I arrived and saw
this landmark called Vessel, shaped like a honeycomb. The office offers
an amazing view spanning New Jersey, Manhattan and Queens. In the
afternoon, hispanic heritage month-themed happy hour took place. Flags
of hispanic countries were hung on a shelf. It was interesting to see
Equatorial Guinea flag there as well along with the usuals.

For dinner, I met up with Jinyi and her husband in Chinatown Manhattan
by a Yellow cab, yes the iconic yellow cab that’s featured in Jay-Z’s
Empire State of Mind. There was a waitress there giving us the backstory
of the Asian fusion food that they serve. We had a great time.

After dinner, Boxin and I met up at the infamous tech roast show
“Socially Inept” that happened to be in town. The guy was brilliant
dishing out inside jokes in tech, and had a knack engaging with the
audience and had them tell their stories that then get roasted by him
and 2 other partners in a hilarious way.

Oct 14th, I gave a talk at work that aimed to recruit engineers to join
a project I’m working on. It went really well. After work, Daniel and I
met at a Jazz Bar. It was just this one bar that i visited but I had
this feeling that NYC jazz scene was off the charts. Daniel was in
agreement as a jazz fan. We later had dinner at an Ukrainian restaurant
that had this slogan on the wall “make borscht not war”, referring to
the war that was taking place in Ukraine. The borscht was fantastic. We
later strolled along the east river in williamsburg. The tranquility of
atmosphere, the clearness of the river water, and the awe-inspiring
skyscrapers in the background invoked an unforgettable experience.

Oct 15th, it’s finally a weekend for me to explore NYC in more depth. In
the morning I walked from the hotel through this flea market that
features bizarre woodwork and apparel albeit enjoying a huge cliente, to
time square. There’s a disney shop there where I got a souvenir for my
Colombian friend. Then I ventured eastward, saw amazing jewish bakeries,
romanian consulate, the grandiose public library, and met up with Taesun
to have lunch at a Korean bbq place. The meat was succulent and the
Korean soju was strong and smooth. I felt very fortunate to eat Korean
food with a great korean friend. Later we arrived nextdoor at a japanese
cocktail place to the delight of Taesun as a let’s say food and liquor
